Is Verbal Definition a Conceptual Demand?
Discusses debate over whether verbal definition is a conceptual demand with refutation of Taftazani and Dawani, and final refinement.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the debate over whether a verbal definition belongs to conceptual demands or linguistic inquiry, and the correct view that it must both present the meaning and indicate the coinage.

Core Idea
A verbal definition informs the coinage of a word, not directly its meaning.

Scholars like al-Taftazani argued that verbal definition is a conceptual demand, while others saw it as linguistic. This lesson explores the arguments and the refined position that a verbal definition both presents the meaning and reveals that the word is coined for it.

Guided Path
1
Read al-Taftazani's view
Locate al-Taftazani's claim that verbal definition is a conceptual demand and that it is synonymous with nominal definition.
The text mentions: 'ذهب إلى أن التعريف اللفظي من المطالب التصورية'.
2
Understand the refutation
The commentator refutes al-Taftazani, saying he was misled by terminology and lacks training.
See: 'وإنما أتى عليه من عدم التدرب بالصناعة وقلة التدبر'.
3
Analyze al-Dawani's distinction
Al-Dawani distinguishes between aiming to know the coinage (linguistic) and aiming to conceive the meaning (conceptual).
Find his statement: 'إن كان الغرض معرفة حال اللفظ أنه موضوع لذلك المعنى، كان بحثاً لغوياً...'.
4
Grasp the final nuance
The author criticizes al-Dawani, arguing that a verbal definition must also convey coinage, and merely presenting an already known picture is insufficient.
The final criticism: 'ليس كل ما يفيد إحضار صورة حاصلة تعريفاً لفظياً'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
( والثاني التعريف اللفظي وهو أن لا يكون اللفظ واضح الدلالة ) على معنى ( فيفسر بلفظ أوضح دلالة ) على ذلك المعنى كقولك : الغضنفر الأسد وليس هذا تعريفاً حقيقياً يراد به إفادة تصور غير حاصل، إنما المراد تعيين ما وضع له لفظ الغضنفر من بين سائر المعاني، ليلتفت إلى ذلك قوله : ( وليس هذا تعريفاً حقيقياً إلخ إذ التعريف الحقيقي ما يكون تصوره سبباً لتصور آخر، ولما لم يكن في التعريف اللفظي المغايرة إلا من حيث اللفظ، لا يتحقق ها هنا تصوران متغايران بالذات، أو بالاعتبار فضلاً عن كون أحدهما سبباً للآخر، وما قيل: من أن المفهوم من حيث إنه مدلول اللفظ الأول مغاير لنفسه من حيث إنه مدلول اللفظ الثاني، فبالحيثية الثانية سبب وبالحيثية الأولى مسبب ففيه أن المفاد من التعريف اللفظي إحضار ذات اللفظ الأول بتوسط اللفظ الثاني، لا إحضاره مقيداً بكونه مدلول اللفظ الأول بتوسط إحضاره مقيداً بكونه مدلول اللفظ الثاني . قوله : ( إنما المراد إلخ ) إذ معنى قولنا الغضنفر الأسد أن ما وضع له الغضنفر هو ما وضع له الأسد، فالمستفاد منه تعيين ما وضع له لفظ الغضنفر، والعلم بوضعه له وفيه رد على المحقق التفتازاني، حيث ذهب إلى أن التعريف اللفظي من المطالب التصورية وقال في شرح الشرح : الحد اللفظي عند المحققين هو أن يقصد بيان ما تعقله الواضع فوضع الاسم بإزائه سواء كان بلفظ مرادف أو باللوازم أو بالذاتيات وبهذا عرف الحد الاسمي في التلويح، فجعل اللفظي والاسمي مترادفين وقال الشارح في حواشي العضدي : وإنما أتى عليه من عدم التدرب بالصناعة وقلة التدبر في مقاصد القوم، والاغترار بمجرد اطلاقهم الاسمي في مقام اللفظي، وقال المحقق الدواني : وأنت خبير بأنه إذا كان الغرض معرفة حال اللفظ أنه موضوع لذلك المعنى، كان بحثاً لغوياً خارجاً عن المطالب التصورية، وأما إذا كان الغرض منه تصور معنى اللفظ أي إحضاره، فليس كذلك كما إذا قلنا : الغضنفر موجود فلم يفهم السامع من الغضنفر معنى، ففسرناه بالأسد ليحصل له تصور معناه، فذلك من المطالب التصورية انتهى، وفيه أن هذا التفسير لإحضار صورة حاصلة ليحكم عليه بموجود وليس كل ما يفيد إحضار صورة حاصلة تعريفاً لفظياً، وإلا لكان الألفاظ المعلومة ،أوضاعها تعريفات لفظية لكونها مفيدة إحضار صورة حاصلة، بل هو ما يفيد إحضار صورة حاصلة، وليعلم منه بأن اللفظ موضوع بازائها كقولنا : الغضنفر الأسد على أنه يرد على قوله ففسرنا بالأسد ليحصل معناه أنه إن أراد به أن التفسير يفيد حصول المعنى ابتداء فممنوع، وإن أراد به أنه يفيد بتوسط إفادته العلم بأنه موضوع له ،فمسلم، لكن حينئذ يكون التفسير المذكور للعلم بالوضع وحصول المعنى يتبعه فتدبر.
And the second [is] the verbal definition (al-taʿrīf al-lafẓī), which is that the word is not clear in indication to a meaning, so it is interpreted by a word clearer in indication to that meaning, like your saying: al-ghaḍanfar is the lion. And this is not a real definition intended to produce a conception not already obtained; rather, the intention is to determine what the word 'al-ghaḍanfar' was coined for among other meanings, so that one attends to that. His saying: (and this is not a real definition etc.) because a real definition is that whose conception is a cause of another conception, and since in verbal definition there is no diversity except in terms of wording, there are not two conceptions diverse in essence or even in consideration, let alone one being a cause of the other. And what is said: that the concept insofar as it is the signification of the first word is other than itself insofar as it is the signification of the second word, so by the second relativity it is a cause and by the first it is an effect — in it [is] that the import of verbal definition is to present the essence of the first word by means of the second word, not to present it restricted by being the signification of the first word by means of presenting it restricted by being the signification of the second word. His saying: (rather the intention etc.) because the meaning of our saying 'al-ghaḍanfar is the lion' is that what al-ghaḍanfar was coined for is what the lion was coined for, so what is derived from it is the determination of what the word 'al-ghaḍanfar' was coined for, and knowledge that it was coined for it. In it is a refutation of the investigator al-Taftazani, who held that verbal definition is among the conceptual demands (al-maṭālib al-taṣawwuriyya) and said in Sharḥ al-Sharḥ: The verbal definition according to the investigators is that one intends to explain what the coiner conceived and therefore placed the name for it, whether with a synonymous word, or with concomitants, or with essences; and by this the nominal definition (al-ḥadd al-ismī) was defined in al-Talwīḥ, thus he made the verbal and the nominal synonymous. And the commentator said in the glosses of al-ʿAḍudī: He was only brought to this by lack of training in the craft and lack of reflection on the aims of the people, and being deceived by their mere using of 'nominal' in the place of 'verbal'. And the investigator al-Dawānī said: And you are experienced that if the purpose is to know the state of the word — that it is coined for that meaning — then it is a linguistic inquiry outside the conceptual demands; but if the purpose is to conceive the meaning of the word, i.e., to present it, then it is not so, as when we say: al-ghaḍanfar is existent, and the listener did not understand a meaning from al-ghaḍanfar, so we interpreted it by 'lion' to obtain for him its conception — that is from the conceptual demands. End. And in it [i.e., in this view] is that this interpretation is to present an already obtained picture in order to judge it as existent, and not everything that yields the presentation of an already obtained picture is a verbal definition; otherwise known words whose coinages are known would be verbal definitions because they yield the presentation of an already obtained picture. Rather, it is what yields the presentation of an already obtained picture and also conveys that the word is coined for it, like our saying: al-ghaḍanfar is the lion. And it is objected to his saying: 'so we interpreted it by 'lion' to obtain its meaning' that if he means that the interpretation yields the initial attainment of the meaning, it is denied; if he means that it yields by means of conveying knowledge that it is coined for it, it is conceded; but then that interpretation is for the knowledge of the coinage, and the attainment of the meaning follows. So consider carefully.
Source pages p. 9

Commentary
This passage discusses verbal definition (al-taʿrīf al-lafẓī), which is used when a word is not clear in its indication; it is then explained by a more clearly indicative word, such as 'al-ghaḍanfar is the lion'. The passage argues that this is not a real definition intended to produce a new conception; rather, it simply assigns the word's referent. The commentary elaborates that a real definition involves a conception that causes another conception, but in verbal definition, there is no diversity of conceptions, only a diversity in wording. A potential counterargument—that the concept as signified by the first word is different from the concept as signified by the second—is rejected because verbal definition only presents the essence of the first word, not the concept under a description. The passage then addresses a debate: al-Taftazani considered verbal definition as a conceptual demand, but the commentator refutes this, citing al-Dawani's distinction: if the aim is to know the word's coinage, it is linguistic; if to conceive the meaning, it is conceptual. However, the commentator further criticizes al-Dawani, arguing that not every presentation of an already known picture is a verbal definition; it must also inform that the word is coined for that picture. The example 'al-ghaḍanfar is the lion' achieves both. The passage ends with a careful nuance: the interpretation yields meaning only indirectly through knowledge of coinage.

Key Terms
مطالب تصورية
حد اسمي
وضع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A verbal definition is always a conceptual demand.
Correction: It is only a conceptual demand if the aim is to conceive the meaning; otherwise it is linguistic. But even then, it must also convey the coinage.

Checkpoint
Why does the author criticize al-Dawani's example of 'al-ghaḍanfar is existent'?
A) Because 'al-ghaḍanfar' is not a real word
B) Because it presents the meaning but does not indicate coinage
C) Because it is a real definition, not a verbal one
D) Because it uses a synonym incorrectly

Key Takeaway
A true verbal definition both presents the meaning and indicates the coinage of the word.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you learn a new Islamic term, do you merely seek a synonym, or do you ensure you understand the exact coinage and usage? How often do you settle for a superficial definition instead of grasping the precise intent of the scholar who coined it?
?
Al-Taftazani was criticized for lack of training. In your own study of aqeedah, are you content with surface-level understanding, or do you push to master the technical distinctions that guard against error? What specific area of your knowledge might be built on a misconception similar to Taftazani's?
?
The author insists that a true verbal definition both presents the meaning and indicates coinage. In your daily practice, do you treat words of the Quran and Sunnah with such precision? Where might you be missing the intention behind a term, and how will you correct that starting today?

The Proverb 'Stripping the Qatād' Explained
Explains the proverb 'خرط القتاد دونه' with lexical notes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the meaning and usage of the Arabic proverb 'خرط القتاد دونه' in classical texts.

Core Idea
Even the most difficult tasks can be compared using vivid imagery from nature.

This lesson unpacks a striking proverb used by the commentator to describe the intensity of the effort required. The image of stripping thorns from a desert shrub evokes a powerful sense of hardship, and the commentator offers two possible interpretations of its comparative 'dūnahu'. Understanding this phrase helps you appreciate the rhetorical depth of scholarly Arabic.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Notice how the commentator breaks down the proverb's components.
Pay attention to the lexical notes on 'qatād' and 'kharṭ'.
2
Identify the two interpretations of 'dūnahu'
The commentator gives two possible meanings for 'dūnahu': either the stripping is easier than the real task, or it is a prerequisite obstacle. Contrast these nuances.
3
Reflect on the use of proverbs in scholarly texts
Consider why the commentator includes this explanation. Proverbs make abstract difficulties tangible, aiding comprehension.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( وكان خرط القتاد دونه ) القتاد شجر له شوك صعب والخرط سوق اليد من أعلاه إلى أسفله ليندفع به شوكه وقولهم : خرط القتاد دونه مثل في الأمر الأشق، ومعنى دونه أن هذا الخرط أدنى منه في المشقة، أو أنه قبيله وهو محفوف به لا يمكن الوصول إليه بدون هذا الخرط .
قوله : ( وكذا يتجه على الحد النقض والمعارضة) أي ما هو شبيه بهما باعتبار الدعاوى الضمنية، وإلا فالاصطلاحيان إنما يجريان بعد إقامة الدليل على المطلوب.
His saying: (and it was like stripping the qatād is more difficult [dūnahu]) – the qatād is a thorny tree, and stripping is passing the hand from its top to its bottom to remove its thorns. Their saying 'stripping the qatād is more difficult' is a proverb for the most difficult matter; the meaning of 'dūnahu' is that this stripping is easier compared to it, or that it is of the same kind but surrounded by it, one cannot reach it without this stripping.
His saying: (and likewise the limit is subject to naqḍ and mu‘āraḍa) i.e., something resembling them due to the implicit claims; otherwise, the technical terms apply only after establishing evidence for the sought conclusion.
Source pages p. 11

Commentary
This passage clarifies two expressions. First, it explains the proverb 'stripping the qatād is more difficult' (خرط القتاد دونه): the qatād is a thorny plant, and stripping it by hand is painful; the proverb is used for extremely difficult tasks. The commentator notes whether 'dūnahu' means this stripping is easier than the task, or that it is a preliminary obstacle. Second, it explains the phrase 'the limit is subject to naqḍ and mu‘āraḍa' by saying these terms apply not directly but by way of resemblance because of the implicit claims (about the correctness of the definition). In technical usage, naqḍ and mu‘āraḍa are applied only after a proof has been established, but here they are used loosely for the objections to definitional claims.

Key Terms
خرط القتاد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the commentary, what does 'dūnahu' in the proverb 'stripping the qatād is dūnahu' mean?
A) This stripping is easier than the task
B) This stripping is the same as the task
C) Both A and B as possible interpretations
D) Neither

Key Takeaway
The proverb 'خرط القتاد دونه' uses the painful act of stripping a thorny tree as a metaphor for an even greater hardship.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of the single most difficult task you have faced in the past year. Did you see it as 'stripping the qatād'—a painful but necessary step—or did you resent it as an obstacle? How would viewing it as a prerequisite 'stripping' change your attitude toward the hardship that still lies ahead?
?
The proverb describes a difficulty so great that even the excruciating act of stripping a thorny tree is easier by comparison. What is one area of your life where you have been avoiding the 'stripping' (the initial pain) and therefore remain stuck, unable to reach what you truly want? What concrete step will you take this week to begin that stripping, trusting it is the only way forward?

How Naqḍ and Mu‘āraḍa Apply to Definitions
Clarifies the indirect applicability of naqḍ and mu‘āraḍa to definitions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the logical methods of naqḍ and mu‘āraḍa apply to definitions only indirectly, by virtue of implicit claims.

Core Idea
Naqḍ and mu‘āraḍa critique proofs, not definitions—unless definitions contain hidden claims.

This lesson clarifies an important methodological nuance: the terms naqḍ (contradiction) and mu‘āraḍa (opposition) are technical tools used after a proof is established. But when applied to definitions, they function metaphorically, because a definition implicitly asserts its own correctness. The commentator distinguishes the technical from the figurative use.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin with the Arabic and English. Notice the commentator's phrasing: 'shabīh bihimā' (resembling them).
2
Identify the two parts: resemblance and condition
First, the commentator explains that they apply by resemblance due to implicit claims. Second, he states the technical condition: only after a proof is established.
The implicit claims are the definition's claim to be correct and comprehensive.
3
Understand why definitions involve implicit claims
When someone gives a definition, they are implicitly claiming that it is accurate and covers all cases. This makes it open to objection, though not in the strict technical sense.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( وكان خرط القتاد دونه ) القتاد شجر له شوك صعب والخرط سوق اليد من أعلاه إلى أسفله ليندفع به شوكه وقولهم : خرط القتاد دونه مثل في الأمر الأشق، ومعنى دونه أن هذا الخرط أدنى منه في المشقة، أو أنه قبيله وهو محفوف به لا يمكن الوصول إليه بدون هذا الخرط .
قوله : ( وكذا يتجه على الحد النقض والمعارضة) أي ما هو شبيه بهما باعتبار الدعاوى الضمنية، وإلا فالاصطلاحيان إنما يجريان بعد إقامة الدليل على المطلوب.
His saying: (and it was like stripping the qatād is more difficult [dūnahu]) – the qatād is a thorny tree, and stripping is passing the hand from its top to its bottom to remove its thorns. Their saying 'stripping the qatād is more difficult' is a proverb for the most difficult matter; the meaning of 'dūnahu' is that this stripping is easier compared to it, or that it is of the same kind but surrounded by it, one cannot reach it without this stripping.
His saying: (and likewise the limit is subject to naqḍ and mu‘āraḍa) i.e., something resembling them due to the implicit claims; otherwise, the technical terms apply only after establishing evidence for the sought conclusion.
Source pages p. 11

Commentary
This passage clarifies two expressions. First, it explains the proverb 'stripping the qatād is more difficult' (خرط القتاد دونه): the qatād is a thorny plant, and stripping it by hand is painful; the proverb is used for extremely difficult tasks. The commentator notes whether 'dūnahu' means this stripping is easier than the task, or that it is a preliminary obstacle. Second, it explains the phrase 'the limit is subject to naqḍ and mu‘āraḍa' by saying these terms apply not directly but by way of resemblance because of the implicit claims (about the correctness of the definition). In technical usage, naqḍ and mu‘āraḍa are applied only after a proof has been established, but here they are used loosely for the objections to definitional claims.

Key Terms
النقض
المعارضة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Naqḍ and mu‘āraḍa can be applied to definitions in the same technical sense as to arguments.
Correction: They apply indirectly, resembling technical use, only because definitions have implicit claims; the technical process requires an established proof.

Checkpoint
In what way do naqḍ and mu‘āraḍa apply to definitions according to the commentator?
A) They apply directly and technically.
B) They apply only after a proof is established.
C) They apply in a metaphorical sense due to implicit claims.
D) They do not apply at all.

Key Takeaway
Naqḍ and mu‘āraḍa critique definitions only indirectly, through the implicit claims they contain, not as full-fledged logical objections.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you define your beliefs about God or the unseen, do you ever examine the hidden assumptions those definitions carry? What implicit claims have you accepted without scrutiny, and will you challenge them today?
?
If someone objects to a definition you hold dear, do you dismiss it as irrelevant because definitions aren't proofs? The passage shows that definitions do carry weight. Whose definition of tawḥīd, for example, are you relying on, and have you verified its implicit claims?
?
Your intellectual habits shape your faith. Are you willing to submit every definition you hold—about justice, mercy, or Allah's attributes—to the same rigorous critique you demand of others? What will you do to ensure your definitions are not just inherited but rationally affirmed?

The Three Types of Inference: Qiyas, Istiqra', Tamthil
Classification and definition of three inference types

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definitions of qiyas, istiqra', and tamthil, and how they differ in the direction of inference while being essentially the same logical operation.

Core Idea
All inference moves either general to specific, specific to general, or specific to specific—but it's all syllogism with a middle term.

In this passage, the author classifies three types of logical inference according to the direction of reasoning: from universal to particular, particular to universal, and particular to particular. These categories are foundational in Islamic logic and help us understand how arguments are constructed. Let’s see how each one works.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the definitions of each type and the final remark that differences are in aspect, not essence.
The passage is a single continuous paragraph.
2
Identify the three types
Find the definitions of qiyas, istiqra', and tamthil. For each, note the direction of inference: universal→particular, particular→universal, particular→particular.
3
Understand the unifying principle
The author concludes that the divisions differ only in consideration, not essence. Reflect on how all three can be reduced to syllogism if the common element is taken as the middle term.
This is a key insight of the passage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد السادس - المقصد الثالث : الاستدلال الكلي والجزئي
تحققه في جزئي مخصوص، ثم استدل منه على ثبوت ذلك الحال لأمر آخر، أو انتفائه عن ذلك الأمر لكونه جزئياً لذلك الكلي، ومندرجاً تحته فهو القياس، وإن كان المعلوم ثبوت حال الجزئي من حيث خصوصه ثم استدل منه على ثبوته للكلي بأن تتبع جميع جزئياته أو أكثرها، فعلم ثبوت ذلك الحال لها، ثم انتقل منه إلى ثبوته لذلك الأمر الكلي، فهو الاستقراء وإن كان المعلوم ثبوت حال الجزئي معين ثم استدل منه على ثبوته لجزئي آخر مندرج معه، تحت ثالث بأن علم علية الأمر المشترك لثبوت ذلك الحال في الجزئي المستدل عنه فوجد ذلك الأمر في الجزئي المستدل عليه فحكم بثبوت ذلك الحال له فهو التمثيل وبالجملة الفرق بين الأقسام باعتبار الحيثيات والاعتبارات لا بحسب الذات، حتى يصير الاستقراء والتمثيل أيضاً قياساً إذا جعل الأمر المشترك بين الجزئيات أوسط .
The sixth observatory - the third objective: Universal and particular inference. Its realization in a specific particular, then inferring from it the affirmation of that state for another thing, or its negation from that thing by virtue of it being a particular of that universal and subsumed under it, that is qiyas (syllogism). And if what is known is the affirmation of the state of the particular in terms of its specificity, then inferring from it to its affirmation for the universal by surveying all its particulars or most of them, so the affirmation of that state for them is known, then transferring from that to its affirmation for that universal thing, that is istiqra' (induction). And if what is known is the affirmation of the state of a specific particular, then inferring from it to its affirmation for another particular subsumed with it under a third, by knowing the causality of the common thing for the affirmation of that state in the particular from which inference is made, and finding that common thing in the particular to which inference is made, so he judges the affirmation of that state for it, that is tamthil (analogy). In summary, the difference between the divisions is in terms of aspects and considerations, not in essence, such that istiqra' and tamthil also become qiyas if the common thing among the particulars is made the middle term.
Source pages p. 14

Commentary
This passage is a classification of three types of inference based on the direction of reasoning. The author defines qiyas (syllogism) as reasoning from a universal to a particular: one knows the state of a universal and applies it to a particular subsumed under it. Istiqra' (induction) is reasoning from particular to universal: one observes the state in many particulars and infers it for the universal. Tamthil (analogy) is reasoning from particular to particular: one finds a common cause in one particular and transfers the judgment to another particular sharing that cause. The author emphasizes that the distinction is not essential but based on perspective; if the common element is taken as a middle term, both induction and analogy can be reformulated as syllogisms (qiyas).

تمثيل

جزئي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Induction and analogy are fundamentally different types of inference from syllogism.
Correction: The author states that they differ only in perspective, not essence; with a common middle term, they become syllogisms.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what distinguishes tamthil (analogy) from qiyas (syllogism)?
A) Tamthil infers from universal to particular
B) Tamthil infers from particular to particular
C) Tamthil uses a middle term
D) Tamthil is not a valid inference

Key Takeaway
The three types of inference differ in direction but share the same logical structure when reduced to a middle term.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Are you using qiyas (from a universal principle), istiqra' (from observed particulars), or tamthil (comparing to other things)? Examine your own reasoning and ask: is it sound according to the standard described in this passage? What would it take to strengthen your inference?
?
The passage says that the difference between the types is in perspective, not essence. How does this affect the way you evaluate arguments you encounter in religious discussions? Can you think of a time when you dismissed an analogy as weak, but could have reframed it as a syllogism? What would change in your assessment?
?
Induction requires surveying either all or most particulars. In matters of faith, what are the 'particulars' that you consider when forming a general belief? Are you relying on enough evidence? How can you ensure you are not drawing a hasty generalization?

The Definition of Qiyas and Its Conditions
The author defines qiyas and explains its conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the technical definition of qiyas (syllogism) as a composite statement from which another statement necessarily follows, and the key conditions of intrinsic entailment and the exceptions like qiyas al-musawat.

Core Idea
A valid syllogism is a composed statement that forces a conclusion by its own internal structure.

In this passage, the author defines qiyas (syllogism), the foundation of logical inference in Islamic thought. He carefully chooses each word to avoid common misunderstandings, and he introduces crucial conditions such as 'by itself' (li-dhatihi) and exceptions like the syllogism of equality. Understanding this definition is essential for building sound arguments.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the structure: qiyas is defined as a statement composed of propositions.
Pay attention to the words 'qawl', 'mu'allaf', and 'qadaya'.
2
Identify why 'mu'allaf' is necessary
The author explains that without 'mu'allaf', one might misunderstand qiyas as a part of the premises rather than a composition. Understand this nuance.
3
Grasp the condition 'li-dhatihi'
The entailment must arise from the premises themselves, not from an extraneous premise. This ensures the syllogism is self-sufficient.
Two exceptions are given: qiyas al-musawat and cases using contrapositive.
4
Reflect on the example
The example 'A=B, B=C' shows that equality is transitive, so 'A=C' follows. Contrast with disparity, which is not transitive.
This illustrates the condition of intrinsic entailment.

المرصد السادس - المقصد الثالث : الاستدلال الكلي والجزئي
القياس وعرف بأنه قول : ( أي مركب إما مسموع وهو جنس للقياس المسموع، وإما معقول وهو جنس للقياس المعقول، وإنما احتيج إلى قوله ( مؤلف ) لأنك إذا قلت : قول من قضايا تبادر منه أنه بعض منها، فصرح بأنه مؤلف ( من قضايا ) وأراد بها ما فوق الواحدة ( متى سلمت تلك القضايا، سواء كانت مسلمة في نفس الأمر أو لا لزم عنه ) أي عن ذلك القول ( لذاته ) أي لا لمقدمة أجنبية غير لازمة لشيء من المقدمتين، كما في قياس المساواة أو غريبة لازمة لإحدى المقدمتين مغايرة لها في طرفيها، كما إذا بين اللزوم بعكس النقيض ( قول آخر أراد به المعقول لأن المسموع )
قوله : (إما مسموع) قابل المسموع دون الملفوظ بالمعقول إشارة إلى أن القياس الملفوظ إنما يتحقق عند إفادة الغير.
قوله : ( لأنك إذا قلت : إِلخ) وذلك لأن القول في أصل اللغة مصدر استعمل بمعنى المقول، واشتهر في المركب وليس في مفهومه التركيب، حتى يتعلق الجار به لغواً، فلو قيل: قول من قضايا يكون تعلق الجار به استقراراً أي كائن من قضايا فيتبادر منه أنه بعض منها بخلاف ما إذا قيل : مؤلف فإنه يفهم منه التركيب، فيتعلق به لغواً .
قوله : ( كما في قياس المساواة ) وهو ما يكون متعلق المحمول في الصغرى موضوعاً في الكبرى، نحو ا مساو لب وب مساو لج، فإنه ينتج ا مساو لج بواسطة صدق أن مساوي المساوي مساو ولا ينتج ا مباين لب وب مباين لج لعدم صدق مباين المباين مباين.
The sixth observatory - the third objective: Universal and particular inference. Qiyas (syllogism) is defined as a statement (qawl), i.e., a composite (murakkab), either audible (masmu') which is a genus for audible syllogism, or intelligible (ma'qul) which is a genus for intelligible syllogism. The need for his saying 'composed' (mu'allaf) is because if you say 'a statement from propositions', it immediately suggests it is a part of them, so he explicitly says it is composed of propositions, meaning more than one. Whenever those propositions are granted, whether they are actually granted or not, there follows from it (i.e., from that statement) by itself (bi-dhatihi), i.e., not by an extraneous premise not necessary to either of the two premises, as in the syllogism of equality (qiyas al-musawat), nor by a strange premise necessarily attached to one of the premises but different from it in its two ends, as when entailment is shown by contrapositive (aks al-naqid). (Another statement) meaning the intelligible, because the audible... His saying: 'either audible' contrasts audible with intelligible, indicating that the vocalized syllogism (al-qiyas al-malfuz) is only realized when conveying to others. His saying: 'because if you say etc.' That is because 'statement' (qawl) in the original language is a verbal noun used in the sense of what is said (maqul), and it is famous for the composite, but composition is not in its concept, so that the preposition attaches to it as an adverb; if one said 'a statement from propositions', the preposition would attach as a stable adverbial meaning 'consisting of propositions', so it would immediately suggest it is a part of them, unlike if one said 'composed' (mu'allaf), for composition is understood from it, so the preposition attaches as an adverb. His saying: 'as in the syllogism of equality' which is when the predicate of the minor premise is the subject of the major, like 'A equals B' and 'B equals C', it yields 'A equals C' by virtue of the truth that 'the equal of the equal is equal', and it does not yield 'A is disparate from B and B is disparate from C' because 'the disparate of the disparate is disparate' is not true.
Source pages p. 14

Commentary
This passage defines qiyas (syllogism) and explains its technical terms. The definition states: qiyas is 'a statement composed of propositions such that when granted, another statement necessarily follows by itself.' The commentator clarifies each part: 'statement' (qawl) here means a composite (murakkab), either audible (vocalized) or intelligible (mental). The word 'composed' (mu'allaf) is needed because if one simply said 'statement from propositions', it might be understood as a part of those propositions, not a composite. 'By itself' (li-dhatihi) means the entailment arises from the premises themselves, not from an extraneous premise. Two exceptions are noted: (1) the syllogism of equality (qiyas al-musawat), where the middle term links the extremes directly, and (2) cases where the entailment is shown by contrapositive (aks al-naqid), which involves an additional premise. The commentator then explains why 'audible' and 'intelligible' are distinguished, and why 'mu'allaf' is crucial: 'qawl' alone does not imply composition, so using 'mu'allaf' clarifies that the statement is composed of propositions. Finally, the example of qiyas al-musawat illustrates the condition of entailment: in 'A=B, B=C' the conclusion 'A=C' follows by the transitivity of equality, but 'A is disparate from B and B from C' does not yield 'A is disparate from C' because disparity is not transitive.

قول
مؤلف
قضايا
لزوم ذاتي
قياس المساواة
عكس النقيض

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the word 'qawl' by itself implies composition, so adding 'mu'allaf' is redundant.
Correction: The text explains that 'qawl' alone does not imply composition; it could be understood as a part of the premises. Adding 'mu'allaf' clarifies that qiyas is a composition of propositions.

Checkpoint

C) To avoid the misunderstanding that qiyas is a part of the propositions
D) Because 'qawl' is not a technical term

Key Takeaway
Qiyas is technically defined as a statement composed of propositions from which another statement necessarily follows by itself, with the crucial condition that the entailment is intrinsic.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Are you quick to accept arguments that 'sound right' without checking whether the conclusion truly follows from the premises by themselves? What standard of proof do you actually apply to your most deeply held beliefs?
?
The condition 'by itself' (li-dhatihi) excludes relying on unstated assumptions. Is there an area of your faith or life where you rely on an argument that, if examined, would depend on a hidden premise you never tested?
?
This definition forces you to distinguish between an argument that compels belief by its own structure and one that only seems convincing because of external factors. When was the last time you honestly admitted that a cherished belief was based on a flawed argument? What change does that demand?

Induction: Definition and Types (Complete and Incomplete)
Al-Jurjani defines induction and distinguishes complete from incomplete induction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand what induction is in logic, and how complete induction yields certainty while incomplete induction yields only opinion.

Core Idea
Induction proves a universal by its particulars; only complete induction guarantees certainty.

Al-Jurjani introduces induction as a mode of reasoning from particular to universal. This passage defines it and distinguishes between the certain and the probable. As you read, note how the example of number demonstrates certainty, while the animal jaw example shows the limitation of incomplete induction.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the linguistic derivation of 'istisra' from 'following a trace.'
2
Identify the definition of induction
Find how Al-Jurjani defines induction logically: establishing a universal judgment because it holds in its particulars.
3
Compare the two types
Note the two types: complete induction (all particulars examined) yields certainty; incomplete induction (some particulars) yields only opinion. See the examples given.
The number example is a divisive syllogism, equivalent to complete induction.
4
Reflect on the counterexample
Consider the crocodile example. Why does it show that incomplete induction is not certain?
Because the crocodile moves its upper jaw, contradicting the general claim.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإما بالجزئي على الكلي ( أي بحال الجزئي على حال الكلي ) وهو الاستقراء من استقريت الشيء إذا تتبعته ) وهو إثبات الحكم الكلي لثبوته في جزئياته، إما كلها فيفيد اليقين كقولنا : العدد إما زوج وإما فرد وكل زوج يعده الواحد، وكل فرد يعده الواحد ، فكل عدد يعده الواحد ومثل ذلك يسمى قياسا مقسماً، واستقراء تاماً أو بعضها فلا يفيد إلا الظن لجواز أن يكون ما لم يستقرأ من جزئيات ذلك الكلي على خلاف ما استقرئ منها ( كما يقال : كل حيوان يحرك عند المضغ فكه الأسفل لأن الإنسان والفرس وغيرهما مما نشاهده) من الحيوانات ( كذلك مع أن التمساح بخلافه فإنه عند المضغ يحرك فكه الأعلى
And as for [the inference] by the particular to the universal (i.e., by the state of the particular to the state of the universal), it is induction (istisra'), derived from 'I traced something when I followed it.' It is establishing the universal judgment because it is established in its particulars. If [it is established] in all of them, it yields certainty (yaqin), like our statement: Number is either even or odd, and every even is divisible by one, and every odd is divisible by one, so every number is divisible by one. Such is called a divisive syllogism (qiyas muqassam) and complete induction (istisra' tam). Or [if established] in some of them, it yields only opinion (zann) because it is possible that what was not examined among the particulars of that universal is contrary to what was examined, as is said: Every animal moves its lower jaw when chewing, because man, horse, and others that we observe do so, while the crocodile is contrary to that, for when it chews it moves its upper jaw.
Source pages p. 15

Commentary
This passage defines induction. It first gives the linguistic derivation from 'following a trace'. Then it defines it logically: establishing a universal judgment because it holds in its particulars. Two types are distinguished: complete induction, where all particulars are examined, yields certainty; an example is the divisive syllogism about number. Incomplete induction, where only some particulars are examined, yields only opinion because a counterexample may exist, as demonstrated by the crocodile example. The commentator clarifies that complete induction is equivalent to a divisive syllogism.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Induction always gives certainty.
Correction: Only complete induction yields certainty; incomplete induction yields only probability and can be wrong, as shown by the crocodile counterexample.

Analogy and the Three Types of Inference
Al-Jurjani defines analogy and classifies inference into three types by inclusion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of analogy (tamthil) and how inference is classified into three types based on the relation of inclusion (ishtimal).

Core Idea
All valid inference requires a specific suitability of inclusion between evidence and proven.

This passage from Al-Jurjani's work defines analogy and presents a threefold classification of inference. The key concept is that the relationship between evidence and proven must involve 'containment' (ishtimal)—either the evidence contains the proven, the proven contains the evidence, or a third thing contains both. Understanding this classification clarifies the logical structure of analogy and its relation to syllogism and induction.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how Al-Jurjani defines analogy and then distinguishes three types of inference based on inclusion.
2
Identify the three types
Look for the three types: syllogism (qiyas), induction (istisra'), and analogy (tamthil). Understand the direction of inclusion for each.
Syllogism: universal contains particular; Induction: particular contains universal; Analogy: a third contains both.
3
Understand the common cause
Analogy is based on participation in a common cause (illah) which is a universal encompassing both particulars. That universal is the 'third thing' that contains both.
4
Test your understanding
Answer the checkpoint question to see if you can identify which type corresponds to which inclusion direction.
The question is from the passage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما بجزئي على جزئي) أي بحاله على حاله ) وهو التمثيل ويسميه الفقهاء قياساً، وهو مشاركة أمر لأمر آخر ( في علة الحكم وهي الكلي الشامل لذينك الجزئيين، قالوا: لا بد بين الدليل والمدلول من مناسبة مخصوصة، وتلك إما باشتمال الدليل على المدلول وهو القياس، أو باشتمال المدلول على الدليل وهو الاستقراء أو باشتمال أمر ثالث عليهما وهو التمثيل
And as for [the inference] from particular to particular (i.e., by its state to its state), it is analogy (tamthil), which jurists call qiyas. It is the participation of one thing with another in the cause of the judgment, i.e., the universal that encompasses both of those particulars. They said: There must be a specific suitability between the evidence and the proven. That [suitability] is either by the evidence containing the proven, which is syllogism (qiyas), or by the proven containing the evidence, which is induction (istisra'), or by a third thing containing both, which is analogy (tamthil).
Source pages p. 15

Commentary
This passage defines analogy (tamthil) and presents a threefold classification of inference based on the relation of inclusion (ishtimal). Analogy is inference from one particular to another because they share a common cause (the universal). The classification: (1) syllogism—the evidence (universal) contains the proven (particular); (2) induction—the proven (universal) contains the evidence (particulars); (3) analogy—a third thing (the common universal/cause) contains both evidence and proven. All valid inference must have this suitability of inclusion.

Key Terms

القياس
الاستقراء
المناسبة
الاشتمال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
Analogy is inference from particular to particular based on their common cause, and it is one of three types of inference classified by the direction of inclusion between evidence and proven.

The Fourth Type of Inference: Reduction to Analogy
Al-Jurjani discusses the objection of a fourth type and reduces it to analogy or denies relation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Jurjani reduces the proposed fourth type of inference (from universal to universal) to analogy or denies any relation, and why this classification matters in logical theory.

Core Idea
Inference from universal to universal is not a separate type; it either reduces to analogy or has no logical connection.

In this passage, al-Jurjani addresses an objection that there is a fourth type of inference beyond the three already discussed: inference from universal to universal. He argues that such inference either falls under analogy (when both universals share a common third universal) or is invalid (when no relation exists). He also engages with a counterexample involving equal universals to refine his position.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
First, read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation. Notice the flow: objection, response, counter-objection, and the beginning of a further response.
The passage cuts off at the beginning of the final answer. The main argument is completed before that.
2
Identify the two cases presented
The author gives two cases: (1) the two universals fall under a third common universal, (2) they do not. Understand the consequence of each case.
Case 1 reduces to analogy; case 2 has no relation.
3
Analyze the counter-objection about equal universals
The objector presents an example with equal universals (human and rational) that seems to be a valid syllogism outside the three types. Consider how this challenges the author's reduction.
The author's response is cut off, but you can infer he likely argues it still falls under syllogism or analogy.

If you say: Here is another division besides the three mentioned, namely inference from universal to universal. We say: If the two universals in question fall under a third common universal that necessitates the judgment, then they are particulars (in a relative sense) to that third universal, which is the cause of the judgment. For 'particular' here means what is subsumed under another, i.e., relative particular (juz'i idafi), not that which prevents sharing in notion, i.e., the real particular (juz'i haqiqi). Then inference from one to the other falls under analogy, not an independent division. But if they do not fall under a third common universal that is the cause of the judgment, then there is no relation between them, so the judgment of one cannot be transferred to the other at all. Then it might be objected: It does not follow from their not falling under a third that there is no relation enabling the transfer of judgment, for if you say 'Every human is rational and every rational is animal,' you have inferred from one of the two equal universals to the other, not from the universal to the particular. Such an inference is outside the types you mentioned, yet it is unanimously considered a syllogism, which is why some said: If one infers from the universal to the particular or from one of two equal universals to the other, that is syllogism. I say: The point is that we...
Source pages p. 15

Commentary
This passage addresses the objection that there is a fourth type of inference: from universal to universal. The author first argues that if the two universals fall under a third common universal (the cause), then they are relative particulars and the inference actually becomes analogy. If they do not, there is no relation and no transfer of judgment. The objection uses the example of two equal universals (e.g., human and rational) where inference from one to the other is a valid syllogism but does not fit the three types. The author begins an answer ('I say: The point is...') but the text is cut off. The passage shows the complexity of classifying inference and the need to account for cases of equal universals.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think inference from universal to universal is always a separate fourth type independent of analogy or syllogism.
Correction: Al-Jurjani shows that such inference either reduces to analogy (when sharing a cause) or is invalid (when no relation). Even the equal universals case is generally considered a syllogism, not an independent type.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
Inference from universal to universal is not a distinct logical type; it either reduces to analogy when a common cause exists or is invalid otherwise, and the case of equal universals is handled as syllogism.

Reasoning from Universal to Universal and Its Condition
Defines condition for universal-to-universal reasoning to be analogical.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the condition under which reasoning from one universal to another is considered valid analogical reasoning (tamthil) and when it fails.

Core Idea
Universal-to-universal reasoning is only valid if both share a common cause.

This passage from the commentary on al-Mawaqif clarifies a subtle point in Islamic logic. The author addresses the question: when can we infer a judgment from one universal to another? He explains that such inference is not a separate type but falls under analogical reasoning (tamthil) under a specific condition, and without it, no inference is possible.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation to grasp the overall argument.
Pay attention to the conditional structure: 'if...then...otherwise'.
2
Identify the condition for valid reasoning
Find the condition that makes the two universals into relative particulars and thus brings the reasoning under tamthil.
The condition involves a third universal that is the cause of the judgment.
3
Understand the types of 'particular'
Distinguish between relative particular (subsumed under another) and real particular (cannot be shared). This distinction is crucial for the argument.
The passage explicitly contrasts these two meanings.
4
Recognize the consequence when condition is absent
Note what happens when the two universals do not fall under a common cause: no relation exists and judgment cannot transfer.
The commentator explains this with the phrase 'fala yata'adda'.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الذي هو المرصد السادس - المقصد الثالث : الاستدلال الكلي والجزئي الاستدلال بكلي على كلي قلنا إن دخلا أي الكليان المذكوران (تحت) كلي ( ثالث مشترك ) بينهما ( يقتضي الحكم فهما جزئيان (له ) أي لذلك الكلي الثالث علة الحكم ) لأن المراد بالجزئي هاهنا المندرج تحت الغير، وهو المسمى بالإضافي لا ما يمنع نفس تصور الشركة فيه ( أعني ( المسمى بالحقيقي) وحينئذ كان الاستدلال بأحدهما على الآخر داخلاً في التمثيل لا قسما برأسه ( وإلا ) أي وإن لم يدخلا تحت ثالث مشترك هو علة الحكم فلا تعلق بينهما فلا يتعدى حكم أحدهما إلى الآخر أصلاً قوله : ( فلا يتعدى إلخ ) إذ المفروض أن ليس شيء منهما جزئياً للآخر ولا جزئياً لثالث .
This is the sixth observatory - third goal: Universal and particular reasoning. Reasoning from universal to universal: we said if the two mentioned universals enter under a third universal common between them that necessitates the judgment, then they are two particulars relative to that third universal, which is the cause of the judgment, because what is meant by particular here is that which is subsumed under another, called relative particular, not that which prevents the very conception of sharing in it, i.e., the real particular. Then reasoning from one of them to the other falls under analogical reasoning, not as an independent division. Otherwise, if they do not enter under a third universal that is the cause of the judgment, there is no relation between them, so the judgment of one does not transfer to the other at all. His saying: 'So it does not transfer, etc.' because the assumption is that neither of them is a particular of the other nor a particular of a third.
Source pages p. 16

Commentary
This passage defines the classification of reasoning from one universal to another. The commentator first states that if two universals both fall under a third universal that is the cause of the judgment, then each is a relative particular of that third universal. In that case, reasoning from one to the other is actually analogical reasoning (tamthil), not a separate type. The term 'particular' here means relative particular, i.e., subsumed under another, not the real particular that cannot be shared. If they are not under a common cause, then there is no relation and the judgment does not transfer. The commentator then explains the phrase 'so it does not transfer' by noting that neither is a particular of the other nor of a third.

Correction: The passage shows it is only valid when both universals share a common cause, and then it falls under analogical reasoning (tamthil), not an independent division.

Key Takeaway

Reasoning from Equal Universals
Objection to the rule and two replies defending it.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how reasoning from equal universals is defended by the commentator as ultimately a case of universal-to-particular reasoning.

Core Idea
Reasoning from equal universals always reduces to reasoning from universal to particular.

The objection challenges the previous claim that reasoning from universal to universal requires a third cause. The commentator shows that even in the example given, the reasoning ultimately relies on a universal concept applied to particulars. This lesson clarifies that apparent counterexamples are still consistent with the original rule.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage and translation, focusing on the objection and the example 'Every human is rational, every rational is animal'.
Notice that the objector claims this reasoning does not use a third cause.
2
Understand the objector's challenge
The objector argues that reasoning from one universal to another (equal universals) is agreed to be a valid syllogism, contradicting the earlier rule.
3
Examine the commentator's first reply
The commentator explains that the reasoning actually goes from the universal concept 'rational' to each particular human, so it is universal-to-particular after all.
The judgment of animality is established for each human because of the concept 'rational'.
4
Consider the second reply and its limitation
The second reply treats each equal as a relative particular of the other, but the commentator notes this fails when the universals are not co-extensive (e.g., 'Some animal is black').

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل) لا يلزم من عدم دخولهما تحت ثالث، يقتضي الحكم أن لا يكون بينهما تعلق يتعدى به حكم أحدهما إلى الآخر، فإنك إذا قلت كل إنسان ناطق وكل ناطق حيوان فقد استدللت بأحد الكليين ( المتساويين على الآخر لا بالكلي على الجزئي ) فمثل هذا خارج عما ذكرتموه من أنواع الاستدلال، مع أنه من قبيل القياس اتفاقا ولهذا قال بعضهم: إنه إن استدل بالكلي على الجزئي أو بأحد المتساويين على الآخر فهو القياس (قلت : المقصود أنا أثبتنا في المثال المذكور لكل واحد واحد من أفراد الإنسان الحيوانية لاتصافه بمفهوم الناطق، فإن ملاحظة مفهوم الناطق هو ( الأمر ( الذي يفيدنا الحكم بها أي بالحيوانية على كل واحد واحد من أفراد الإنسان والحاصل أن الاستدلال بمفهوم الناطق على كل واحد من جزئيات الإنسان، ولا شك أن كل واحد منها جزئي لمفهوم الناطق، فرجع إلى الاستدلال بالكلي على الجزئي، وقد يجاب أيضاً بأن كل واحد من المتساويين يعد جزئياً إضافياً للآخر، إذ يقع كل منهما موضوعاً للآخر كلياً، وهو معنى اندراجه فيه ولا يخفى بعده ، وعدم جريانه في مثل قولنا : بعض الحيوان أسود وكل أسود كذا،
If it is said: It does not follow from their not entering under a third that necessitates the judgment that there is no relation between them by which the judgment of one transfers to the other, for if you say 'Every human is rational and every rational is animal', you have reasoned from one of the two equal universals to the other, not from universal to particular. So such a case is outside the types of reasoning you mentioned, while it is by agreement a syllogism. For this reason, some said: If one reasons from universal to particular or from one of two equals to the other, it is a syllogism. I say: The point is that we established in the mentioned example for every single individual of humans animality because of its description by the concept of rational. For observing the concept of rational is the thing that gives us the judgment of animality on every single individual of humans. The result is that the reasoning is by the concept of rational on each of the particulars of human, and there is no doubt that each of them is a particular of the concept of rational, so it reduces to reasoning from universal to particular. And it may also be answered that each of the two equals is considered a relative particular of the other, since each is predicated universally of the other, and this is the meaning of its being subsumed under it, and its remoteness is not hidden. And it does not apply in our saying: 'Some animal is black, and every black is such-and-such.'
Source pages p. 16

Commentary
This passage presents an objection to the previous classification. The objector argues that even if two universals are not under a common cause, they can still be related through equality, as in 'Every human is rational, every rational is animal'. This reasoning from one universal to another is agreed to be a valid syllogism, so the earlier claim that only reasoning from universal to particular is syllogism seems false. The commentator gives two answers: First, the example actually involves reasoning from the universal concept of 'rational' to particular humans, because the judgment of animality is established for each human due to the concept of rational. Thus it reduces to universal-to-particular. Second, one may argue that each of the two equals is a relative particular of the other, since each can be predicated of the other universally. However, the commentator notes that this second answer has a problem, as it does not apply when the universals are not co-extensive, e.g., 'Some animal is black, every black is such-and-such'.

Correction: The commentator shows that it still reduces to universal-to-particular reasoning because the universal concept is applied to the particulars of the other universal.

Key Takeaway
Apparent reasoning from equal universals always reduces to reasoning from a universal to its particulars.

Conditional Syllogisms Challenge Universal-Particular Classification
Critique of three-type reasoning using conditional syllogisms

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why conditional syllogisms (connected and disjunctive) do not fit the three-type classification of reasoning and how they rely on the relation of consequence (ملازمة) rather than inclusion.

Core Idea
Conditional syllogisms prove by consequence, not by inclusion—they break the universal-particular mold.

The commentator critiques the elegant but incomplete classification of reasoning into universal-to-particular, particular-to-particular, etc. He argues that conditional syllogisms work through logical consequence between propositions, not through subsumption of an individual under a universal. This forces us to reconsider what counts as a valid inference.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on conditional syllogisms
Read the Arabic excerpt where the commentator states that connected and disjunctive conditionals do not involve reasoning from universal to particular.
Notice the examples: sunrise-daytime and Zayd-in-sea.
2
Identify the key claim
The commentator says 'فالصواب أن يقال: المناسبة بين الدليل والمدلول إما' which indicates the correct relation is not universal-particular but something else.
The phrase 'المناسبة' refers to the relation between evidence and proven.
3
Understand the critique of the three types
The earlier text restricted reasoning to three types: universal-particular, particular-particular, etc. This passage rejects that by showing conditionals fall outside.
This is a key logical insight.
4
Reflect on the implication
Ask yourself: if conditionals are not universal-particular, what type are they? The answer is that they are based on 'ملازمة' (consequence).
This broadens the definition of valid inference.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهاهنا بحث آخر وهو أن القياس الاستثنائي المتصل في مثل قولك : كلما كانت الشمس طالعة كان النهار موجوداً، لكنها طالعة أو لكن النهار ليس بموجود لم يستدل فيه بالكلي على الجزئي أصلا، وكذا الحال في الاستثنائي المنفصل في مثل قولنا : إما أن يكون زيد في البحر وإما أن لا يغرق لكنه ليس في البحر فلا يغرق، أو لكنه غرق فيكون في البحر، فالصواب أن يقال : المناسبة بين الدليل والمدلول إما قوله: (وهو معنى اندراجه فيه يعني أنهم عرفوا الجزئي الإضافي بالمندرج تحت آخر، وأرادوا بالاندراج فيه أن يكون محمولاً عليه كلياً، سواء كان له فرد آخر أولا فيشمل المساوي أيضاً . قوله : ( ولا يخفى بعده لأن الظاهر من الاندراج أن يكون أخص منه . قوله : ( وعدم جريانه إلخ يعني أنه لو أورد النقض على حصر الاستدلال في الأنواع الثلاثة، وحصر القياس في الاستدلال بالكلي على الجزئي بهذه الصورة لا يجرى الجواب المذكور بقوله : وقد يجاب إذ ليس فيه الاستدلال بالكلي على الجزئي، ولا بالجزئي على الجزئي ولا بالجزئي على الكلي ولا بأحد المتساويين على الآخر، فيكون خارجاً عن الأنواع الثلاثة وعن القياس مع كونه منه . قوله: (وهاهنا بحث آخر إلخ ) يرد على حصر الاستدلال في الأنواع الثلاثة أنه قد وهو يستدل بالملازمة بين الشيئين لا باشتماله . قوله: (فالصواب إلخ) إنما قال ذلك : لأن القول بأن إنتاج الاستثنائي لاشتماله على الشكل الأول على ما قالوا : تحكم لأن إنتاج كل منهما بديهي والاستلزام من الجانبين فلا أنه قياس واعترض عليه بأن التحقيق المذكور يؤدي إلى أن يكون الاستدلال في الاستقراء بالجزئي على الجزئي، لا الاستدلال من أحوال كل زوج وكل فرد على كل واحد من جزئيات العدد، والجواب أن في قولنا: إن كل عدد يعده الواحد اعتبارين أحدهما اعتبار مفهوم العدد نظراً إلى ذاته، ووجوده في ضمن جميع أفراده وثانيهما اعتبار أفراده فالأول هو الملحوظ في الاستقراء، مقدماته لأن الاعتبار الثاني حاصل ،قبل والثاني في القياس لأن المقصود الأصلي في هو الثبوت والكلية.
And here is another investigation: The connected conditional syllogism, like your saying: 'Whenever the sun rises, daytime exists, but it is rising, or daytime does not exist' – here reasoning is not from universal to particular at all. Similarly for the disjunctive conditional syllogism, like our saying: 'Either Zayd is in the sea or he does not drown, but he is not in the sea, so he does not drown, or he drowns, so he is in the sea.' The correct is to say: The relation between evidence and proven is either... His saying: 'And this is the meaning of its being subsumed in it' means that they defined the relative particular as that which is subsumed under another, and they meant by subsumption that it is predicated universally of it, whether or not it has another individual, so it includes the equal as well. His saying: 'And its remoteness is not hidden' because the apparent meaning of subsumption is that it is more specific than it. His saying: 'And it does not apply, etc.' means that if one objects to the restriction of reasoning to three types, and the restriction of syllogism to reasoning from universal to particular in this form, the mentioned answer by saying 'and it may be answered' does not apply because it involves neither reasoning from universal to particular, nor from particular to particular, nor from particular to universal, nor from one equal to another, so it falls outside the three types and outside syllogism while being from it. His saying: 'And here is another investigation, etc.' rejects the restriction of reasoning to three types, that it is rather reasoning by the relation of consequence between two things, not by inclusion. His saying: 'The correct is, etc.' He said that because the claim that the production of the conditional is due to its containing the first figure, as they said, is arbitrary, because the production of each is self-evident and the implication from both sides, so it is a syllogism. And it was objected that the aforementioned analysis leads to reasoning in induction being from particular to particular, not reasoning from the states of every pair and every individual to each of the particulars of number. The answer is that in our saying: 'Every number is such that one counts it', there are two considerations: one is the consideration of the concept of number with regard to its essence and its existence within all its individuals; the second is the consideration of its individuals. The first is what is observed in the premises of induction, because the second consideration is already obtained; and the second is in the syllogism, because the primary goal is affirmation and universality.
Source pages p. 17

Commentary
This passage introduces a further investigation concerning conditional syllogisms. The commentator points out that connected conditionals (e.g., 'If the sun rises, then daytime exists') do not involve reasoning from universal to particular; they rely on a relation of consequence. Similarly, disjunctive conditionals. Thus the earlier classification of reasoning into three types (universal to particular, etc.) is incomplete. The commentator criticizes those who try to reduce conditionals to the first figure, arguing that their productivity is self-evident and independent. Then the discussion turns to induction, where an objection is raised: the analysis would make induction reasoning from particular to particular, not from states of every individual to each particular. The answer distinguishes two considerations in a universal statement: the concept itself (which is the basis for induction) and the individuals (which are the basis for syllogistic reasoning). In induction, the premises consider the concept as existing in all individuals, while in syllogism, the focus is on the individuals.

Key Terms
قياس استثنائي متصل
قياس استثنائي منفصل
ملازمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All valid reasoning can be classified as universal-to-particular, particular-to-particular, etc.
Correction: Conditional syllogisms show that reasoning by logical consequence is a distinct type not fitting those categories.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the key feature of conditional syllogisms that challenges the three-type classification?
A) They involve universal premises.
B) They rely on the relation of consequence (ملازمة) between propositions.
C) They are always particular-to-particular.
D) They are invalid inferences.

Key Takeaway
Conditional syllogisms demonstrate that valid inference can be based on logical consequence rather than subsumption, expanding the scope of reasoning beyond the universal-particular dichotomy.

?

Are you willing to embrace a form of certainty that comes from logical consequence rather than categorical inclusion? Write a short commitment.
?

Induction: Reasoning from Concept, Not Individuals
Defense of induction against the charge of particular-to-particular reasoning

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how induction avoids reasoning from particular to particular by considering the concept of number as existing in all individuals.

Core Idea
Induction reasons from a universal concept, not from each particular; its premises consider the concept in all instances.

The passage also addresses induction, responding to an objection that induction would be reasoning from particular to particular. The answer distinguishes between considering the concept of number in itself (which is universal) and its individuals. This clarifies the logical basis of induction.

Guided Path
1
Read the induction objection and answer
Locate the part starting from 'واعترض عليه بأن التحقيق المذكور يؤدي...' which presents the objection about induction.
The objection claims induction becomes particular-to-particular.
2
Understand the two considerations
The answer distinguishes two ways of looking at 'every number is such that one counts it': the concept of number (essence) and its individuals.
Induction uses the first consideration, syllogism the second.
3
Grasp the solution
Thus induction reasons from the universal concept (existing in all individuals) to the universal conclusion, not from individual to individual.
This preserves the universality of inductive conclusions.
4
Reflect on the broader lesson
This shows that careful logical analysis can resolve apparent violations of the reasoning categories.
It also highlights the importance of distinguishing sense and reference.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهاهنا بحث آخر وهو أن القياس الاستثنائي المتصل في مثل قولك : كلما كانت الشمس طالعة كان النهار موجوداً، لكنها طالعة أو لكن النهار ليس بموجود لم يستدل فيه بالكلي على الجزئي أصلا، وكذا الحال في الاستثنائي المنفصل في مثل قولنا : إما أن يكون زيد في البحر وإما أن لا يغرق لكنه ليس في البحر فلا يغرق، أو لكنه غرق فيكون في البحر، فالصواب أن يقال : المناسبة بين الدليل والمدلول إما قوله: (وهو معنى اندراجه فيه يعني أنهم عرفوا الجزئي الإضافي بالمندرج تحت آخر، وأرادوا بالاندراج فيه أن يكون محمولاً عليه كلياً، سواء كان له فرد آخر أولا فيشمل المساوي أيضاً . قوله : ( ولا يخفى بعده لأن الظاهر من الاندراج أن يكون أخص منه . قوله : ( وعدم جريانه إلخ يعني أنه لو أورد النقض على حصر الاستدلال في الأنواع الثلاثة، وحصر القياس في الاستدلال بالكلي على الجزئي بهذه الصورة لا يجرى الجواب المذكور بقوله : وقد يجاب إذ ليس فيه الاستدلال بالكلي على الجزئي، ولا بالجزئي على الجزئي ولا بالجزئي على الكلي ولا بأحد المتساويين على الآخر، فيكون خارجاً عن الأنواع الثلاثة وعن القياس مع كونه منه . قوله: (وهاهنا بحث آخر إلخ ) يرد على حصر الاستدلال في الأنواع الثلاثة أنه قد وهو يستدل بالملازمة بين الشيئين لا باشتماله . قوله: (فالصواب إلخ) إنما قال ذلك : لأن القول بأن إنتاج الاستثنائي لاشتماله على الشكل الأول على ما قالوا : تحكم لأن إنتاج كل منهما بديهي والاستلزام من الجانبين فلا أنه قياس واعترض عليه بأن التحقيق المذكور يؤدي إلى أن يكون الاستدلال في الاستقراء بالجزئي على الجزئي، لا الاستدلال من أحوال كل زوج وكل فرد على كل واحد من جزئيات العدد، والجواب أن في قولنا: إن كل عدد يعده الواحد اعتبارين أحدهما اعتبار مفهوم العدد نظراً إلى ذاته، ووجوده في ضمن جميع أفراده وثانيهما اعتبار أفراده فالأول هو الملحوظ في الاستقراء، مقدماته لأن الاعتبار الثاني حاصل ،قبل والثاني في القياس لأن المقصود الأصلي في هو الثبوت والكلية.
And here is another investigation: The connected conditional syllogism, like your saying: 'Whenever the sun rises, daytime exists, but it is rising, or daytime does not exist' – here reasoning is not from universal to particular at all. Similarly for the disjunctive conditional syllogism, like our saying: 'Either Zayd is in the sea or he does not drown, but he is not in the sea, so he does not drown, or he drowns, so he is in the sea.' The correct is to say: The relation between evidence and proven is either... His saying: 'And this is the meaning of its being subsumed in it' means that they defined the relative particular as that which is subsumed under another, and they meant by subsumption that it is predicated universally of it, whether or not it has another individual, so it includes the equal as well. His saying: 'And its remoteness is not hidden' because the apparent meaning of subsumption is that it is more specific than it. His saying: 'And it does not apply, etc.' means that if one objects to the restriction of reasoning to three types, and the restriction of syllogism to reasoning from universal to particular in this form, the mentioned answer by saying 'and it may be answered' does not apply because it involves neither reasoning from universal to particular, nor from particular to particular, nor from particular to universal, nor from one equal to another, so it falls outside the three types and outside syllogism while being from it. His saying: 'And here is another investigation, etc.' rejects the restriction of reasoning to three types, that it is rather reasoning by the relation of consequence between two things, not by inclusion. His saying: 'The correct is, etc.' He said that because the claim that the production of the conditional is due to its containing the first figure, as they said, is arbitrary, because the production of each is self-evident and the implication from both sides, so it is a syllogism. And it was objected that the aforementioned analysis leads to reasoning in induction being from particular to particular, not reasoning from the states of every pair and every individual to each of the particulars of number. The answer is that in our saying: 'Every number is such that one counts it', there are two considerations: one is the consideration of the concept of number with regard to its essence and its existence within all its individuals; the second is the consideration of its individuals. The first is what is observed in the premises of induction, because the second consideration is already obtained; and the second is in the syllogism, because the primary goal is affirmation and universality.

Commentary
This passage introduces a further investigation concerning conditional syllogisms. The commentator points out that connected conditionals (e.g., 'If the sun rises, then daytime exists') do not involve reasoning from universal to particular; they rely on a relation of consequence. Similarly, disjunctive conditionals. Thus the earlier classification of reasoning into three types (universal to particular, etc.) is incomplete. The commentator criticizes those who try to reduce conditionals to the first figure, arguing that their productivity is self-evident and independent. Then the discussion turns to induction, where an objection is raised: the analysis would make induction reasoning from particular to particular, not from states of every individual to each particular. The answer distinguishes two considerations in a universal statement: the concept itself (which is the basis for induction) and the individuals (which are the basis for syllogistic reasoning). In induction, the premises consider the concept as existing in all individuals, while in syllogism, the focus is on the individuals.

Refined Classification: Inclusion and Implication
Al-Sharif al-Jurjani refines the classification of reasoning into inclusion and implication.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the refined classification of reasoning into inclusion (ishtimal) and implication (istilzam), and why exceptive syllogisms are based on implication rather than inclusion.

Core Idea
Reasoning relates evidence to conclusion either by inclusion or by implication — not by three fixed forms.

In this passage, al-Sharif al-Jurjani challenges the earlier classification that limited reasoning to three types. He argues that exceptive syllogisms operate through logical implication rather than inclusion, and proposes a cleaner twofold division. This refines our understanding of how arguments work in logic.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the flow: objection, refutation of old view, and presentation of the correct classification.
The passage is dense; read slowly.
2
Identify the objection
The passage starts by objecting to limiting reasoning to three types. The objection is that sometimes reasoning is by implication (istilzam) between two things, not by inclusion (ishtimal). Understand this distinction.
Inclusion means the evidence contains the conclusion; implication means the evidence implies the conclusion without containing it.
3
Examine the refutation of the old view
The commentator dismisses the claim that exceptive syllogisms work because they 'contain the first figure' as arbitrary. Instead, each exceptive is self-evident and the implication is from both sides.
This clears the ground for the new classification.
4
Understand the refined classification
The correct view is that the relation between evidence and conclusion is either by inclusion (as previously defined) or by implication without inclusion. Implication is further divided into explicit (conjunctive exceptives) and implicit (disjunctive exceptives). Conditional conjunctives revert to either.
Focus on the two main categories and their subcategories.
5
Reflect on 'conditional conjunctives'
The passage notes that conditional conjunctives (shartiyyat iqtiraniyya) revert to either inclusion or implication. This means they are not a separate third category but can be analyzed into the two fundamental relations.
The phrase 'so reflect' (fata'ammal) encourages deep thought.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (وهاهنا بحث آخر إلخ ) يرد على حصر الاستدلال في الأنواع الثلاثة أنه قد وهو يستدل بالملازمة بين الشيئين لا باشتماله . قوله: (فالصواب إلخ) إنما قال ذلك : لأن القول بأن إنتاج الاستثنائي لاشتماله على الشكل الأول على ما قالوا : تحكم لأن إنتاج كل منهما بديهي والاستلزام من الجانبين فلا . ثم قال: فالصواب أن يقال : المناسبة بين الدليل والمدلول إما بالاشتمال كما ذكروا وإما بالاستلزام الذي لا اشتمال معه، فإما صريحاً كما في الاستثنائيات المتصلة، وإما غير صريح كما في الاستثنائيات المنفصلة، وأما الاقترانيات الشرطية فراجعة إما إلى الاستلزام أو الاشتمال فتأمل .
His statement (may there be another discussion, etc.) objects to limiting reasoning to three types: indeed, sometimes one reasons by implication between two things, not by inclusion. His statement (the correct view, etc.) he said that because the claim that the exceptive syllogism yields its conclusion due to its containing the first form, as they said, is arbitrary, because the productivity of each is self-evident and the implication is from both sides. Then he said: the correct view is to say: the relation between the evidence and what is evidenced is either by inclusion as they mentioned, or by implication without inclusion, either explicitly as in conjunctive exceptives, or implicitly as in disjunctive exceptives; and conditional conjunctives revert either to implication or to inclusion, so reflect.
Source pages p. 17

Commentary
This passage presents an objection to the earlier classification of reasoning into three types (universal-particular, etc.) and provides a refined classification. The objection is that the exceptive syllogism (qiyas istithna'i) does not involve reasoning from universal to particular; rather, it uses implication (istilzam) between two things, not inclusion (ishtimal). The commentator notes that the claim that the exceptive syllogism works because it contains the first figure (shakl al-awwal) is arbitrary, as each exceptive is self-evident and the implication is from both sides. The correct view, therefore, is to say that the relationship between evidence and conclusion is either by inclusion (as previously described) or by implication without inclusion. Implication can be explicit, as in conjunctive exceptive syllogisms (e.g., 'Whenever the sun rises, day exists; but it is rising, so day exists'), or implicit, as in disjunctive exceptive syllogisms (e.g., 'Either Zayd is in the sea or he does not drown; but he is not in the sea, so he does not drown'). Conditional conjunctives (shartiyyat iqtiraniyya) revert to either implication or inclusion. The passage concludes with 'so reflect' (fata'ammal), urging careful thought.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the exceptive syllogism still involves inclusion (i.e., the evidence contains the conclusion), but the author stresses it is pure implication.
Correction: The exceptive syllogism works by logical implication between the premises, not by the conclusion being contained within the evidence.

Checkpoint

B) Inclusion and implication
C) Essential and accidental
D) Certain and probable

Key Takeaway
Reasoning is fundamentally about finding the appropriate relation between evidence and conclusion, which is either inclusion or implication.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Al-Jurjani calls the claim that exceptive syllogisms work by containing the first form 'arbitrary' (تحكم). Is there any belief you hold that you have not questioned, simply because it fits a familiar pattern? How would you test whether the evidence truly supports your conclusion?
