The Method of الإلزامات: Arguments Based on Opponent's Admission
Defines ilzamat, illustrates its two forms, and critiques its certainty.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand what الإلزامات are, their two forms (qiyas al-tard and qiyas al-'aks), and why they do not yield certainty.

Core Idea
Arguments that rely on opponent's agreement can persuade but never prove.

In theological debates, sometimes you argue not from your own premises but from what your opponent already accepts. This method, called الإلزامات, is a powerful dialectical tool but has a crucial limitation. In this lesson, we explore its definition, forms, and the reason it falls short of certainty.

Guided Path
1
Understand the definition
Read the opening where الإلزامات is defined as qiyas based on the opponent's admission for a differentiating cause. Note that it relies on a common cause the opponent accepts.
The definition sets the stage for why it is a dialectical argument.
2
Identify the two forms
The passage distinguishes qiyas al-tard (co-extensive) and qiyas al-'aks (co-exclusive). Examine how each form operates in affirmation and negation.
Tard reasons from existence of malzum to existence of lazim; 'aks reasons from non-existence of lazim to non-existence of malzum.
3
Examine the examples
Look at the Ash'ari examples: for tard, the argument about God's knowledge; for 'aks, the argument about human actions. Understand how they fit the definitions.
4
Grasp the critique
The passage states that ilzamat do not yield certainty because the ruling in the original is only agreed upon, not proven. Moreover, the opponent may deny the cause in the original, so even entalment fails.
This is the key weakness: dialectical success does not guarantee truth.
5
Distinguish tard in negation from 'aks
The commentary clarifies the difference between qiyas al-tard in negation and qiyas al-'aks. Both involve negation but have different logical structures.
Tard in negation is still from malzum to lazim, whereas 'aks is from lazim to malzum.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد السادس - المقصد الخامس : ماهي الطرق القوية وثالثها ) أي ثالث الأمور التي هي أشهر الطرق في إثبات العلة المشتركة ( الإلزامات وهو القياس على ما يقول به الخصم لعلة فارقة توجد في الأصل الذي يقول به الخصم ولا توجد في الفرع الذي يقاس عليه قال الإمام الرازي وهي أي الإلزامات من أنواع القياس بالحقيقة، فتارة تكون على صورة قياس الطرد، إما في الإثبات كقول الأشعرية الله عالم بالعلم لأنه مريد بالإرادة اتفاقاً، وأما النفي كقولهم النظر لا يولد العلم لأن تذكره لا يولده وأخرى تكون على صورة قياس العكس كقول الأشعرية في خلق الأعمال لو كان العبد قادراً على الإيجاد لكان قادراً على الإعادة كالباري تعالى ولما لم يكن قادراً على الإعادة اتفاقاً لم يكن قادراً على الإيجاد أيضاً (وهو) أي هذا النوع من الاستدلال القياس المسمى بالإلزامات (لا يفيد اليقين لأن حكم الأصل غير متیقن به بل هو متفق عليه فيما بين المتخاصمين ) ولا ) يفيد ( الإلزام ) أيضاً ) لأن الخصم بين منع ) وجود (علة الأصل ) قوله : وهو القياس إلخ) أي قياس الفرع على أصل يقول الخصم به أي يحكم لعلة بينهما، فقوله : لعلة متعلق بيقول ومتعلق القياس محذوف أي لعلة مشتركة بينهما في زعم القائس . قوله: (قياس الطرد أي طرد حكم الأصل في الفرع، سواء كان ذلك الحكم ثبوتياً، فيكون الطرد في الإثبات أو عدمياً فيكون الطرد في النفي، وحاصله الاستدلال بتحقق الملزوم على تحقق اللازم كأنه قيل في مثال التذكر لو كان التذكر لا يولد العلم كان النظر لا يولده، والمقدم حق فكذا التالي، وقياس العكس إجراء انتفاء الحكم في الأصل في الفرع، فهو استدلال بانتفاء اللازم على انتفاء الملزوم ففي قياس الإيجاد في عدم المقدورية على الإعادة مثلاً يرجع إلى قولنا، لو كان العبد قادراً على الإيجاد كان قادراً على الإعادة لكنه ليس قادراً عليها بالاتفاق، فلا يكون قادراً على الإيجاد أيضاً، فظهر الفرق بين قياس الطرد في النفي وبين قياس العكس . قوله : ( لأنه مريد بالإرادة (اتفاقاً أي بيننا وبين من يخاصمه كبعض المعتزلة، فلا يقدح هذا الاتفاق ذهاب النجار في أحد قوليه إلى أنه تعالى مريد بالذات .
The sixth observatory - the fifth aim: What are the strong methods? And the third of them—that is, the third of the matters that are the most famous methods for establishing the common cause—is al-ilzamat (entalments), which is analogy (qiyas) based on what the opponent says, for a differentiating cause that exists in the original case which the opponent affirms and does not exist in the branch case that is measured against it. Imam al-Razi said: They—i.e., al-ilzamat—are in reality types of qiyas. Sometimes they take the form of qiyas al-tard (co-extensive analogy), either in affirmation, as when the Ash'aris say: God is knowing by knowledge because He is willing by will, by agreement; or in negation, as when they say: Reflection does not generate knowledge because recollection does not generate it. And sometimes they take the form of qiyas al-'aks (co-exclusive analogy), as when the Ash'aris say regarding the creation of actions: If the servant were able to bring into existence, he would be able to recreate, like God most high; but since he is not able to recreate by agreement, he is not able to bring into existence either. (And it)—i.e., this type of reasoning, the analogy called al-ilzamat—does not yield certainty because the ruling in the original case is not certain but only agreed upon between the disputants; nor does it yield (al-ilzam) (entalment) either, because the opponent may deny the existence of the cause in the original case. His saying: (And it is analogy, etc.) meaning analogy of the branch to an original case which the opponent affirms, i.e., he judges a cause between them. So his saying: 'for a cause' is attached to 'says', and the related term of the analogy is omitted, meaning for a common cause between them in the opinion of the one making the analogy. His saying: (qiyas al-tard) means extending the ruling of the original to the branch, whether that ruling is affirmative, so it is tard in affirmation, or negative, so it is tard in negation. Its essence is reasoning from the existence of the entailed (malzum) to the existence of the entailer (lazim), as if it were said in the recollection example: If recollection does not generate knowledge, then reflection does not generate it; the premise is true, so is the conclusion. And qiyas al-'aks is applying the negation of the ruling in the original to the branch, so it is reasoning from the non-existence of the entailer to the non-existence of the entailed. For example, in the analogy of bringing into existence in the case of inability to recreate, it reduces to our statement: If the servant were able to bring into existence, he would be able to recreate; but he is not able to recreate by agreement, so he is not able to bring into existence either. Thus, the difference between qiyas al-tard in negation and qiyas al-'aks becomes apparent. His saying: (because He is willing by will) by agreement, i.e., between us and his opponent, such as some Mu'tazilites, so this agreement is not harmed by al-Najjar's view in one of his two opinions that He, exalted, is willing by essence.
Source pages p. 33

Commentary
This passage explains the method of الإلزامات (entalments), which is one of the famous methods for establishing the common cause. It is defined as a type of analogy (qiyas) that relies on what the opponent accepts, using a differentiating cause present in the original case affirmed by the opponent but absent in the branch case. Imam al-Razi states that ilzamat are actually types of qiyas. They come in two forms: (1) قياس الطرد (co-extensive analogy), which can be affirmative (e.g., God is knowing by knowledge because He is willing by will, which the opponent agrees on) or negative (e.g., reflection does not generate knowledge because recollection does not). (2) قياس العكس (co-exclusive analogy), e.g., the Ash'ari argument about human actions: if a servant could bring into existence, he could recreate; but he cannot recreate by agreement, so he cannot bring into existence. The commentator notes that ilzamat do not provide certainty (اليقين) because the ruling in the original is not proven but only agreed upon. Moreover, they do not even provide effective entalment (الإلزام) because the opponent may deny the existence of the cause in the original case. The commentary then clarifies the meanings of qiyas al-tard and qiyas al-'aks with examples, showing that tard is reasoning from the existence of the entailed (ملزوم) to the existence of the entailer (لازم), while 'aks is reasoning from the non-existence of the entailer to the non-existence of the entailed. A brief note indicates that the example of 'willing by will' is agreed upon between Ash'aris and some Mu'tazilites, not affected by al-Najjar's opinion.

Key Terms
إلزامات
قياس الطرد
قياس العكس
علة فارقة
يقين
ملزوم
لازم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Arguments based on the opponent's admission are strong and conclusive.
Correction: They do not yield certainty because the premise is only agreed upon, not proven; the opponent may also deny the cause in the original.

Checkpoint
What is the main criticism of the method of الإلزامات?
A) It relies on analogy which is a weak form of reasoning.
B) It does not yield certainty because the ruling in the original is only agreed upon, not proven.
C) It requires the opponent to accept the entire argument.
D) It can only be used in negative cases.

Key Takeaway
Ilzamat arguments are useful for debate but lack demonstrative certainty because they depend on agreed-upon, not proven, premises.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When have you accepted a belief about your faith simply because 'everyone agrees' – without seeking proof? How does this lesson challenge you to reexamine that belief today?
?
The text says al-ilzamat lack certainty. Are there areas where you have mistaken consensus for certainty? What steps will you take this week to ground your conviction in proof rather than agreement?
?
Imagine you are defending a truth of Islam to someone who only accepts premises based on their own assumptions. This lesson warns that such arguments persuade but do not prove. How can you avoid relying on weak reasoning, even when it seems effective?

Al-Sabr: Non-Exhaustive Division and Its Weakness
Defines al-sabr and demonstrates its weak conclusiveness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand what al-sabr is, how it is used to establish a common cause, and its inherent weakness as a non-exhaustive method.

Core Idea
Al-sabr lists possibilities but never guarantees completeness.

Al-sabr (probing) is a method used in Islamic legal theory and theology to identify the common cause in analogical reasoning. The passage defines it, gives a concrete example, and critiques its conclusiveness. Understanding its non-exhaustive nature is key to evaluating arguments based on division.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation. Let the text's structure come through.
Notice the example about blackness and visibility.
2
Identify the definition and example
The passage defines al-sabr as a non-exhaustive division and gives an example listing possible causes for blackness being visible. Identify the definition and the list.
The list includes existence, accident, originated, color, blackness.
3
Understand the objection, reply, and critique
The passage presents an objection: the real cause might be outside the list. The reply dismisses it without evidence. The author notes this is weak because enumeration is incomplete.
This highlights the limitation of non-exhaustive division.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد السادس - المقصد الخامس : ماهي الطرق القوية المثبتة للعلة المشتركة السبر وهو قسمة غير منحصرة كأن يقال مثلاً : علة كون السواد مرئياً إما وجوده أو كونه عرضاً أو محدثاً أو لوناً، أو كونه سواداً والكل باطل سوى الوجود والله سبحانه موجود فيصح رؤيته فإذا قيل: قد تكون العلة المقتضية لصحة الرؤية في السواد أمراً آخر سوى هذه الأقسام قيل في الجواب لا دليل على ثبوت ذلك الأمر الآخر فينتفي وهذا رجوع إلى أول الطريقين وقد انكشف لك ضعفه. قوله : (السبر) في الصحاح سبرت الجرح أسبره إذا نظرت ما غوره.
The Sixth Observatory - The Fifth Objective: What are the strong methods that establish the common cause? Al-Sabr is a non-exhaustive division, such as saying: The cause of blackness being visible is either its existence, or its being an accident, or its being originated, or its being a color, or its being blackness. All are false except existence, and God – exalted is He – exists, so His vision is valid. If it is said: The cause necessitating the validity of vision in blackness might be something other than these divisions, it is said in reply: There is no evidence for the existence of that other thing, so it is negated. This is a return to the first of the two ways, and its weakness has become clear to you. His saying: (al-sabr) in al-Sihah: I probed a wound, I probe it, when I look into its depth.
Source pages p. 33

Commentary
This passage defines and critiques the method of al-sabr (probing) as one of the ways to establish the common cause (al-illah al-mushtarakah) in analogical reasoning (qiyas). Al-sabr is a non-exhaustive division (qismah ghayr munhasirah) – meaning it lists several possibilities but does not cover them all exhaustively. The example given is the cause of blackness (sawad) being visible (mar'iyyan). The possibilities listed are: existence, accident, originated, color, or blackness itself. All are eliminated except existence, so since God exists, He should be visible. The objection is that the real cause could be something not listed. The reply is that there is no evidence for that other thing, so it is rejected. The author notes this argument is weak because it assumes the enumeration is complete. The commentary adds the linguistic origin of 'sabr' from probing a wound to inspect its depth.

Key Terms
السبر
قسمة غير منحصرة
العلة المشتركة
الرؤية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think al-sabr is an exhaustive division and thus conclusive.
Correction: The passage explicitly states it is non-exhaustive, so the argument is weak because the true cause may be omitted.

Checkpoint
What is the main weakness of the method of al-sabr according to the passage?
A) It uses false premises.
B) It is a non-exhaustive division, so the cause may be omitted.
C) It only works for colors.
D) It requires agreement between opponents.

Key Takeaway
Al-sabr is a weak method for establishing a common cause because its division is not exhaustive, leaving open the possibility that the true cause is omitted.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you argue for your beliefs to others, do you ever present a list of options that you assume is complete without checking its exhaustiveness? How might this affect the strength of your witness? Commit to examining your arguments for hidden assumptions this week.
?
The scholars admit the weakness of al-sabr. Are you quick to defend your own theological positions even when you sense a flaw in your reasoning? Can you, with humility, say 'this argument is weak' and seek a stronger one?
?
Al-sabr originally means probing a wound. Do you truly probe the depths of your own heart and thoughts, or do you avoid the discomfort of self-examination? Will you set aside time today to probe one area of your faith that you have assumed is sound?

Logic and Technical Terms

Definition of Al-Ilzamat
Defines al-ilzamat and its structure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of al-ilzamat as a method of analogical reasoning based on the opponent's premise.

Core Idea
Al-ilzamat is analogy that uses the opponent's own premise to force a conclusion.

This lesson introduces al-ilzamat, the third method for establishing the common cause in Islamic legal theory. We will examine how it differs from other methods by relying on a premise the opponent already accepts. The passage defines its core structure.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage defining al-ilzamat
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then the translation. Notice the key components: origin, branch, differentiating cause.
2
Identify the key elements
Identify the origin (asl), branch (far'), and the differentiating cause (illah fariqah). Understand that the opponent holds the origin and the cause exists there.
The differentiating cause is what makes the analogy work.
3
Understand the basis
Recognize that al-ilzamat is built on what the opponent says (ma yaqul bihi al-khasm). It is not based on an independent proof.
4
Reflect on persuasive vs. certain
Consider why this method is persuasive but not certain. The passage will later explain the lack of certainty.
This prepares you for later lessons.

وثالثها أي ثالث الأمور التي هي أشهر الطرق في إثبات العلة المشتركة الإلزامات وهو القياس على ما يقول به الخصم لعلة فارقة توجد في الأصل الذي يقول به الخصم ولا توجد في الفرع الذي يقاس عليه قال الإمام الرازي وهي أي الإلزامات من أنواع القياس بالحقيقة، فتارة تكون على صورة قياس الطرد، إما في الإثبات كقول الأشعرية الله عالم بالعلم لأنه مريد بالإرادة اتفاقاً، وأما النفي كقولهم النظر لا يولد العلم لأن تذكره لا يولده وأخرى تكون على صورة قياس العكس كقول الأشعرية في خلق الأعمال لو كان العبد قادراً على الإيجاد لكان قادراً على الإعادة كالباري تعالى ولما لم يكن قادراً على الإعادة اتفاقاً لم يكن قادراً على الإيجاد أيضاً وهو أي هذا النوع من الاستدلال القياس المسمى بالإلزامات لا يفيد اليقين لأن حكم الأصل غير متيقن به بل هو متفق عليه فيما بين المتخاصمين ولا يفيد الإلزام أيضاً لأن الخصم بين منع وجود علة الأصل في الفرع وبين منع ثبوت حكمه أي حكم الأصل لأنه إن سلم له علته فهي ليست موجودة في الفرع، وإن لم يسلم له تلك العلة منع حكم الأصل لأنه إنما قال به لأجلها، فهذا قياس مركب الأصل كما عرفته في التذكرة. قوله : ( وهو القياس إلخ) أي قياس الفرع على أصل يقول الخصم به أي يحكم لعلة بينهما، فقوله : لعلة متعلق بيقول ومتعلق القياس محذوف أي لعلة مشتركة بينهما في زعم القائس. قوله : (قياس الطرد أي طرد حكم الأصل في الفرع، سواء كان ذلك الحكم ثبوتياً، فيكون الطرد في الإثبات أو عدمياً فيكون الطرد في النفي، وحاصله الاستدلال بتحقق الملزوم على تحقق اللازم كأنه قيل في مثال التذكر لو كان التذكر لا يولد العلم كان النظر لا يولده، والمقدم حق فكذا التالي، وقياس العكس إجراء انتفاء الحكم في الأصل في الفرع، فهو استدلال بانتفاء اللازم على انتفاء الملزوم ففي قياس الإيجاد في عدم المقدورية على الإعادة مثلاً يرجع إلى قولنا، لو كان العبد قادراً على الإيجاد كان قادراً على الإعادة لكنه ليس قادراً عليها بالاتفاق، فلا يكون قادراً على الإيجاد أيضاً، فظهر الفرق بين قياس الطرد في النفي وبين قياس العكس. قوله : ( لأنه مريد بالإرادة اتفاقاً أي بيننا وبين من يخاصمه كبعض المعتزلة، فلا يقدح هذا الاتفاق ذهاب النجار في أحد قوليه إلى أنه تعالى مريد بالذات.
And the third of the things that are the most famous methods in establishing the common cause: al-ilzamat (necessary implications). It is the analogy based on what the opponent says, for a differentiating cause that exists in the origin which the opponent holds and does not exist in the branch which is compared to it. Imam al-Razi said: al-ilzamat are types of real analogy. Sometimes they take the form of qiyas al-tard (analogy of coextensiveness), either in affirmation, as the Ash'aris say: God is knowing through knowledge because He is willing through will, by agreement; or in negation, as they say: reflection does not generate knowledge because its recollection does not generate it. And sometimes they take the form of qiyas al-aks (analogy of converse), as the Ash'aris say concerning the creation of actions: if the servant were capable of bringing into existence, he would be capable of re-creation like the Exalted God, and since he is not capable of re-creation by agreement, he is not capable of bringing into existence either. And this type of reasoning, the analogy called al-ilzamat, does not yield certainty because the ruling of the origin is not certain but is agreed upon between the disputants, nor does it yield necessary implication because the opponent can either deny the existence of the cause of the origin in the branch, or deny the establishment of its ruling, i.e., the ruling of the origin, because if he concedes the cause, it does not exist in the branch, and if he does not concede that cause, he denies the ruling of the origin because he only held it on account of it. So this is a qiyas murakkab al-asl as you have learned in al-Tadhkira. His saying: (and it is the analogy etc.) meaning analogy of the branch to an origin that the opponent holds, i.e., he rules for a cause between them. So his saying: for a cause is attached to 'he says' and the object of the analogy is omitted, i.e., for a common cause between them in the claimant's estimation. His saying: (qiyas al-tard) meaning extending the ruling of the origin to the branch, whether that ruling is positive, so the extension is in affirmation, or negative, so the extension is in negation. Its summary is inferring the existence of the necessary from the existence of the necessary condition, as if it were said in the example of recollection: if recollection does not generate knowledge, then reflection does not generate it, and the premise is true, so is the consequent. And qiyas al-aks is applying the negation of the ruling in the origin to the branch, so it is inferring the negation of the necessary condition from the negation of the necessary. So in the analogy of bringing into existence in the incapability for re-creation, for example, it leads to our saying: if the servant were capable of bringing into existence, he would be capable of re-creation, but he is not capable of it by agreement, so he is not capable of bringing into existence either. Thus the difference between qiyas al-tard in negation and qiyas al-aks becomes clear. His saying: (because He is willing through will, by agreement) meaning between us and the one he disputes, such as some Mu'tazila, so this agreement is not harmed by the view of al-Najjar in one of his two sayings that the Exalted is willing by essence.
Source pages p. 33

Commentary
This passage defines al-ilzamat as the third method for establishing the common cause, explains its two types, and argues it does not yield certainty. The definition: al-ilzamat is a qiyas (analogy) based on a premise that the opponent holds, using a differentiating cause (illah fariqah) that exists in the opponent's origin but not in the branch. Imam al-Razi is quoted saying al-ilzamat are types of real qiyas. Two forms: (1) qiyas al-tard (analogy of coextensiveness), which extends the ruling from origin to branch, either in affirmation (e.g., God is knowing through knowledge because He is willing through will) or in negation (e.g., reflection does not generate knowledge because recollection does not). (2) qiyas al-aks (analogy of converse), which applies the negation of the ruling, e.g., if the servant cannot recreate, he cannot create. The author then says this method does not yield certainty because the ruling of the origin is not certain but merely agreed upon. Moreover, it does not compel the opponent, because the opponent can either deny the cause of the origin in the branch, or if he concedes the cause, deny its existence in the branch, or deny the ruling of the origin altogether since it was based on that cause. Thus it is a qiyas murakkab al-asl. The commentary explains the terms: qiyas al-tard and qiyas al-aks, providing examples and clarifying the difference. For instance, in qiyas al-aks, the inference is from the negation of the necessary (the ruling) to the negation of the necessary condition (the cause). The commentary also addresses the example about God's will, noting that the agreement is with some Mu'tazila, and that al-Najjar's view that God wills by essence does not harm the argument.

Key Terms
الإلزامات
القياس
العلة الفارقة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the basis of al-ilzamat according to the passage?
A) The opponent's axiom
B) A differentiating cause accepted by the opponent
C) The common cause between origin and branch
D) The authority of the scholar

Key Takeaway
Al-ilzamat is an argument that uses the opponent's own premise to prove a conclusion, relying on a cause present in their origin but absent in the branch.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you are in a disagreement about religious matters, do you usually argue from your own assumptions or do you try to find common ground with the other person's premises? How might the method of al-ilzamat change your approach in your next discussion? Will you commit to starting from the other person's accepted points rather than your own?
?
The differentiating cause (illah fariqah) is a critical element in al-ilzamat—it must only exist in the opponent's position. Are you willing to honestly examine your own beliefs to see if you are using inconsistent premises? If you found a contradiction in your own stance, would you be ready to correct it even if it means losing an argument?
?
This passage describes a logical tool for winning debates by forcing conclusions. But is your goal in seeking knowledge to win arguments or to arrive at truth? How can you ensure that when you use tools like al-ilzamat, it is for the sake of truth and not ego? Pause and ask yourself: what is the intention behind my next theological discussion?

Logic and Technical Terms

Types of Al-Ilzamat: Qiyas al-Tard and Qiyas al-Aks
Classifies al-ilzamat into qiyas al-tard and qiyas al-aks with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between qiyas al-tard (coextensive) and qiyas al-aks (converse) as forms of al-ilzamat.

Core Idea
Al-ilzamat appears in two forms: one that affirms a ruling and one that negates it.

Imam al-Razi classifies al-ilzamat into two types based on the direction of the analogy. The passage provides examples from Ash'ari theology to illustrate each. This lesson breaks down these two forms.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage about the two types
Read the Arabic and translation. Note that Imam al-Razi is quoted.
2
Identify qiyas al-tard
Find the example of qiyas al-tard in affirmation and negation. Understand that it extends the ruling from origin to branch.
The example in affirmation: God is knowing because He is willing.
3
Identify qiyas al-aks
Find the example of qiyas al-aks. Note how it uses the negation of the ruling.
The example: if the servant cannot recreate, he cannot create.
4
Compare the logical direction
Notice that tard extends the ruling (from presence of cause to presence of effect), while aks moves from absence of effect to absence of cause.
5
Practice identification
Try to classify additional examples as tard or aks. This reinforces understanding.
For instance, 'If it is not created, it is not contingent' would be qiyas al-aks.

And the third of the things that are the most famous methods in establishing the common cause: al-ilzamat (necessary implications). Commentary
This passage defines al-ilzamat as the third method for establishing the common cause, explains its two types, and argues it does not yield certainty. The definition: al-ilzamat is a qiyas (analogy) based on a premise that the opponent holds, using a differentiating cause (illah fariqah) that exists in the opponent's origin but not in the branch. The author then says this method does not yield certainty because the ruling of the origin is not certain but merely agreed upon. Moreover, it does not compel the opponent, because the opponent can either deny the cause of the origin in the branch, or if he concedes the cause, deny its existence in the branch, or deny the ruling of the origin altogether since it was based on that cause. Checkpoint
Which of the following is an example of qiyas al-aks?
A) God is willing by will, so He is knowing by knowledge
B) If the servant cannot recreate, he cannot create
C) Reflection does not generate knowledge because recollection does not
D) The world is created because it is contingent

Key Takeaway
Al-ilzamat has two forms: qiyas al-tard (coextensive) and qiyas al-aks (converse), each serving to either affirm or negate a ruling based on an agreed premise.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you reflect on your own beliefs, do you ever use arguments that affirm something about God based on another attribute (like knowledge from will)? Are you confident that these analogies are sound, or do you accept them on authority? How would you defend your conviction to someone who challenges the logical step from divine willing to divine knowing?
?
The Ash'ari argument about human incapacity to create actions uses qiyas al-aks: if we cannot re-create, we cannot create. Do you truly accept that you cannot create even your own actions? How does that change your sense of responsibility for your deeds? Will you examine your daily actions today to see where you attribute your choices to yourself rather than to God?
?
Al-ilzamat are necessary implications. Think of an implication in your own life: if you truly believe that God is all-knowing, what does that necessarily imply about your secret thoughts? Are you willing to let that implication change how you act when no one is watching?

Why Al-Ilzamat Does Not Yield Certainty
Critiques al-ilzamat as not yielding certainty.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two reasons al-ilzamat does not produce certainty: the uncertainty of the origin's ruling and the opponent's ability to contest the cause.

Core Idea
Al-ilzamat is persuasive but not certain because it relies on agreement, not proof.

After defining al-ilzamat and its types, the author argues that this method does not yield certain knowledge. The passage explains why: the ruling of the origin is only agreed upon, not certain, and the opponent can always deny the cause in the branch. This lesson unpacks that critique.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on lack of certainty
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the two reasons given by the author.
2
Identify the first reason
The ruling of the origin is not certain but agreed upon. That means it is not absolute proof.
This is the primary reason: hukm al-asl ghayr mutayaqqan.
3
Identify the second reason
The opponent can either deny the cause in the branch (if he accepts the cause in origin) or deny the ruling of the origin (if he does not accept the cause).
This is a double defense: the opponent can always escape the implication.
4
Understand the term 'qiyas murakkab al-asl'
This type of analogy is called 'compound analogy of the origin' because the origin is not firmly established. The author refers to previous knowledge.
This terminology is important for advanced studies.
5
Discuss implications
Consider why this critique matters: it shows that al-ilzamat is weak for establishing truth but useful for dialectics.

وثالثها أي ثالث الأمور التي هي أشهر الطرق في إثبات العلة المشتركة الإلزامات وهو القياس على ما يقول به الخصم لعلة فارقة توجد في الأصل الذي يقول به الخصم ولا توجد في الفرع الذي يقاس عليه قال الإمام الرازي وهي أي الإلزامات من أنواع القياس بالحقيقة، فتارة تكون على صورة قياس الطرد، إما في الإثبات كقول الأشعرية الله عالم بالعلم لأنه مريد بالإرادة اتفاقاً، وأما النفي كقولهم النظر لا يولد العلم لأن تذكره لا يولده وأخرى تكون على صورة قياس العكس كقول الأشعرية في خلق الأعمال لو كان العبد قادراً على الإيجاد لكان قادراً على الإعادة كالباري تعالى ولما لم يكن قادراً على الإعادة اتفاقاً لم يكن قادراً على الإيجاد أيضاً وهو أي هذا النوع من الاستدلال القياس المسمى بالإلزامات لا يفيد اليقين لأن حكم الأصل غير متيقن به بل هو متفق عليه فيما بين المتخاصمين ولا يفيد الإلزام أيضاً لأن الخصم بين منع وجود علة الأصل في الفرع وبين منع ثبوت حكمه أي حكم الأصل لأنه إن سلم له علته فهي ليست موجودة في الفرع، وإن لم يسلم له تلك العلة منع حكم الأصل لأنه إنما قال به لأجلها، فهذا قياس مركب الأصل كما عرفته في التذكرة. قوله : ( وهو القياس إلخ) أي قياس الفرع على أصل يقول الخصم به أي يحكم لعلة بينهما، فقوله : لعلة متعلق بيقول ومتعلق القياس محذوف أي لعلة مشتركة بينهما في زعم القائس. قوله : (قياس الطرد أي طرد حكم الأصل في الفرع، سواء كان ذلك الحكم ثبوتياً، فيكون الطرد في الإثبات أو عدمياً فيكون الطرد في النفي، وحاصله الاستدلال بتحقق الملزوم على تحقق اللازم كأنه قيل في مثال التذكر لو كان التذكر لا يولد العلم كان النظر لا يولده، والمقدم حق فكذا التالي، وقياس العكس إجراء انتفاء الحكم في الأصل في الفرع، فهو استدلال بانتفاء اللازم على انتفاء الملزوم ففي قياس الإيجاد في عدم المقدورية على الإعادة مثلاً يرجع إلى قولنا، لو كان العبد قادراً على الإيجاد كان قادراً على الإعادة لكنه ليس قادراً عليها بالاتفاق، فلا يكون قادراً على الإيجاد أيضاً، فظهر الفرق بين قياس الطرد في النفي وبين قياس العكس. قوله : ( لأنه مريد بالإرادة اتفاقاً أي بيننا وبين من يخاصمه كبعض المعتزلة، فلا يقدح هذا الاتفاق ذهاب النجار في أحد قوليه إلى أنه تعالى مريد بالذات.
And the third of the things that are the most famous methods in establishing the common cause: al-ilzamat (necessary implications). It is the analogy based on what the opponent says, for a differentiating cause that exists in the origin which the opponent holds and does not exist in the branch which is compared to it. Imam al-Razi said: al-ilzamat are types of real analogy. Sometimes they take the form of qiyas al-tard (analogy of coextensiveness), either in affirmation, as the Ash'aris say: God is knowing through knowledge because He is willing through will, by agreement; or in negation, as they say: reflection does not generate knowledge because its recollection does not generate it. And sometimes they take the form of qiyas al-aks (analogy of converse), as the Ash'aris say concerning the creation of actions: if the servant were capable of bringing into existence, he would be capable of re-creation like the Exalted God, and since he is not capable of re-creation by agreement, he is not capable of bringing into existence either. And this type of reasoning, the analogy called al-ilzamat, does not yield certainty because the ruling of the origin is not certain but is agreed upon between the disputants, nor does it yield necessary implication because the opponent can either deny the existence of the cause of the origin in the branch, or deny the establishment of its ruling, i.e., the ruling of the origin, because if he concedes the cause, it does not exist in the branch, and if he does not concede that cause, he denies the ruling of the origin because he only held it on account of it. So this is a qiyas murakkab al-asl as you have learned in al-Tadhkira. His saying: (and it is the analogy etc.) meaning analogy of the branch to an origin that the opponent holds, i.e., he rules for a cause between them. So his saying: for a cause is attached to 'he says' and the object of the analogy is omitted, i.e., for a common cause between them in the claimant's estimation. His saying: (qiyas al-tard) meaning extending the ruling of the origin to the branch, whether that ruling is positive, so the extension is in affirmation, or negative, so the extension is in negation. Its summary is inferring the existence of the necessary from the existence of the necessary condition, as if it were said in the example of recollection: if recollection does not generate knowledge, then reflection does not generate it, and the premise is true, so is the consequent. And qiyas al-aks is applying the negation of the ruling in the origin to the branch, so it is inferring the negation of the necessary condition from the negation of the necessary. So in the analogy of bringing into existence in the incapability for re-creation, for example, it leads to our saying: if the servant were capable of bringing into existence, he would be capable of re-creation, but he is not capable of it by agreement, so he is not capable of bringing into existence either. Thus the difference between qiyas al-tard in negation and qiyas al-aks becomes clear. His saying: (because He is willing through will, by agreement) meaning between us and the one he disputes, such as some Mu'tazila, so this agreement is not harmed by the view of al-Najjar in one of his two sayings that the Exalted is willing by essence.
Source pages p. 33

Commentary
This passage defines al-ilzamat as the third method for establishing the common cause, explains its two types, and argues it does not yield certainty. The definition: al-ilzamat is a qiyas (analogy) based on a premise that the opponent holds, using a differentiating cause (illah fariqah) that exists in the opponent's origin but not in the branch. Imam al-Razi is quoted saying al-ilzamat are types of real qiyas. Two forms: (1) qiyas al-tard (analogy of coextensiveness), which extends the ruling from origin to branch, either in affirmation (e.g., God is knowing through knowledge because He is willing through will) or in negation (e.g., reflection does not generate knowledge because recollection does not). (2) qiyas al-aks (analogy of converse), which applies the negation of the ruling, e.g., if the servant cannot recreate, he cannot create. The author then says this method does not yield certainty because the ruling of the origin is not certain but merely agreed upon. Moreover, it does not compel the opponent, because the opponent can either deny the cause of the origin in the branch, or if he concedes the cause, deny its existence in the branch, or deny the ruling of the origin altogether since it was based on that cause. Thus it is a qiyas murakkab al-asl. The commentary explains the terms: qiyas al-tard and qiyas al-aks, providing examples and clarifying the difference. For instance, in qiyas al-aks, the inference is from the negation of the necessary (the ruling) to the negation of the necessary condition (the cause). The commentary also addresses the example about God's will, noting that the agreement is with some Mu'tazila, and that al-Najjar's view that God wills by essence does not harm the argument.

Key Terms
اليقين
متفق عليه
علة الأصل
قياس مركب الأصل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Al-ilzamat yields certain knowledge because it uses the opponent's own premise.
Correction: The passage explains that the opponent's premise is only agreed upon, not certain, and the opponent can still avoid the conclusion by denying the cause in the branch.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the main reason al-ilzamat does not yield certainty?
A) The opponent can deny the conclusion
B) The ruling of the origin is merely agreed upon, not certain
C) The cause is always different
D) It is based on a fallacious assumption

Key Takeaway
Al-ilzamat is not a certain proof because the origin's ruling is only agreed upon, and the opponent can always contest the presence of the cause in the branch.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a belief you hold about your faith that you accepted because it was commonly agreed upon by people around you, not because you verified it from the Quran and Sunnah. How does this lesson challenge you to seek certainty rather than consensus?
?
When you engage in discussions with others, do you sometimes rely on arguments that sound persuasive but lack solid foundations? What would it look like for you to commit to only accepting truths that are certain (yaqeen) from divine revelation, even if it means admitting you don't know?

The Argument for an Infinite Number from Division
Al-Jurjani presents a disjunctive argument for an infinite number.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Al-Jurjani uses a disjunctive argument to establish the necessity of an infinite number based on the impossibility of preference among homogenous numbers.

Core Idea
If numbers are equally possible, the existence of any number forces an infinite chain.

Al-Jurjani presents a concise logical argument that if numbers are homogenous in possibility, then the actual existence of any number implies that an infinite number must exist. This argument serves as a reductio against opponents who deny the possibility of infinite series. In this lesson, we unpack the reasoning and the crucial grammatical distinction between division (tanwi') and hesitation (tardid) in the disjunction.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the structure: a disjunction followed by a grammatical clarification.
The word 'or' is key – it indicates division, not hesitation.
2
Identify the two options
The disjunction presents two possibilities: no number exists, or an infinite number exists. The first is rejected as false.
3
Understand the premise
The argument relies on the impossibility of preferring one number over another without a cause. If numbers are homogenous, any number could be actualized, leading to an infinite chain.
This is a kalam principle: preference without a cause is impossible.
4
Analyze the commentator's note
The commentator clarifies that the word 'or' (أو) here means division (تنويع), not hesitation (ترديد). This emphasizes that the two options are mutually exclusive and exhaustive.
This grammatical point strengthens the logical force of the disjunction.
5
Reflect on the application
Ask: How does this argument challenge those who deny infinite chains? The argument shows that if any finite number is actual, an infinite must be actual.
This connects to broader debates about the age of the world and causal chains.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكذا إذا أرادوا إثبات عدد غير متناه، قالوا : إما أن لا يثبت عدد أصلا وهو باطل أو يثبت عدد غير متناه لامتناع ترجيح عدد على عدد
قوله : (أو يثبت إلخ عطف على قوله : فينتفي العدد وقدر الشارح لكل واحد من المعطوفين شرطاً إشارة إلى أن كلمة أو للتنويع لا للترديد .
And likewise, if they wanted to establish an infinite number, they said: 'Either no number is established at all, which is false, or an infinite number is established, due to the impossibility of preferring one number over another.' His statement: 'or an infinite number is established, etc.' is connected to his statement: 'so the number is negated,' and the commentator estimated for each of the two conjuncts a condition, indicating that the word 'or' is for division (al-tanwi') not for hesitation (al-tardid).
Source pages p. 45

Commentary
This passage presents an argument used by those who aim to prove an infinite number. They offer a disjunction: either no number is established, or an infinite number is established. The first option is false because clearly numbers do exist (e.g., the number of gods if multiple, or the number of created objects). Therefore, an infinite number must be established. The reasoning relies on the impossibility of preferring one finite number over another without a cause; if one number is actualized, then by the same logic, any number could be actualized, leading to an infinite chain. The commentator clarifies that the word 'or' (أو) here is for division (tanwi') meaning the two options are mutually exclusive and exhaustive, not for hesitation. He also notes that the commentator (possibly al-Jurjani himself) adds a condition for each conjunct to make the argument precise: the first is 'if no number is established' and the second is 'if a number is established, it must be infinite'.

تنويع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument proves that an actual infinite number exists in the physical world.
Correction: The argument is a logical reductio used to challenge opponents who assume numbers are homogenous; it does not necessarily assert an actual infinite in the world, but rather shows the consequence of their assumptions.

Checkpoint
What is the purpose of the disjunction 'either no number or an infinite number' in this argument?
A) To prove that numbers are impossible.
B) To show that if any number exists, an infinite number must exist.
C) To argue that God can create infinite things.
D) To deny the reality of numbers.

Key Takeaway
The existence of any number implies an infinite number because all numbers are equally possible and preference without a cause is impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the impossibility of preferring one number over another forces an infinite number, what does that say about the preferences you make daily? Are your choices grounded in real causes or mere whim? How can you ensure your decisions are based on reason and righteousness?
?
The existence of an infinite number implies that finite numbers are not ultimate. In your life, what finite things do you treat as if they were infinite? Are you willing to let go of them in light of the infinite reality?
?
The argument shows that without a cause, no preference is possible. Think of a time you acted without clear reason. How did that affect your spiritual state? Commit today to aligning your actions with divine wisdom, not arbitrary desire.

Denying Priority Among Numbers in Reality
Al-Jurjani denies the premise of no priority and rejects the mental argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the premise of no priority among numbers is denied in reality and why mental lack of priority is not proof.

Core Idea
Reality may have hidden priorities; ignorance does not equal absence.

Al-Jurjani begins by directly rejecting the foundational premise of the infinite regress argument. He argues that it is possible for some numbers to have intrinsic priority over others in reality, so the existence of one number does not force the existence of all. He then warns that simply not knowing about priority does not justify denying it.

Guided Path
1
Understand the premise being critiqued
The premise is that no number has priority over another in reality. Al-Jurjani denies this by saying it is 'mamnu' (forbidden/denied).
The premise is used to argue that if one number exists, all must exist, leading to an infinite regress.
2
See why priority is possible in reality
He gives an example: the second number could exist while the third is impossible. This breaks the necessity of infinite chain.
He does not claim to prove such priority, only that it is possible.
3
Distinguish mental from real priority
Even if you don't know about priority, that doesn't mean it doesn't exist. The argument from ignorance is invalid.
He rejects the principle 'what has no proof must be negated' as false.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فنقول : ( في بيان ضعف هذه المقدمة ( عدم الأولوية ) بين عدد وعدد (في نفس الأمر ممنوع) لجواز أن يكون لبعض الأعداد رجحان وأولوية على بعض في نفس الأمر، فجاز أن يكون الثاني مثلا حاصلا مع استحالة الثالث فلا يلزم من ثبوت عدد ثبوت عدد آخر ولا من انتفاء عدد انتفاء عدد آخر
( و ) عدم الأولوية ( في ذهنك لا يفيد ) إذ لا يلزم من عدم العلم بالأولوية عدمها في نفسها إلا أن يقال : ما لا دليل عليه وجب نفيه، وقد عرفت بطلانه
فإن قال : المستدل نختار الأول وهو الأولوية في نفس الأمر ونقول : حكم الشيء الذي هو عدد من الأعداد مثلا حكم مثله من سائر الأعداد فإن المثلين يتشاركان في الأحكام اللازمة، فلو صح الثاني الثالث والرابع إلى ما لا يتناهى من أمثاله، وإذا لم تصح تلك الأمثال لم يصح هو أيضا
قلنا ما ذكره إعادة للدعوى بعبارة أخرى مع أنه لزمه في صورة الاستدلال على نفي الأعداد نفي الواحد أيضا لأنه مثل الثاني والثالث، فإذا انتفيا انتفي الواحد قطعا
فإن قيل : ليس الواحد مثل العدد قلنا إن كان العدد نفس الآحاد فقط كان الواحد مثلا له و إن اعتبر مع كل عدد صورة منوعة هي مبدأ لخواصه لم تكن
قوله : ( وعالميته أمر (واجب) يحتمل أن يشير به إلى عدم النقض بعدم علمنا بما لا يتناهى مع أن هذه المقدمة مستدركة لا يحتاج إليها
قوله : ( ما ذكره إعادة إلخ فيه بحث لأن الدعوى أنه ليس عدد أولى من عدد آخر في الثبوت والانتفاء، ونفس الأمر والدليل قولنا : لأن مراتب الأعداد متماثلة وحكم الأمثال واحد 
قوله : ( فإن قيل إلخ) لا يخفى أن المذكور سابقا أن الواحد مثل الثاني والثالث فلو انتفى الثاني والثالث انتفى الواحد، لأن حكم الأمثال واحد لأن الواحد مثل الاثنين والثلاثة، فلا ورود للاعتراض
قوله : ( إن كان العدد إلخ ) الملازمة ممنوعة لأنه يلزم تماثل الكل والجزء 
قوله : ( صورة منوعة) سواء كان أمرا وجوديا أو اعتباريا 
قوله : (كان الواحد مثلا له فيه بحث لأن مجموع أنفس الآحاد كم منفصل فله حقيقة غير حقيقة الوحدة لأنها ليست من قبيل الكم.
So we say: In explaining the weakness of this premise: the lack of priority between one number and another is denied in reality, because it is possible that some numbers have preference and priority over others in reality. Thus it is possible that the second exists while the third is impossible; so the existence of one number does not entail the existence of another, nor does the negation of one entail the negation of another. And the lack of priority in your mind is not useful, because lack of knowledge of priority does not entail its non-existence in itself, unless one says: what has no proof must be negated, and you have known the falsity of that. If the proponent says: we choose the first—priority in reality, and we say: the ruling of a thing which is a number among numbers, for example, is the ruling of its like from the rest of the numbers, because similars share in necessary rulings. So if the second is valid, then the third, fourth, and so on to infinity of its likes; and if those likes are not valid, then it is not valid either. We say: what he mentioned is just a restatement of the claim in other words. Moreover, it entails, in the case of the argument for the negation of numbers, the negation of the one as well, because it is similar to the second and third. So if they are negated, the one is negated absolutely. If it is said: the one is not like the number; we say: if the number is nothing but the ones themselves, then the one is like it; and if with each number a diversified form is considered which is the principle of its properties, then not. His statement: 'and His omniscience is a necessary thing'—he possibly means by that to prevent the objection from our not knowing what is infinite, even though this premise is redundant and not needed. His statement: 'what he mentioned is a restatement etc.'—there is discussion in it, because the claim is that no number has priority over another in existence and non-existence, and in reality the proof is our saying: because the ranks of numbers are similar and the ruling of similars is one. His statement: 'if it is said etc.'—it is not hidden that what was mentioned earlier is that the one is like the second and third; so if the second and third are negated, the one is negated, because the ruling of similars is one, because the one is like two and three; so the objection does not arise. His statement: 'if the number is etc.'—the implication is denied because it would entail the similarity of the whole and the part. His statement: 'a diversified form'—whether it is an existential or an intentional matter. His statement: 'the one would be like it'—there is discussion in it, because the totality of the ones themselves is a discrete quantity, so it has a reality other than the reality of oneness, because it is not of the category of quantity.
Source pages p. 45

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the principle that no number has priority over another, which is used in the earlier arguments. The author begins by denying that this principle holds in reality; it is possible that some numbers have intrinsic priority (e.g., two might be possible while three is not). Therefore, the existence of one number does not necessitate the existence of another, breaking the infinite regress. He then addresses a possible response: the opponent might claim that since there is no proof of priority, we must assume none exists; but this is rejected because absence of proof does not entail absence in reality. The opponent then restates the argument in terms of similars: numbers are similar, so the ruling for one applies to all. The author points out this is just a restatement and, moreover, it leads to the absurd conclusion that the number one itself must be negated, because it is similar to two and three. If two and three are impossible, then one is impossible too. The opponent objects that the number one is not like the number (plural), but the author says if a number is just a collection of ones, then one is similar; if each number has a unique property, then they are not similar. The commentary then adds further refinements: the premise may be redundant, the argument about similars is debated, and the objection about one being similar to two is valid. The final part discusses the nature of quantity and oneness, noting that the sum of ones is a discrete quantity, distinct from the mere concept of oneness.

ممنوع
جواز
عدم العلم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that because we cannot perceive priority among numbers, none exists in reality.
Correction: The author clarifies that ignorance of priority does not prove its absence; possibility remains.

Checkpoint
Why does Al-Jurjani deny the premise of no priority in reality?
A) He proves that numbers are unequal.
B) He shows it is possible that some numbers have priority over others.
C) He says the mind cannot conceive of priority.
D) He uses a different premise.

Key Takeaway
The premise of no priority among numbers is not necessarily true in reality, and ignorance of priority is not evidence against it.

?
This passage distinguishes between what is in your mind and what is in reality. Where in your life have you confused your ignorance with a fact? Will you now admit that possibility?
?
The author says 'lack of priority in your mind is not useful'. How often do you assume that your lack of understanding of a matter means the matter is false? What would it take for you to hold that possibility open?

The Similars Argument and Its Refutation
Al-Jurjani refutes the similars argument as circular and self-defeating.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the opponent's similars argument and Al-Jurjani's refutation that it is circular and leads to absurdity.

Core Idea
The similars argument restates the claim and implies the negation of one itself.

The opponent restates the priority argument using the principle of similars: numbers are similar, so what applies to one applies to all. Al-Jurjani points out that this is just a restatement of the original claim, and more seriously, it leads to the absurd conclusion that even the number one must be negated, since it is similar to other numbers.

Guided Path
1
Read the opponent's similars argument
The opponent says numbers are similar, so the ruling for one applies to all. If the second is valid, then all numbers to infinity are valid; if not, none are.
This is an attempt to prove that if one number exists, all must, or if one is impossible, all are.
2
Identify Al-Jurjani's first response
He says this is just a restatement of the original claim in different words, not a new proof.
The argument doesn't provide new evidence.
3
Understand the second and stronger response
If the similars argument is used to negate numbers, it also negates the number one, because one is similar to two and three. This is absurd.
The opponent's logic backfires.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فنقول : ( في بيان ضعف هذه المقدمة ( عدم الأولوية ) بين عدد وعدد (في نفس الأمر ممنوع) لجواز أن يكون لبعض الأعداد رجحان وأولوية على بعض في نفس الأمر، فجاز أن يكون الثاني مثلا حاصلا مع استحالة الثالث فلا يلزم من ثبوت عدد ثبوت عدد آخر ولا من انتفاء عدد انتفاء عدد آخر
( و ) عدم الأولوية ( في ذهنك لا يفيد ) إذ لا يلزم من عدم العلم بالأولوية عدمها في نفسها إلا أن يقال : ما لا دليل عليه وجب نفيه، وقد عرفت بطلانه
فإن قال : المستدل نختار الأول وهو الأولوية في نفس الأمر ونقول : حكم الشيء الذي هو عدد من الأعداد مثلا حكم مثله من سائر الأعداد فإن المثلين يتشاركان في الأحكام اللازمة، فلو صح الثاني الثالث والرابع إلى ما لا يتناهى من أمثاله، وإذا لم تصح تلك الأمثال لم يصح هو أيضا
قلنا ما ذكره إعادة للدعوى بعبارة أخرى مع أنه لزمه في صورة الاستدلال على نفي الأعداد نفي الواحد أيضا لأنه مثل الثاني والثالث، فإذا انتفيا انتفي الواحد قطعا
فإن قيل : ليس الواحد مثل العدد قلنا إن كان العدد نفس الآحاد فقط كان الواحد مثلا له و إن اعتبر مع كل عدد صورة منوعة هي مبدأ لخواصه لم تكن
قوله : ( وعالميته أمر (واجب) يحتمل أن يشير به إلى عدم النقض بعدم علمنا بما لا يتناهى مع أن هذه المقدمة مستدركة لا يحتاج إليها
قوله : ( ما ذكره إعادة إلخ فيه بحث لأن الدعوى أنه ليس عدد أولى من عدد آخر في الثبوت والانتفاء، ونفس الأمر والدليل قولنا : لأن مراتب الأعداد متماثلة وحكم الأمثال واحد 
قوله : ( فإن قيل إلخ) لا يخفى أن المذكور سابقا أن الواحد مثل الثاني والثالث فلو انتفى الثاني والثالث انتفى الواحد، لأن حكم الأمثال واحد لأن الواحد مثل الاثنين والثلاثة، فلا ورود للاعتراض
قوله : ( إن كان العدد إلخ ) الملازمة ممنوعة لأنه يلزم تماثل الكل والجزء 
قوله : ( صورة منوعة) سواء كان أمرا وجوديا أو اعتباريا 
قوله : (كان الواحد مثلا له فيه بحث لأن مجموع أنفس الآحاد كم منفصل فله حقيقة غير حقيقة الوحدة لأنها ليست من قبيل الكم.
So we say: In explaining the weakness of this premise: the lack of priority between one number and another is denied in reality, because it is possible that some numbers have preference and priority over others in reality. Thus it is possible that the second exists while the third is impossible; so the existence of one number does not entail the existence of another, nor does the negation of one entail the negation of another. And the lack of priority in your mind is not useful, because lack of knowledge of priority does not entail its non-existence in itself, unless one says: what has no proof must be negated, and you have known the falsity of that. If the proponent says: we choose the first—priority in reality, and we say: the ruling of a thing which is a number among numbers, for example, is the ruling of its like from the rest of the numbers, because similars share in necessary rulings. So if the second is valid, then the third, fourth, and so on to infinity of its likes; and if those likes are not valid, then it is not valid either. We say: what he mentioned is just a restatement of the claim in other words. Moreover, it entails, in the case of the argument for the negation of numbers, the negation of the one as well, because it is similar to the second and third. So if they are negated, the one is negated absolutely. If it is said: the one is not like the number; we say: if the number is nothing but the ones themselves, then the one is like it; and if with each number a diversified form is considered which is the principle of its properties, then not. His statement: 'and His omniscience is a necessary thing'—he possibly means by that to prevent the objection from our not knowing what is infinite, even though this premise is redundant and not needed. His statement: 'what he mentioned is a restatement etc.'—there is discussion in it, because the claim is that no number has priority over another in existence and non-existence, and in reality the proof is our saying: because the ranks of numbers are similar and the ruling of similars is one. His statement: 'if it is said etc.'—it is not hidden that what was mentioned earlier is that the one is like the second and third; so if the second and third are negated, the one is negated, because the ruling of similars is one, because the one is like two and three; so the objection does not arise. His statement: 'if the number is etc.'—the implication is denied because it would entail the similarity of the whole and the part. His statement: 'a diversified form'—whether it is an existential or an intentional matter. His statement: 'the one would be like it'—there is discussion in it, because the totality of the ones themselves is a discrete quantity, so it has a reality other than the reality of oneness, because it is not of the category of quantity.
Source pages p. 45

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the principle that no number has priority over another, which is used in the earlier arguments. The author begins by denying that this principle holds in reality; it is possible that some numbers have intrinsic priority (e.g., two might be possible while three is not). Therefore, the existence of one number does not necessitate the existence of another, breaking the infinite regress. He then addresses a possible response: the opponent might claim that since there is no proof of priority, we must assume none exists; but this is rejected because absence of proof does not entail absence in reality. The opponent then restates the argument in terms of similars: numbers are similar, so the ruling for one applies to all. The author points out this is just a restatement and, moreover, it leads to the absurd conclusion that the number one itself must be negated, because it is similar to two and three. If two and three are impossible, then one is impossible too. The opponent objects that the number one is not like the number (plural), but the author says if a number is just a collection of ones, then one is similar; if each number has a unique property, then they are not similar. The commentary then adds further refinements: the premise may be redundant, the argument about similars is debated, and the objection about one being similar to two is valid. The final part discusses the nature of quantity and oneness, noting that the sum of ones is a discrete quantity, distinct from the mere concept of oneness.

حكم الأمثال
تماثل
نفي الواحد

Checkpoint
Why does Al-Jurjani say the similars argument leads to the negation of the number one?
A) Because the one is not similar to other numbers.
B) Because if the second and third are negated, the one, being similar, must also be negated.
C) Because the opponent said so.
D) Because the one is the first number.

Key Takeaway
The similars argument is not a valid proof because it is circular and leads to the absurd consequence of negating the number one.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever used an argument that seemed convincing but was actually circular? When have you assumed something was true without realizing it was just a restatement of your claim? This happens when we rely on familiar but unexamined ideas. Will you commit to testing your own reasoning against clear, independent proofs?
?
Al-Jurjani shows that a flawed argument can lead to absurd consequences, like negating the obvious existence of the number one. Where in your life do you hold onto a belief that, if followed to its logical end, would contradict something you know is true? Are you willing to trace that belief to its conclusion and let go if it fails?
?
The similars argument seemed plausible but was self-defeating. Do you have any cherished theological or personal convictions that you have not subjected to rigorous logical scrutiny? If this lesson has taught you that even clever reasoning can be hollow, will you now ask yourself: 'What if my most comfortable belief is actually circular?'

Objection: One Is Not Like a Number
Al-Jurjani addresses the objection about one not being like a number and discusses the nature of number.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that the number one is not like other numbers and Al-Jurjani's response regarding the nature of number.

Core Idea
Whether one is similar to numbers depends on how number is defined.

The opponent objects that the number one is not like a number (plural), so the previous refutation fails. Al-Jurjani replies with a conditional: if a number is simply a collection of ones, then one is indeed like a number; but if each number has a unique form, then they are not similar. The commentary adds further nuance about the nature of quantity and oneness.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
The opponent says the number one is not like other numbers (two, three, etc.), so the argument about similars doesn't apply to one.
The objection aims to salvage the similars argument by excluding one.
2
Al-Jurjani's conditional response
He says if a number is nothing but the ones themselves (e.g., two = 1+1), then one is like any other number. If each number has a distinct form, then not.
He does not commit to either view but shows the consequence.
3
Reflect on the commentary
The commentary adds that the sum of ones is a discrete quantity, different from the concept of oneness, so one may not be similar in all respects.
This is a more technical point about the ontology of numbers.

Refuting the Premise of No Priority Among Numbers
The commentator refutes the premise of no priority among numbers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the commentator refutes the premise that no number has priority over another, and why this undermines the proof for divine knowledge and power.

Core Idea
The premise 'no priority among numbers' is not necessary—intrinsic priority is possible.

The commentator examines a key premise used in proving God's knowledge of all things: that no number has priority over any other, so if God knows one thing, He must know all. He attacks this premise by showing that it is not necessarily true—some numbers may have inherent priority. This lesson walks through his refutation, including responses to objections and further absurdities that follow from the premise.

Guided Path
1
Understand the original proof
The proof argues: God knows more than one thing; no number has priority, so He must know everything knowable. Similarly for power.
This sets up the premise that the commentator will refute.
2
Identify the commentator's first objection
He denies that there is no priority in itself—it is possible that some numbers have preponderance over others, so the premise is not granted.
This is the core attack: the premise is not necessarily true.
3
Follow the response to a counter-argument
The disputant claims that likes share judgments, so if one number is valid, all similar numbers must be valid. The commentator shows this leads to negating the number one itself.
He uses the opponent's own logic against them.
4
Examine the further absurdity about the world's origination
If all numbers are equal, then the world could have been created at any time, leading to infinite possible creation times—something the disputants reject.
This shows the premise leads to unacceptable consequences.
5
Consider the final distinction
The commentator notes that if the infinite is impossible by proof, it cannot be compared to the finite; if not impossible, then the conclusion of the proof is not impossible, so the proof fails.
This wraps up the refutation by addressing both sides.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
عدد وذلك ) نحو كون الله عالماً بكل معلوم ) فإنه تعالى عالم بأكثر من معلوم واحد، وعالميته أمر واجب وليس عدد أولى من عدد فإما أن لا يجب كونه عالماً بأكثر من واحد وهو باطل اتفاقاً، أو يجب كونه عالماً بكل ما يصح أن يعلم وهو المطلوب (و) نحو كون الله تعالى ( قادراً على كل (ممكن فإنهم أثبتوه بهذه الطريقة ( فنقول : ( في بيان ضعف هذه المقدمة ( عدم الأولوية ) بين عدد وعدد (في نفس الأمر ممنوع) لجواز أن يكون لبعض الأعداد رجحان وأولوية على بعض في نفس الأمر، فجاز أن يكون الثاني مثلا حاصلاً مع استحالة الثالث فلا يلزم من ثبوت عدد ثبوت عدد آخر ولا من انتفاء عدد انتفاء عدد آخر ( و ) عدم الأولوية ( في ذهنك لا يفيد ) إذ لا يلزم من عدم العلم بالأولوية عدمها في نفسها إلا أن يقال : ما لا دليل عليه وجب نفيه، وقد عرفت بطلانه ( فإن قال : ( المستدل نختار الأول وهو الأولوية في نفس الأمر ونقول : ( حكم الشيء ) الذي هو عدد من الأعداد مثلاً ) حكم مثله من سائر الأعداد فإن المثلين يتشاركان في الأحكام اللازمة، فلو صح الثاني الثالث والرابع إلى ما لا يتناهى من أمثاله، وإذا لم تصح تلك الأمثال لم يصح هو أيضا، قلنا ما ذكره إعادة للدعوى بعبارة أخرى مع أنه ( لزمه ) في صورة الاستدلال على نفي الأعداد ( نفي الواحد ) أيضا لأنه مثل الثاني والثالث، فإذا انتفيا انتفي الواحد قطعاً، فإن قيل : ليس الواحد مثل العدد قلنا إن كان العدد نفس الآحاد فقط كان الواحد مثلاً له وإن اعتبر مع كل عدد صورة منوعة هي مبدأ لخواصه لم تكن الأعداد متماثلة أصلاً ، ولزمه في صورة الاستدلال على إثبات ما لا يتناهى من فساد آخر أشار إليه بقوله : ( وإذا يلزمهم صحة عدم العالم ) فإنه يصح تقديم إحداثه على الوقت الذي حدث فيه بوقت واحد وبوقتين وبأوقات ثلاثة وهلم جرا، لأن الأوقات كلها متساوية فيلزم صحة تقديم إحداثه على ذلك الوقت بأوقات لا نهاية لها، مع أنهم لا يقولون بها وهذا الذي ذكرناه من ضعف المقدمة الأولى مشترك بين جانبي النفي والإثبات كما تحققته ويخص جانب النفي بسؤال وهو أن ما لا يتناهي) من الأعداد ( إن امتنع لدليل قاطع دل عليه (لم يقس عليه ما لا يمتنع ) الأعداد المتناهية إذ ليس يلزم من تجويز ما لا دليل على امتناعه تجویز ما قام الدليل على امتناعه ) وإلا ) أي وإن لم يمتنع ما لا يتناهى من الأعداد لدليل دل عليه (لم يمكن نفيه ودعوى استحالته، فلا يكون اللازم من إثبات عدد مخصوص أمراً محالاً فلا يتم الاستدلال
([So] the number and that) — such as that God is knowing of everything known — for He, exalted, is knowing of more than one known thing, and His being knowing is a necessary matter, and no number has priority over another number. So either it is not necessary that He be knowing of more than one, which is false by agreement, or it is necessary that He be knowing of everything that can be known, which is the desired [conclusion]. And (similarly) such as that God, exalted, is powerful over every possible [thing] — for they proved it by this method. So we say: (in explaining the weakness of this premise: (the lack of priority) between number and number (in itself is denied) because it is possible that some numbers have preponderance and priority over others in themselves. So it is possible that the second, for example, exists while the third is impossible. So from the existence of a number the existence of another number does not follow, nor from the non-existence of a number the non-existence of another number. (And) the lack of priority (in your mind is not useful) for from the lack of knowledge of priority its absence in itself does not follow, unless it is said: what has no proof for it must be negated, and you know its invalidity. (For if the disputant says: we choose the first, which is priority in itself, and we say: (the ruling of a thing) that is a number, for example, (is the ruling of its likes among the rest of numbers) because two likes share in necessary judgments. So if the second is valid, then the third and the fourth up to infinity of its likes, and if those likes are not valid, it is also not valid. We say: what he mentioned is a repetition of the claim in other words, and moreover (it entails him) in the case of the proof for negating numbers (the negation of the one) also because it is like the second and the third. So if they are negated, the one is negated definitely. If it is said: the one is not like the number, we say: if the number is only the ones themselves, then the one is a likeness to it; but if with each number a differentiated form is considered that is the principle of its special properties, then numbers are not essentially identical. And it entails him in the case of the proof for establishing the infinite another absurdity, which he indicated by his saying: (and if so, it entails them the validity of the non-eternity of the world) because it is valid to advance its origination upon the time in which it occurred by one time, by two times, by three times, and so on, because all times are equal. So it entails the validity of advancing its origination upon that time by infinite times, while they do not affirm them. And this which we have mentioned of the weakness of the first premise is common between the sides of negation and affirmation as you have realized. And the side of negation is specified by a question: that what is infinite of numbers, if it is impossible due to a decisive proof that indicates it, then what is not impossible should not be measured against it, because it is not necessary that from considering permissible what there is no proof for its impossibility follows considering permissible what there is proof for its impossibility. Otherwise — i.e. if what is infinite of numbers is not impossible due to a proof indicating it — then its negation and claim of impossibility are not possible. So the necessary [conclusion] from the existence of a specific number is not an impossible matter, so the proof is not complete.
Source pages p. 46

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the first premise used in a proof for God's knowledge and power. The premise states that no number has priority over another, so if God knows one thing, He must know all. The commentator attacks this by arguing that intrinsic priority among numbers is possible, so the premise is not necessarily true. He then responds to a counter-argument that uses the principle 'the ruling of a thing is the ruling of its likeness,' showing that this leads to negating the number one itself. Furthermore, he points out that if numbers are essentially identical, it would imply the absurdity of the world being pre-eternal in infinite ways. The argument is concluded by showing that the proof fails because either the infinite is impossible by proof, and then it cannot be measured against the finite, or it is not impossible, and then the conclusion is not impossible. The commentary follows the order of the original: stating the premise, attacking it, handling the objection, and drawing the final conclusion.

Key Terms
عدم الأولوية
حكم الشيء حكم مثله
المتماثلة
ما لا يتناهى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if we have no knowledge of priority among numbers, then there is no priority in reality.
Correction: The commentator clarifies that lack of knowledge does not imply non-existence; priority may exist even if unknown.

Checkpoint
What is the first objection the commentator raises against the premise 'no number has priority over another'?
A) Numbers are essentially different.
B) It is possible that some numbers have intrinsic priority over others.
C) The premise contradicts divine power.
D) The premise leads to infinite regress.

Key Takeaway
The premise that no number has priority over another is not necessarily true, so the proof for God's knowledge that relies on it fails.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just seen how a flawed premise can lead to a false conclusion about God. Are there any premises in your own understanding of God that you have never critically examined? What would it take for you to scrutinize them, and are you willing to do so now?
?
This lesson shows that lack of evidence for something does not prove its non-existence. Where in your life have you confidently dismissed a spiritual truth—like God's mercy being sufficient for all sins—simply because you didn't see evidence of it? How will you adjust your thinking to leave room for what reason does not forbid?
?
The commentator refutes the argument that 'likes share judgments' by pointing out underlying differences. Are there people you have judged as 'just like everyone else'? This lesson asks you to reconsider: are you treating individuals as essentially identical when they are uniquely created by God? How will you see the unique form in every person?

The Word 'Rubbama': Doubt or Certainty?
Sanusi justifies the use of 'rubbama' despite permanent lack of certainty.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the word 'rubbama' is used in theological arguments even when the lack of certainty is permanent, and how it can indicate sufficiency or verification.

Core Idea
The word 'rubbama' can signify sufficiency and certainty, not just doubt.

In this passage, Sanusi responds to a potential objection about the use of the word 'rubbama' (perhaps) in the earlier argument. One might think that because the lack of certainty is constant, the word 'rubbama' is inappropriate. Sanusi clarifies that the word here serves to indicate that the possibility is sufficient for the argument, and it can also function rhetorically as a marker of certainty, as seen in the Qur'an.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice how the commentator justifies the phrasing.
2
Identify the objection
Why might someone question the use of 'rubbama'? Because the lack of certainty is constant, so why use a word that suggests doubt?
3
Understand the first justification
The word indicates that the possibility is sufficient (كفاية) for the argument at hand. It doesn't need to imply doubt.
4
Understand the second justification
'Rubbama' can also be used for verification (تحقيق), as in the Qur'anic verse where it expresses certainty of a future event.
The Qur'anic example shows that 'rubbama' can mean 'certainly' or 'surely'.
5
Reflect on the rhetorical usage
Think about how context determines meaning. In Islamic theology, words like 'rubbama' are flexible and serve rhetorical purposes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (ربما لم يحصل إلخ) زاد لفظ رب مع أن عدم حصول الجزم لأجل هذا الاحتمال دائم إشارة إلى كفايته فيما نحن بصدده ويجوز أن تكون كلمة رب للتحقيق كما قالوا: في قوله تعالى : ﴿ ربما يود الذين كفروا لو كانوا مسلمين ﴾ [ الحجر : ٢ ] .
His statement: '(Perhaps it is not obtained, etc.)' He added the word 'rubba' (ربما) even though the lack of obtaining certainty due to this probability is permanent, as an indication of its sufficiency in what we are dealing with. And it is permissible that the word 'rubba' is for verification (tahqiq), as they said in His statement, Exalted: 'Perhaps those who disbelieve will wish that they had been Muslims' [Al-Hijr: 2].
Source pages p. 57

Commentary
This brief passage comments on the author's use of the term 'rubbama' (ربما) in the earlier argument. The commentator notes that the lack of certainty due to the possibility of impossibility is constant, so one might wonder why the word 'rubbama' (which suggests uncertainty) is used. The explanation is that the word here indicates that this possibility is sufficient for the argument at hand, and it can also be understood as a rhetorical device for emphasis (tahqiq), similar to its use in the Quranic verse where it does not indicate doubt but rather certainty of a future event.

Key Terms
ربما
التحقيق
الاحتمال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The word 'rubbama' always indicates uncertainty or doubt.
Correction: In theological and rhetorical contexts, 'rubbama' can indicate sufficiency or be used for verification (tahqiq), as in the Quranic verse.

Checkpoint
Why does the commentator justify the use of 'rubbama'?
A) To show that the lack of certainty is temporary
B) To indicate that the possibility is sufficient for the argument
C) To express doubt about the argument
D) To negate the permanence of the lack of certainty

Key Takeaway
The word 'rubbama' can be used to indicate sufficiency or certainty, not just doubt, depending on context.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
