Al-Katibi's Objection and the Answer on Psychological Attributes
Al-Katibi objects that psychological attributes break the definition; the answer clarifies the meaning of 'li-mawjud'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Katibi's objection challenges the definition of hal and why the answer hinges on the interpretation of 'li-mawjud'.

Core Idea
The meaning of 'li-mawjud' determines whether psychological attributes are states.

This lesson presents a crucial objection from al-Katibi against the definition of hal. He argues that psychological attributes like substanceness break the definition because they exist for non-existent things. The answer clarifies that 'li-mawjud' means 'related to an existent', not 'exclusively owned by an existent'. This distinction saves the definition.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation once to get the overall flow of objection and answer.
Notice the technical terms for psychological attributes.
2
Identify the basis of the objection
Al-Katibi assumes that 'li-mawjud' means exclusive possession. Find where this assumption appears and how it leads to the counterexample.
The objection cites substanceness, blackness, and whiteness.
3
Understand the answer's reinterpretation
The answer interprets 'li-mawjud' as indicating mere relation and occurrence. See how this allows psychological attributes to be related to non-existents in a general sense.
The phrase 'في الجملة' is key.
4
Reflect on the significance
Consider why this distinction matters for the definition of hal. How does it preserve the definition from the objection?
The answer shows that the definition is not broken.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعترض الكاتبي على هذا التعريف بأنه منقوض بالصفات النفسية، كالجوهرية والسوادية والبياضية فإنها عندهم أحوال حاصلة للذوات حالتي وجودها وعدمها، والجواب أن المراد بكونه صفة للموجود أنه يكون صفة له في الجملة. قوله : (واعترض إلخ) مبنى الاعتراض حمل اللام في قوله لموجود على الاختصاص كما هو الظاهر وحاصل الجواب حمله على مجرد الارتباط والحصول فلا يضر حصوله للمعدوم أيضاً، إلا أنه لا حالاً إلا يسمى عند حصوله للموجود ليكون له تحقق تبعي في الجملة، فالصفات النفسية للمعدومات ليست بأحوال إلا إذا خرج تلك المعدومات فحينئذ تكون أحوالاً.
Al-Katibi objected to this definition that it is refuted by psychological attributes, such as substanceness, blackness, and whiteness, for they according to them are states (ahwal) that inhere in substances in both states of existence and non-existence. The answer is that what is meant by it being an attribute of an existent is that it is an attribute of it in a general sense (fi l-jumla). His statement: (And he objected etc.) The basis of the objection is taking the lam in his statement 'li-mawjud' as indicating specific possession, as is apparent, and the gist of the answer is interpreting it as mere relation and occurrence, so its occurrence for the non-existent does not harm, except that it is not called a state (hal) except when it occurs for the existent, so that it may have a dependent realization in a general sense. Thus, the psychological attributes of non-existent things are not states until those non-existent things come into existence; then they become states.
Source pages p. 64

Commentary
This passage presents an objection by al-Katibi against the definition of hal and the response to it. The objection states that psychological attributes (صفات نفسية) like 'substanceness' (الجوهرية), 'blackness' (السوادية), and 'whiteness' (البياضية) are considered states (أحوال) by the proponents, yet they inhere in substances both when the substance exists and when it does not exist (i.e., in the mind or as quiddities). This seems to violate the condition that a hal must be an attribute of an existent. The answer clarifies that the phrase 'li-mawjud' (of an existent) does not imply that the attribute always belongs to an existent at every moment; rather, it means that the attribute is related to an existent in a general sense (في الجملة). The commentator elaborates: the objection mistakenly interprets the lam as indicating exclusive possession, but it actually indicates mere relation and occurrence. Therefore, a psychological attribute can also relate to a non-existent thing, but it is only called a state (hal) when it actually occurs for an existent, because only then does it have dependent realization (تحقق تبعي). Thus, psychological attributes of non-existents are not states until those non-existents become existent.

Why 'Li-Mawjud' Is Not Redundant in Defining Hal
Objection and answer defending the qualifier 'li-mawjud' in the definition of hal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the qualifier 'li-mawjud' (of an existent) is essential to the definition of hal (state) and not redundant despite the prior exclusion of non-existent attributes.

Core Idea
Redundancy requires the first qualifier to suffice; here the second is needed for the concept itself.

This passage tackles a subtle objection: if the definition of hal already excludes non-existent attributes, why add 'of an existent'? The answer reveals a key principle of kalam terminology—qualifiers often convey essential meaning beyond mere exclusion. As you read, note how the commentator distinguishes between redundancy and conceptual necessity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
Read the first part of the passage where the objection is raised. It claims that 'li-mawjud' is redundant because the earlier qualifier 'la ma'duma' already excludes attributes of non-existents.
The objection uses a logical rule about redundancy.
2
Understand the answer's principle
The answer defines redundancy: it occurs only when the first qualifier makes the second unnecessary. Here, the first does not suffice without the second, so no redundancy.
3
Grasp the conceptual role
The answer explains that 'li-mawjud' is not for exclusion but because it is integral to the concept of hal—a hal is essentially an attribute dependent on an existent.
This is the key insight: the qualifier adds meaning, not just exclusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( لأن صفة المعدوم إلخ) أي الصفة المختصة بالمعدوم، فلا ترد الأحوال القائمة بالمعدوم كالصفات النفسية عند من قال : بحاليتها لا يقال : إذا كانت صفات المعدوم معدومة فهي خارجة بقوله : ولا معدومة فيكون قوله لموجود مستدركاً، لأنا نقول : الاستدراك أن يكون القيد الأول مغنياً عن الآخر دون العكس، نعم يرد على من قال : إنها لا موجودة ولا معدومة قائمة بموجود، ويجاب بأن ذكره لكونه معتبراً في مفهوم الحال لا للإخراج.
His statement: (Because the attribute of the non-existent etc.) meaning the attribute specific to the non-existent, so it does not rebut the states (ahwal) subsisting in the non-existent, like psychological attributes according to those who say they are states. It cannot be said: If the attributes of the non-existent are non-existent, then they are excluded by his statement 'and not non-existent', so his statement 'of an existent' becomes redundant. For we say: Redundancy occurs when the first qualifier suffices for the second without the reverse. Yes, it applies to those who say: They are neither existent nor non-existent, subsisting in an existent. The answer is that its mention is because it is considered in the concept of hal (state), not for exclusion (ikhraj).
Source pages p. 65

Commentary
This passage addresses an objection that the qualifier 'li-mawjud' (of an existent) in the definition of hal is redundant because the qualifier 'la ma'duma' (not non-existent) already excludes attributes of non-existent things. The commentator explains that redundancy occurs when the first qualifier renders the second unnecessary, but here the reverse is not true. Moreover, the qualifier 'li-mawjud' is not merely for exclusion but is integral to the very concept of hal: a hal is essentially an attribute that has a dependent realization (تحقق تبعي) in relation to an existent. Without this relation, it would not be a hal. Thus, 'li-mawjud' is not redundant; it conveys an essential aspect of the definition. The commentator also notes a variant view that describes hal as 'neither existent nor non-existent, subsisting in an existent', and the answer is the same: the mention of 'li-mawjud' is for the concept, not just exclusion.

استدراك
قيد
مفهوم الحال
تحقق تبعي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Correction: The passage shows that 'li-mawjud' is not redundant; it is integral to the concept of hal, which requires dependent realization in an existent.

Key Takeaway

Defining 'Hal' and Defending Against al-Katibi's Objection
Al-Mawaqif defines 'hal' and responds to an objection by al-Katibi.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the precise definition of 'hal' (state) in classical kalam and how it withstands al-Katibi's objection concerning psychological attributes.

Core Idea
'Hal' is an attribute of an existent, yet neither existent nor non-existent—defined with nuance.

In this passage, al-Mawaqif presents a three-part definition of 'hal' (state) that distinguishes it from accidents and negations. Al-Katibi then challenges this definition by pointing to psychological attributes like substantiality, which seem to be states even when the subject does not exist. The response clarifies a key nuance that preserves the definition.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition carefully
Read the Arabic definition of 'hal' and its three conditions: (1) attribute of an existent, (2) not itself existent, (3) not non-existent. Understand the rationale for each.
Notice the logical reasons: accidents are excluded because they have their own existence; negations are excluded because they are purely non-existent.
2
Identify al-Katibi's objection
Al-Katibi objects that psychological attributes (like substantiality) are considered states even when their subject does not exist. Find where this objection appears and see why it seems to break the definition.
The objection is that substantiality is a state of an essence whether the essence exists or not, but the definition requires the attribute to be of an existent.
3
Analyze the response
The answer says 'the attribute of an existent' means 'in general' (fi al-jumla), not 'always'. Understand how this resolves the objection: a psychological attribute can be a state only when its subject exists, not necessarily at all times.
This nuance saves the definition while allowing for attributes that are not tied to existence at every moment.
4
Reflect on the significance
Consider why this distinction matters for kalam reasoning about divine attributes and the nature of states. This definition is foundational for later discussions.
Understanding 'hal' is crucial for debates on whether attributes are distinct from essence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قولنا : الموجود لأن صفة المعدوم معدومة فلا تكون حالاً وقولنا : لا موجودة لتخرج الأعراض فإنها متحققة باعتبار ذواتها فهي من قبيل الموجود دون الحال وقولنا : ولا معدومة لتخرج السلوب التي يتصف بها الموجود فإنها معدومة لا أحوال واعترض الكاتبي على هذا التعريف بأنه منقوض بالصفات النفسية، كالجوهرية والسوادية والبياضية فإنها عندهم أحوال حاصلة للذوات حالتي وجودها وعدمها، والجواب أن المراد بكونه صفة للموجود أنه يكون صفة له في الجملة، لا أنه
Our saying: 'existing' because the attribute of the non-existent is non-existent, so it cannot be a state. And our saying: 'not existing' to exclude accidents, for they are realized with respect to their essences, so they are of the kind of the existent, not the state. And our saying: 'not non-existent' to exclude negations which the existent is described by, for they are non-existent, not states. And al-Katibi objected to this definition that it is invalidated by the psychological attributes, such as substantiality, blackness, and whiteness, for according to them they are states occurring to essences in both states of their existence and non-existence. The answer is that what is meant by 'attribute of the existent' is that it is an attribute for it in general, not that it is an attribute for it always.
Source pages p. 65

Commentary
This passage presents and defends a technical definition of 'hal' (state) in kalam. The definition has three parts: (1) it is an attribute of an existent, (2) it is not itself existent (to exclude accidents which have their own existence), and (3) it is not non-existent (to exclude mere negations that are non-existent). Al-Katibi raises an objection: psychological attributes like 'substantiality' are considered states even when the subject does not exist, which seems to contradict the requirement that the attribute be of an existent. The response clarifies that the definition only requires the attribute to belong to an existent 'in general' (fi al-jumla), not at every moment. Thus, a psychological attribute can be a state when its subject exists, even if it was not a state before the subject's existence.

السلوب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the definition of 'hal' excludes psychological attributes entirely because they are attributed to non-existent essences.
Correction: The definition only requires the attribute to be of an existent 'in general', meaning it suffices that the attribute belongs to an existent when it exists. Psychological attributes are states only when their subject exists, not necessarily at all times.

Checkpoint

A) Because it is an accident.
B) Because the definition only requires the attribute to be of an existent 'in general', not always.
C) Because it is a negation.
D) Because it is existent in itself.

Key Takeaway
A 'hal' is defined as an attribute of an existent that is neither existent (like accidents) nor non-existent (like negations), and this definition accommodates psychological attributes through the nuance of 'in general'.

The Three Real Categories of the Known
Explains the third opinion: threefold division and rejection of fourth category.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three real categories of the known according to the third opinion and why the absolute non-existent is not a separate fourth category.

Core Idea
The known is divided into three, not four: negated, existent, and possible non-existent.

This passage clarifies a key classification in Islamic theology. Different scholars offered different divisions of the known. Here, the author presents the third opinion, which refines the categories to three real ones and warns against a common misunderstanding about the absolute non-existent.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing; then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm and argument come through.
2
Identify the three categories
The passage explicitly lists three categories: al-manfi (the negated), al-thabit al-mawjud (the fixed-existent), and the fixed which is al-ma'dum al-mumkin (the possible non-existent).
3
Understand why absolute non-existent is not a fourth category
The passage argues that the absolute non-existent (al-ma'dum mutlaqan) goes back to either the negated or the possible non-existent, so it is not an independent fourth category.
4
Reflect on the author's precaution
Notice the author explains why these thinkers avoided dividing the fixed into existent and non-existent: to prevent misunderstanding. Then he dismisses that concern.
This shows careful reasoning in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فقد ذكر على رأي هؤلاء تقسيمين، لكن الأقسام عندهم في الحقيقة ثلاثة هي المنفي والثابت الموجود، والثابت الذي هو المعدوم الممكن، وأما المعدوم مطلقاً فهو راجع إلى المنفي والمعدوم الممكن فلا يكون قسماً رابعاً، وكأنه لم يقسم الثابت على رأيهم إلى الموجود والمعدوم كما فعله غيره، لئلا يتوهم من إطلاق المعدوم على المنفي كون قسيم الثابت قسما منه، لكنه مندفع بأن قسم الثابت هو المعدوم الذي له ثبوت أعني المعدوم الممكن، وذلك لا يطلق على المنفي، وإنما يطلق عليه المعدوم مطلقاً، وليس قسماً من الثابت حقيقة.
He mentioned two divisions according to these people, but the categories according to them in reality are three: the negated, the existent-which-is-fixed, and the fixed which is the possible non-existent. As for the absolute non-existent, it goes back to the negated and the possible non-existent, so it is not a fourth category. It is as if he did not divide the fixed according to their view into the existent and the non-existent as others did, so that it would not be imagined from the unrestricted application of 'non-existent' to the negated that the counterpart of the fixed is a part of it. But this is repelled by the fact that the division of the fixed is the non-existent that has fixity, namely the possible non-existent; that term is not applied to the negated; it is only applied to the absolute non-existent, which is not really a part of the fixed.
Source pages p. 67

Commentary
This passage explains the third opinion (القول الثالث) regarding the division of the known. The author states that according to these theologians (some mutakallimun), two divisions were mentioned, but in reality there are three categories: al-manfi (the negated), al-thabit al-mawjud (the fixed-existent), and al-thabit which is al-ma'dum al-mumkin (the possible non-existent). The absolute non-existent (al-ma'dum mutlaqan) is not a separate fourth category because it falls under either the negated (if impossible) or the possible non-existent (if contingent). The author notes that these thinkers avoided dividing the fixed (al-thabit) into existent and non-existent, unlike others, to prevent the misunderstanding that the counterpart of the fixed (i.e., the negated) is a part of it. However, this concern is dismissed because the fixed's division is the non-existent that has fixity (i.e., the possible non-existent), and that term is not applied to the negated; the negated is only called absolute non-existent, not a real part of the fixed.

Key Terms
المنفي
الثابت الموجود
المعدوم الممكن
المعدوم مطلقاً

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think the absolute non-existent is a fourth distinct category alongside the negated, fixed existent, and possible non-existent.
Correction: The absolute non-existent is not a separate category because it either falls under the negated (if impossible) or under the possible non-existent (if contingent but not actual).

Checkpoint

A) Two
B) Three
C) Four
D) Five

Key Takeaway
The known is actually divided into three real categories: the negated, the existent, and the possible non-existent; the absolute non-existent is not a distinct fourth category.

Can you identify one impossible notion you habitually entertain, and commit to dismissing it as truly negated?

The Mu'tazilite View: States and the Fourfold Division of Reality
Presents the Mu'tazilite view that states are real and divisions become four.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazilite position on states (ahwal) and how it leads to a fourfold division of reality, expanding the category of the fixed (al-thabit) to include three types.

Core Idea
For the Mu'tazila, states are real dependent beings, making the fixed include existent, state, and possible non-existent.

This lesson presents the fourth opinion on the classification of the known, attributed to some Mu'tazila who affirm the reality of states. They argue that in addition to independent existents and pure non-existents, there is a third category of dependent beings – states – which are real yet not fully independent. This expands the notion of the fixed to include three distinct types, creating a fourfold division of reality.

Guided Path
1
Understand the context
This is the fourth opinion in a series of classifications of the known. It is attributed to some Mu'tazila who affirm states (ahwal).
Recall that previous opinions had different divisions; this one introduces states as a real category.
2
Learn the key distinction
Beings in external entities (al-ka'in fi al-a'yan) are divided into two: those with independent existence (al-mawjud) and those with dependent existence (al-hal, the state).
The state is real but not self-subsistent.
3
See how the state fits into the fixed
The state is also a division of the fixed (al-thabit), alongside the existent and the possible non-existent.
This means the fixed now includes three items.
4
Grasp the four categories
The passage concludes that the categories are four: existent, state, possible non-existent, and negated (al-munfi).
The negated is that which has no verification at all.
5
Notice the expansion of the fixed
Unlike other opinions, here the fixed includes not only the existent and possible non-existent but also the state, showing the Mu'tazilite commitment to the reality of attributes.
This has implications for discussions about divine attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الاحتمال الرابع المعدوم ثابت والحال حق أيضاً (وهو قول بعض المعتزلة) من مثبتي الأحوال فيقول : الكائن في الأعيان إما أن يكون له كون بالاستقلال وهو الموجود أو يكون له كون بالتبعية وهو الحال فيكون الحال الذي هو قسم من الكائن في الأعيان أيضاً قسماً من الثابت كما أن الموجود والمعدوم الممكن قسمان منه وغيره أي غير الكائن في الأعيان هو المعدوم فإن كان له تحقق وتقرر في نفسه فثابت وإلا فمنفي فالأقسام أربعة فظهر أن الثابت الذي يقابل المنفي يتناول على هذا المذهب أموراً ثلاثة : الموجود والحال والمعدوم الممكن.
The fourth possibility: the non-existent is fixed and the state is also real – this is the view of some Mu'tazila who affirm states. They say: the being in external entities (al-ka'in fi al-a'yan) either has existence independently – and that is the existent – or has existence dependently – and that is the state. So the state, which is a division of the being in external entities, is also a division of the fixed, just as the existent and the possible non-existent are two divisions of it. And other than it, i.e., what is not a being in external entities, is the non-existent. If it has verification and determination in itself, then it is fixed; otherwise it is negated. So the categories are four. Thus it becomes clear that the fixed which is opposite the negated includes on this doctrine three things: the existent, the state, and the possible non-existent.
Source pages p. 67

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth opinion (الاحتمال الرابع) attributed to some Mu'tazila who affirm the reality of states (ahwal). They divide the known (al-ma'lum) as follows: First, what exists in external entities (al-ka'in fi al-a'yan) is either a being with independent existence (mawjud) or a being with dependent existence (hal, i.e., a state). The state is also a part of the fixed (al-thabit). Second, what is not a being in external entities is the non-existent (al-ma'dum); if it has verification in itself, it is fixed (like the possible non-existent), otherwise it is negated (like the impossible). Thus there are four categories: existent, state, possible non-existent, and negated. Consequently, the fixed (which is the opposite of the negated) includes three things: the existent, the state, and the possible non-existent. This view expands the scope of the fixed beyond the previous opinions.

Key Terms
الحال
الكائن في الأعيان

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The state (hal) is not real and has no existence at all.
Correction: According to the Mu'tazila, the state has a dependent existence (كون بالتبعية) and is a real division of the fixed.

Checkpoint

B) A being with independent existence

Key Takeaway
In the Mu'tazilite view, the fixed includes three kinds of beings: the existent, the state, and the possible non-existent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Answering the Objection on Perceptual Particulars
Al-Iji answers the objection that perceptual particulars are mental yet distinct by both essence and individuality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Iji resolves the objection that perceptual particulars are distinguished by both essence and individuality in mental existence.

Core Idea
Perceptual particulars are mental but their individuality belongs to external existence.

In this passage, al-Iji addresses a subtle objection: if perceptual particulars are mental existences, how can they be distinguished by both essence and individuality? He clarifies that the discussion is about their sensory actualization (taḥaqquq ḥissī), not external existence. Understanding this distinction requires careful attention to the difference between the 'known' (ma‘lūm) as a mental entity and 'knowledge' (ʿilm) as an external quality.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the translation. Notice that the passage contains a dialogue: an objection and its answer.
The passage is dense; focus on the main argument first.
2
Understand the objection
Identify the objection: perceptual particulars seem to be external because they are accidents subsisting in bodily powers. Al-Iji counters that this would make mental forms external too.
3
Follow the answer
Al-Iji defines 'external existent' as existing outside the senses. He then explains that perceptual particulars are not distinguished by essence and individuality in their mental actualization—only in their prior external actualization.
Note the shift from sensory actualization to external actualization.
4
Grasp the key distinction
Learn the difference between the 'known' (ma‘lūm, mental) and 'knowledge' (ʿilm, external). The mental content is the known, while knowledge as a psychological quality is external.
This distinction is crucial to avoid confusion.
5
Reflect on the warning
The passage ends with a warning that many scholars have slipped here. Test your understanding by explaining the distinction in your own words.
Use the checkpoint to verify your grasp.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( وبأن الجزئيات المدركة بالحواس الكلام في تحققها الحسي لا الخارجي، إذ لا شبهة فيه فإن قلت : الجزئي المدرك بالحواس يصدق عليه في تحققه الحسي أنه موجود خارجي، لأنه عرض قائم بقوة جسمانية قلت : لو صح الوجود الخارجي بهذا الاعتبار لكان الصور الذهنية موجودات خارجية، وبالجملة المراد بالموجود الخارجي الموجود في الخارج عن المشاعر أي القوى الدراكة فلا إشكال. وذلك كاف لنا فيما نحن بصدده وبأن المدرك بالحواس لا ينحاز في تحققه الذهني بماهية وهوية، تنضم إليها في هذا التحقق بل المنحاز في الخارج بماهية وهوية شخصية قوله : ( وبأن المدرك بالحواس إلخ) يعني أن المراد بالانحياز بالماهية والهوية الانحياز في ذلك التحقق كما هو السابق إلى الفهم، فالمنفي بقوله وإلا الانحياز بهما في ذلك التحقق، وهو أعم من أن لا يكون الانحياز بالهوية أصلاً كما في الكليات، أو يكون لكن لا في ذلك التحقق كما في الجزئيات المدركة بالحواس فإنها وإن كانت منحازة بهما لكن لا في هذا التحقق الحسي، بل في التحقق الخارجي وأما انحيازها بالهويات المنضمة إليها باعتبار ارتسامها في حواس جزئية، فهو انحياز للصور العلمية وهي موجودة في الخارج وليست بموجودات ذهنية إنما الموجود الذهني هي المعلومات أعني تلك المدركات الجزئية مع قطع النظر عن قيامها بالمحال، وتفصيله أن هاهنا معلوماً هو موجود ذهني وعلماً هو موجود خارجي من قبيل الكيفيات النفسانية والتمايز بين المعلوم والعلم على التحقيق بالاعتبار فالمعنى الحاصل في الذهن مع قطع النظر عن قيامه به معلوم وموجود ذهني وباعتبار قيامه به علم وموجود خارجي فتدبر، فإنه قد زل فيه أقدام الناظرين.
His saying: (And by the fact that the particulars perceived by the senses) the discussion is about their sensory actualization, not external, for there is no doubt about it. For if you say: The particular perceived by the senses is described in its sensory actualization as an external existent, because it is an accident subsisting by a bodily power, I say: If external existence were valid in this sense, then mental forms would be external existences. In short, what is meant by external existent is that which exists externally to the senses, i.e., the perceptual powers, so no problem. And that is sufficient for us in what we are dealing with. And that the sense-perceived is not distinguished in its mental actualization by an essence and an identity that are joined to it in this actualization; rather, what is distinguished externally is by an essence and a personal identity. His saying: (And that the sense-perceived etc.) means that what is meant by distinction by essence and identity is the distinction in that actualization, as is the previous understanding. So what is negated by his saying 'otherwise' is the distinction by both in that actualization, and this is more general than that there be no distinction by identity at all, as in universals, or that there be it but not in that actualization, as in the particulars perceived by the senses, for they, although distinguished by both, are not so in this sensory actualization, but in the external actualization. As for their distinction by the identities joined to them in virtue of their being imprinted in particular senses, it is a distinction of the scientific forms, and they are external existences and are not mental existences; rather, the mental existences are the known things, i.e., those perceived particulars, disregarding their subsistence in places. The detail is that here there is a known thing that is a mental existent, and a knowledge that is an external existent of the type of psychological qualities, and the distinction between the known and the knowledge in reality is by consideration. So the meaning obtained in the mind disregarding its subsistence in it is a known thing and a mental existent, and by virtue of its subsistence in it, it is knowledge and an external existent. So reflect, for the feet of the investigators have slipped here.
Source pages p. 69

Commentary
This passage addresses the second objection: perceptual particulars (e.g., objects perceived by the senses) are mental existences but appear to be distinguished by both essence and individuality (like external existences). The answer clarifies that the discussion concerns their sensory actualization (taḥaqquq ḥissī), not external existence. If one argues that sensory particulars are external because they are accidents subsisting in a bodily power, the reply is that then mental forms would also be external, which is false. Instead, 'external existent' means existing outside the senses (al-mawjūd fī al-khārij ʿan al-mashāʿir). The sense-perceived is not distinguished in its mental actualization by an essence and individuality that are joined to it in that mental presence; rather, the distinction by both occurs in its prior external actualization. The commentary further explains that what is negated is distinction by both in the very actualization under discussion. For perceptual particulars, they are distinguished by both essence and individuality, but not in the mental actualization—only in the external one. The distinction by identities that are joined due to being imprinted in particular senses is actually a distinction of the scientific forms (ṣuwar ʿilmiyyah), which are external existences (as psychological qualities), not mental existences. The mental existences are the 'known things' (maʿlūmāt) abstracted from their subsistence in the mind. Thus, there is a distinction between the known (maʿlūm, mental) and the knowledge (ʿilm, external). The passage warns that many have slipped here.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Mental images of perceived objects retain their own external individuality in the mind.
Correction: The individuality (identity) of a perceptual particular belongs to its external actualization, not its mental presence. In the mind, only the abstracted quiddity is present, without the personal identity.

Checkpoint
According to the answer in this passage, why are perceptual particulars not distinguished by individuality in their mental actualization?
A) Because they are universals in the mind.
B) Because their individuality belongs to their external actualization, not their mental presence.
C) Because mental existence has no individuality at all.
D) Because the mind eliminates all individuality.

Key Takeaway
Perceptual particulars are mentally existent as abstracted quiddities without their individual identity, which belongs exclusively to external existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recall a vivid sensory experience from your past, do you ever feel as though that memory has its own independent reality? How does al-Iji's distinction between mental and external actualization challenge you to re-evaluate the status of your own memories and the attachments you have to them? Commit to one memory you will consciously treat as a mental representation rather than a living reality.
?
The passage carefully separates the known (ma'lum) as a mental entity from knowledge ('ilm) as an external psychological quality. How does this distinction affect your understanding of your knowledge of Allah? Is your ʿilm of Him merely a concept in your mind, or does it have a reality that transforms your soul? Take a moment to assess: do you love the idea of Allah or Allah Himself? What practical step will you take today to ensure your knowledge of Allah is a living reality, not just an abstract concept?
?
Al-Iji warns that the feet of investigators have slipped here. Reflect on a time you confused your mental image of a person or situation with the external reality, leading to a misunderstanding or sin. The Joining of Individuality to Essence: Real or Conceptual?
Al-Iji discusses whether individuality is actually or conceptually joined to essence in external existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the debate over whether individuality is actually or conceptually joined to essence in external existence, and how later philosophers defend the conceptual interpretation.

Core Idea
Individuality's joining to essence is not always a real addition; sometimes it's a conceptual distinction.

This passage from al-Iji's Mawaqif tackles a subtle point in metaphysics: how individuality (tashakhkhuṣ) relates to essence (māhiyyah) in external existence. Is it a real additional component, or merely a conceptual consideration? The discussion reveals fundamental differences between earlier realists and later philosophers who deny the extra-mental existence of universal natures.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it, then read the translation. Note the flow from the initial gloss to the objections and responses.
The passage is dense; don't worry about every detail on the first read.
2
Identify the two main interpretations
The passage presents two views: individuality as an actual addition to essence (real joining) and individuality as merely conceptual (iʿtibārī). Find where each is mentioned.
The phrase 'aʿamm min al-taḥqīqī wa al-iʿtibārī' indicates the broader sense encompassing both.
3
Examine the clarification about 'no joining in the external'
Al-Iji clarifies that 'joined' does not mean a separate component in external actualization, because identity is united with essence. Understand why this clarification is necessary.
4
Consider the objection about pure imaginaries
The objector brings up purely mental objects like a winged square. Notice how al-Iji responds: such objects lack individuality because they are not imprinted via the senses.
This objection tests the consistency of the conceptual joining view.
5
Reflect on the broader significance
Think about how this debate connects to the realism vs. nominalism dispute in Islamic philosophy. Which view do you find more convincing?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (بماهية وهوية تنضم إليها إلخ إشارة إلى أن التشخص منضم إلى الماهية في الخارج، ولذا لا يحمل عليه وقالوا : إن الماهية إن اقتضت التشخص لذاتها انحصر نوعها في فرد وإلا يعلل تشخصها بموادها وأعراض تكتنف بها وما قيل : إن التعين أمر انتزاعي فهو مختار المتأخرين القائلين بعدم وجود الطبائع في الخارج، وإن أريد بالانضمام أعم من التحقيقي والانتزاعي يشمل المذهبين. قوله : (بل المنحاز في الخارج بماهية وهوية إلخ ليس المراد أن الموجود في الخارج منحاز بماهية وهوية تنضم إليها في التحقق الخارجي كما يتبادر من سياق كلامه، إذ لا انضمام في الخارج فإن الهوية متحدة مع الماهية في التحقق الخارجي سيأتي، ولذا لم يصرح بالانضمام فيه بل المراد أن الموجود الخارجي ينحاز في تحققه الخارجي بماهية وهوية ابتداء حصولها في ذلك التحقق بخلاف الجزئيات المرتسمة في الحواس فإن ابتداء حصول هوياتها ليس في هذا التحقق بل كانت متحققة في تحققها الخارجي، فارتسم المجموع في الحس ولك أن تكتفي بالمغايرة الاعتبارية في اعتبار الانضمام الخارجي، فإن قلت : هذا الجواب لا يتم في المخيلات الصرفة كمربع مجنح بمربعين فإن شخصيته بحسب الذهن فقط قلت : لا شخصية ولا هوية هناك فإن الشيء إذا لم يرتسم في الخيال من طرق الحواس لا يكون إلا كلياً، وهو ظاهر بالتأمل الصادق، فإن قلت : يصدق على تلك الجزئيات أنها منحازة بماهية وهوية تنضم إليها في تحققها الذهني وهي التشخص الذهني العارض في الذهن، فالسؤال باق قلت : قد سبق أن المراد بالهوية هو التشخص الخارجي سواء حصل في الذهن أيضاً أم لا، وإلا فالماهية الكلية الذهنية أيضاً منحازة بماهية وهوية على أنه قد يدعي أن ذلك التشخص
His saying: (By an essence and an identity joined to it etc.) It is an indication that individuation is joined to the essence in the external, and therefore it is not predicated of it. And they said: If the essence necessitates individuation by itself, its species is confined to a single individual; otherwise, its individuation is caused by its matters and accidents that surround it. And what is said that determination (taʿayyun) is a conceptual matter is the choice of the later scholars who hold that natures do not exist externally. And if what is meant by joining is more general than actual and conceptual, it includes both schools. His saying: (Rather the distinguished in the external by an essence and an identity etc.) is not that what exists externally is distinguished by an essence and an identity that are joined to it in the external actualization, as might be understood from the context of his speech, for there is no joining in the external, since identity is united with essence in the external actualization, as will come; therefore he did not explicitly state joining there. Rather, what is meant is that the external existent is distinguished in its external actualization by an essence and an identity that begin to occur in that actualization, unlike the particulars imprinted in the senses, for the beginning of the occurrence of their identities is not in this actualization; rather, they were actualized in their external actualization, so the whole became imprinted in the sense. And you may suffice with the conceptual difference in considering the external joining. For if you say: This answer does not hold for pure imaginaries like a winged square consisting of two squares, for its individuality is only mental. I say: There is no individuality or identity there, for if a thing is not imprinted in the imagination via the senses, it is only universal, and this is clear upon sincere reflection. For if you say: Those particulars are described as being distinguished by an essence and an identity joined to them in their mental actualization, which is the mental individuation that occurs in the mind, so the question remains. I say: It has preceded that what is meant by identity is external individuation, whether it also occurs in the mind or not. Otherwise, the universal mental essence would also be distinguished by an essence and an identity, except that it may be claimed that that individuation
Source pages p. 70

Commentary
This passage discusses the joining (inḍimām) of individuality (tashakhkhuṣ) to essence (māhiyyah) in external existence. It begins by explaining that in external existence, individuality is 'joined' to the essence, which is why it is not predicated of it. If the essence by itself entails individuality, the species has only one instance; otherwise, individuality is caused by matter and accidents. Later philosophers hold that determination (taʿayyun) is a conceptual (iʿtibārī) matter, while earlier realists hold that natures exist externally. The joining can be interpreted in a real (taḥqīqī) or conceptual (iʿtibārī) sense, accommodating both views. The passage then clarifies a statement: the phrase 'the distinguished in the external by an essence and an identity' does not mean there is a joining in the external actualization, because in the external, identity is united with essence; rather, it means that the external existent's distinction by essence and identity begins in that external actualization, unlike sensory particulars whose identities began in a prior external actualization. Then it addresses an objection about purely imagined objects (e.g., a winged square constructed from two squares): such objects have no individuality because they are not imprinted via the senses; they are universal. Another objection: sensory particulars in the mind have mental individuality. The answer reiterates that 'identity' means external individuality, not the accidental mental one. The text breaks off mid-sentence but the argument is clear: the distinction holds only for external individuation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Individuality is always a real, additional component attached to essence in all existents.
Correction: Some philosophers, especially later ones, hold that individuality is merely a conceptual distinction (iʿtibārī) and not an actual addition, particularly for universal natures.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, when individuality is said to be 'joined' to essence in external existence, what is the range of possible interpretations?
A) Only actual joining (taḥqīqī) is possible.
B) Only conceptual joining (iʿtibārī) is possible.
C) Both actual and conceptual joining are possible and include different schools.
D) No joining occurs; individuality is separate from essence.

Key Takeaway
The joining of individuality to essence can be interpreted either as an actual component or a conceptual distinction, and the choice reflects deeper metaphysical commitments about the existence of universals.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Distinction Between the Mental Known and Knowledge by Consideration
Explains the difference between the known object in the mind and knowledge as an external quality

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the subtle distinction between the mental known object (ma'lūm) and the act of knowledge ('ilm) based on the mode of consideration (i'tibār).

Core Idea
The same meaning is a mental known by itself and knowledge when subsisting in the mind.

This passage from Sharh al-Mawaqif addresses a common confusion in epistemology: what is the relationship between the object of knowledge in the mind and the act of knowing? The author explains that they are the same entity considered from two different perspectives. This distinction is crucial for avoiding philosophical errors about the nature of mental existence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation to grasp the overall argument.
Pay attention to the terms 'mental existent' and 'external existent'.
2
Understand key terms
Identify the terms: al-ma'lūm (the known), al-'ilm (knowledge), al-mawjūd al-dhihnī (mental existent), al-mawjūd al-khārijī (external existent), and al-i'tibār (consideration).
These terms are central to the distinction.
3
Trace the argument about location
Follow the author's explanation of 'being located by essence and identity' and how it applies to universals and particulars.
Universals have no identity location, particulars have external location.
4
Focus on the distinction by consideration
Notice the key point: the same meaning considered without subsistence is a mental known; considered as subsisting it is knowledge. This is the heart of the lesson.
Reflect on why many scholars have slipped here.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( وبأن المدرك بالحواس إلخ) يعني أن المراد بالانحياز بالماهية والهوية الانحياز في ذلك التحقق كما هو السابق إلى الفهم، فالمنفي بقوله وإلا الانحياز بهما في ذلك التحقق، وهو أعم من أن لا يكون الانحياز بالهوية أصلاً كما في الكليات، أو يكون لكن لا في ذلك التحقق كما في الجزئيات المدركة بالحواس فإنها وإن كانت منحازة بهما لكن لا في هذا التحقق الحسي، بل في التحقق الخارجي وأما انحيازها بالهويات المنضمة إليها باعتبار ارتسامها في حواس جزئية، فهو انحياز للصور العلمية وهي موجودة في الخارج وليست بموجودات ذهنية إنما الموجود الذهني هي المعلومات أعني تلك المدركات الجزئية مع قطع النظر عن قيامها بالمحال، وتفصيله أن هاهنا معلوماً هو موجود ذهني وعلماً هو موجود خارجي من قبيل الكيفيات النفسانية والتمايز بين المعلوم والعلم على التحقيق بالاعتبار فالمعنى الحاصل في الذهن مع قطع النظر عن قيامه به معلوم وموجود ذهني وباعتبار قيامه به علم وموجود خارجي فتدبر، فإنه قد زل فيه أقدام الناظرين.
And his statement: 'And that perceived by the senses' etc. means that what is intended by 'being located by essence and identity' is being located in that realization as previously understood. Thus what is negated by his statement 'otherwise' is being located by them in that realization. And it is more general than either that there is no location by identity at all, as in universals, or that there is location but not in that realization, as in particulars perceived by the senses; for although they are located by them, it is not in this sensory realization but in external realization. As for their location by the identities subsumed under them by virtue of their impression in particular senses, that is a location of scientific images, which exist externally and are not mental existents. Rather, the mental existent is the known objects, i.e. those perceived particulars, disregarding their subsistence in places. The detail is that here there is a known (ma'lūm) which is a mental existent, and a knowledge ('ilm) which is an external existent of the type of psychic qualities. The distinction between the known and knowledge, in reality, is by consideration. So the meaning obtained in the mind, disregarding its subsistence in it, is a known and a mental existent; and by virtue of its subsistence in it, it is knowledge and an external existent. So reflect, for the feet of the investigators have slipped in this.
Source pages p. 70

Commentary
This passage defines and clarifies the crucial distinction between the mental entity (al-ma'lūm) and the act of knowledge (al-'ilm) in the context of perception. The author begins by explaining the intended meaning of 'being located by essence and identity' in the statement under commentary. He then explains that negation of location applies to universals (which lack identity) and to sensory particulars (which have identity but not in the sensory realization). He then notes that the images in the senses are scientific forms that exist externally, not mentally. The mental existent is the known object itself, abstracted from its subsistence. Then he provides the key detail: the same meaning considered by itself is a mental existent (ma'lūm), but considered as subsisting in the mind it is knowledge ('ilm) and an external existent. This distinction is by consideration (i'tibār). He warns that many have slipped here.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The mental known object and knowledge are two completely separate entities.
Correction: They are the same meaning considered from two different perspectives: by itself (mental known) and as subsisting in the mind (knowledge).

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the difference between the mental existent (al-mawjūd al-dhihnī) and knowledge (al-'ilm)?
A) They are the same thing.
B) The mental existent is the meaning considered without subsistence, while knowledge is the same meaning considered as subsisting.
C) Knowledge is mental and the existent is external.
D) There is no difference.

Key Takeaway
The distinction between the mental known and knowledge is one of consideration (i'tibār): the same meaning is a mental existent when considered in itself, and knowledge when considered as subsisting in the mind.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you learn a new Islamic teaching, do you tend to treat it as a mental concept ('something I know') or as a quality that should subsist in your soul? Which approach dominates your study of aqeedah, and what would change if you prioritized the latter?
?
The author warns that many have slipped in this distinction. Reflect on one area of your faith where you might be confusing a correct mental understanding with actual knowledge that transforms your heart. What specific step can you take this week to turn a 'mental known' into a living knowledge subsisting in you?
?
Consider the fact that the same meaning can be both a mental existent and an external quality depending on consideration. How does this affect the way you approach the Names and Attributes of Allah? Are you content with mental concepts, or do you seek for them to become real qualities in your worship?

Individuation Joined to Essence in Existence
Explains that individuation is superadded to essence and presents two views on determination.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that individuation is joined to essence in external existence and how different schools explain the relationship between essence and its individuation.

Core Idea
Individuation is not essence; it is joined to it, like a coat on a person.

In this passage, the commentator unpacks a subtle but crucial distinction: in external reality, the essence of a thing and its individuation are not identical. Rather, individuation is something additional that attaches to the essence. He then explores two major ways philosophers and theologians have understood what determines an essence to become a specific individual. Let’s read the source text carefully and see how these positions unfold.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once through, then read the literal translation. Pay attention to the key term 'individuation' (tashakhkhuṣ) and how it relates to 'essence' (māhiyyah).
Notice the phrase 'joined to essence' – this is the central point.
2
Identify the two views on determination
The passage presents two contrasting views: either essence itself necessitates individuation, or individuation comes from external matters and accidents. Think about the implications of each.
The first view limits the species to one individual; the second allows multiplicity.
3
Understand the later scholars' position
The later scholars treat determination (ta‘ayyun) as an abstracted concept, not an extra thing in the world. See how this connects to their denial of natures existing externally.
The commentator then suggests a broad interpretation of 'joining' to reconcile both views.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (بماهية وهوية تنضم إليها إلخ إشارة إلى أن التشخص منضم إلى الماهية في الخارج، ولذا لا يحمل عليه وقالوا : إن الماهية إن اقتضت التشخص لذاتها انحصر نوعها في فرد وإلا يعلل تشخصها بموادها وأعراض تكتنف بها وما قيل : إن التعين أمر انتزاعي فهو مختار المتأخرين القائلين بعدم وجود الطبائع في الخارج، وإن أريد بالانضمام أعم من التحقيقي والانتزاعي يشمل المذهبين.
His statement: 'with an essence and an identity subsumed under it' etc. is an indication that individuation (tashakhkhuṣ) is joined to essence (māhiyyah) in external existence, and therefore is not predicated of it. And they said: If the essence necessitates individuation by itself, then its species would be confined to a single individual; otherwise its individuation is caused by its matters and accidents that surround it. And what was said that determination (ta‘ayyun) is an abstracted matter (amr intizā‘ī) is the choice of the later scholars who deny the existence of natures (ṭabā’i‘) in external existence. And if by 'joining' is meant broader than existential or abstracted, it covers both schools.
Source pages p. 70

Commentary
This passage explains a part of the commentary on the phrase 'with an essence and an identity subsumed under it'. The author points out that individuation is conjoined to essence in external reality, which is why we cannot predicate individuation of essence (it is not identical). Then he presents two views on individuation: either essence itself necessitates individuation (then the species has one individual), or individuation is caused by external matters and accidents. Then he notes that the view that determination is an abstracted concept (amr intizā‘ī) is the position of later scholars who deny that natures exist externally. If the term 'joining' is understood broadly to include both existential and abstracted sense, it covers both positions.

Key Terms

التعين
الأمر الانتزاعي
الطبائع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That essence and individuation are identical in external existence.
Correction: The passage clarifies that individuation is joined to essence, not predicated of it; they are not identical.

Checkpoint
What does the phrase 'if the essence necessitates individuation by itself' imply?
A) The species would have many individuals.
B) The species would be confined to one individual.
C) Individuation is impossible.
D) Essence and individuation are the same.

Key Takeaway
Individuation is not identical to essence; it is either necessitated by essence itself or caused by external factors, and later scholars see it as a mental abstraction.

Definition of Al-Hall in a Spatial Entity
Defines al-hall as specific indwelling in a spatial entity, with examples and exclusions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the technical definition of al-hulul (indwelling) as used in kalam for accidents in substances, and why it does not apply to God's attributes.

Core Idea
Al-hulul is specific indwelling with a single sensory reference, not loose occupancy.

In kalam, the term 'indwelling' (al-hulul) is a precise technical concept. It describes how accidents exist in substances—not like water in a jug, but like the color of a rose. This lesson unpacks that definition and clarifies what it excludes, especially concerning God's attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage and its literal translation to get a sense of the argument. Notice the examples of color in colored object and water in jug.
2
Understand the core definition
The key is that al-hulul requires a single sensory reference. Use the color example to solidify this concept.
3
Distinguish technical from linguistic
Note that 'indwelling' in everyday language (water in jug) is not the same as the technical term. The passage clarifies this.
4
See what al-hulul excludes
The passage explicitly excludes God's attributes and mentions the abstract category. Understand why these are excluded.
5
Reflect on the significance
Why does this matter? It prevents misunderstanding that God's attributes inhere in His essence like accidents.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المتحيز هو العرض ونعني بالحلول فيه ) أي في المتحيز ( أن يختص به بحيث تكون الإشارة ) الحسية ( إليهما واحدة كاللون مع المتلون فإن الإشارة إلى أحدهما عين الإشارة إلى الآخر ( دون الماء مع الكوز ) فإن الإشارة إليهما ليست واحدة، فإن الماء حالاً في الكوز اصطلاحاً وإن كان حالاً فيه لغة، وما ذكره تفسير للحلول في المتحيز كما صرح به، فلا يتجه عليه أنه لا يتناول حلول صفات الواجب تعالى في ذاته، فالأولى أن يفسر بالاختصاص الناعت وما ليس متحيزاً ولا حالاً فيه أعني الذي جعلناه قسماً ثالثاً من أقسام الممكن الحادث وهو المسمى بالمجرد لم يثبت وجوده ليس بالذات في الوجود العقلي وأما الواجب فهو خارج عن المشار إليه لأن المراد به الحادث المشار إليه، فلا يرد أنه لا حاجة في الاحتراز إلى اعتبار وجود المجردات لأنه احتراز عن الواجب تعالى . قوله : ( أن يختص به احتراز عن الماء الساري في الورد فإنه وإن كان الإشارة إليهما واحدة لكن لا اختصاص لأحدهما بالآخر، فإنه فرع وجود كل منهما في نفسه، ولا وجود للورد بدون الماء الساري فيه
That which occupies space (al-mutahayyiz) is the accident (al-ʿaraḍ). We mean by indwelling (al-ḥulūl) in it (i.e., in that which occupies space) that it is specific to it such that the (sensory) reference to both is one, like color with the colored object, for the reference to one of them is identical to the reference to the other, unlike water with the jug, for the reference to both is not one, since water is 'indwelling' in the jug terminologically, although it is indwelling in it linguistically. What he mentioned is an interpretation of indwelling in that which occupies space, as he stated. Thus it is not open to the objection that it does not encompass the indwelling of the attributes of the Necessary Exalted in His essence. Rather, it is better to interpret it as 'specific descriptive association' (al-ikhtiṣāṣ al-nāʿit). And that which is neither occupying space nor indwelling in it—namely that which we made a third division of possible originated things, called the abstract (al-mujarrad)—its existence is not established by essence in the intellectual existence. As for the Necessary, He is outside the pointed-to [referent] because what is meant by it is the originated pointed-to. So it is not objected that there is no need in the exclusion to consider the existence of abstracts, because it is an exclusion from the Necessary Exalted. His statement: 'that it is specific to it' is an exclusion from the water permeating the rose, for although the reference to both is one, there is no specificity of one to the other, for it is a branch of the existence of each in itself, and there is no existence of the rose without the water permeating it.
Source pages p. 74

Commentary
This passage defines the technical term al-ḥulūl (indwelling) as used in kalam for accidents inhering in substances. It begins by stating that al-mutahayyiz (that which occupies space) is the accident (al-ʿaraḍ), and that al-ḥulūl in a spatial entity means that the accident specifically attaches to it such that a sensory pointer to both is identical (e.g., color and the colored object). This is contrasted with water in a jug, where the pointer to water and jug are separate, although water is called 'indwelling' in a loose sense. The author then clarifies that this definition of indwelling in a spatial entity does not apply to the attributes of the Necessary Being (God) because His attributes are not like accidents in a substratum; rather, they are 'descriptive specificity' (al-ikhtiṣāṣ al-nāʿit). The text then turns to the third category of originated possible things—what is neither spatial nor indwelling—called al-mujarrad (the abstract). Its existence is not established, and a note explains that the term 'al-mutahayyiz' in the definition refers only to originated things, excluding the Necessary, so there is no need to consider abstracts for the exclusion. The commentary on the phrase 'أن يختص به' excludes cases like water in a rose where no specificity exists.

Key Terms
الحلول
المتحيز
العرض
الإشارة الحسية
الاختصاص الناعت
المجرد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that al-hulul includes God's attributes inhering in His essence like accidents.
Correction: The passage explicitly excludes this by redefining al-hulul as al-ikhtisas al-na'it (specific descriptive association) and stating that God is outside the definition.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what is the example that illustrates al-hulul in a spatial entity?
A) Water in a jug
B) Color in a colored object
C) Water permeating a rose
D) God's attributes in His essence

Key Takeaway
In kalam, al-hulul is a technical term for accidents that have a single sensory reference with their substance, and it does not apply to God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever unknowingly thought of Allah as being 'inside' creation like an accident in a substance? How does this lesson expose the error of such a thought? Commit to correcting your mental image of Allah's relationship to the world.
?
The passage teaches that true indwelling requires both single reference and specificity. In your own life, do you hold Allah as specifically and inseparably connected to your existence, or do you treat Him as loosely attached like water in a jug? What change in your daily awareness will you make to ensure your relationship with Him is one of true specificity?
?
If the existence of abstract entities (al-mujarrad) is not established, how much more should you focus on the concrete realities of the world as signs of Allah? Will you pay more attention to the precision of Islamic theology to guard your heart from vague or anthropomorphic beliefs?

The Abstract Substance: A Logical Category Without Proven Existence
The abstract substance as a third division and its epistemological status.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the abstract substance is a conceptual division of originated things, but its existence is not established according to Sunni theologians, and why this matters for excluding the Necessary Being.

Core Idea
The abstract substance is possible in logic but unproven in reality.

After dividing originated things into those that occupy space and those that inhere in such things, a third category remains: that which is neither. This is called the abstract (al-mujarrad). But as we will see, its existence is not established—it remains a theoretical possibility. This lesson clarifies its status and why the Necessary Being is not included in this division.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the key terms: mujarrad, mutahayyiz, and the phrase 'lam yathbut wujuduhu'.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the third category
The passage explains that the third category is 'what is neither occupying space nor indwelling' — the abstract substance. Understand that it is a logical division, not necessarily an existing entity.
This category is used in kalam for things like God's attributes or the human soul, but here existence is not affirmed.
3
Understand 'existence not established'
The author says 'lam yathbut wujuduhu' — its existence is not established. This means there is no proof for or against it; it is open. The Mu'tazilite claim about God's will being originated but abstract is given as an example.
Some scholars are content with this agnostic position, while others later argue impossibility.
4
Exclude the Necessary Being
The passage stresses that the Necessary Being (God) is excluded from this division because the division deals only with originated (hadith) things. This prevents confusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وما ليس متحيزاً ولا حالاً فيه أعني الذي جعلناه قسماً ثالثاً من أقسام الممكن الحادث وهو المسمى بالمجرد لم يثبت وجوده ليس بالذات في الوجود العقلي وأما الواجب فهو خارج عن المشار إليه لأن المراد به الحادث المشار إليه، فلا يرد أنه لا حاجة في الاحتراز إلى اعتبار وجود المجردات لأنه احتراز عن الواجب تعالى . قوله : (لم يثبت وجوده عندنا فإن قلت : بعض المعتزلة قالوا : إن إرادة الله تعالى حادثة لا عندنا ) إذ لم نجد عليه دليلاً فجاز أن يكون موجوداً، وأن لا يكون موجوداً سواء كان ممكناً أو ممتنعاً ( فمنهم من قنع بهذا القدر وهو أنه لم يثبت وجوده
And that which is neither occupying space nor indwelling in it—namely that which we made a third division of originated possible things, called the abstract (al-mujarrad)—its existence is not established by essence in the intellectual existence. As for the Necessary, He is outside the pointed-to [referent] because what is meant by it is the originated pointed-to. So it is not objected that there is no need in the exclusion to consider the existence of abstracts, because it is an exclusion from the Necessary Exalted. His statement: 'its existence is not established with us'—for if you say: Some Mu'tazila said that the will of God is originated, not with us—since we have not found a proof for it; so it is possible that it exists or that it does not exist, whether it is possible or impossible—so some of them are content with this measure, namely that its existence is not established.
Source pages p. 74

Commentary
This passage continues the division of originated things by introducing the third category: that which is neither occupying space (al-mutahayyiz) nor indwelling in that which occupies space. This is called al-mujarrad (the abstract). The author states that its existence is not established by essence in the intellectual realm. He reiterates that the Necessary Being (God) is excluded from this division because the division only concerns originated things. The commentary then focuses on the phrase 'its existence is not established with us' (عندنا). It references the Mu'tazila who claimed that God's will is originated but not in a substratum, meaning it would be an abstract. The commentator explains that some scholars are content simply to say that its existence is not proven, without ruling out its possibility or impossibility.

Key Terms

الممكن الحادث
المعطلة
الوجود العقلي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that the abstract substance is a proven existing entity (like angels or the human soul) based on this passage.
Correction: The passage explicitly states 'its existence is not established'. It remains a logical possibility without proof.

C) It is necessarily existing.
D) It is identical to the Necessary Being.

Key Takeaway
The abstract substance is a conceptual category of originated things whose external existence is not established, and it is carefully distinguished from the Necessary Being.

Affirmation as Union in Truth, Not Existence
Explains affirmation as truth-union, not existence-union.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that affirmation in logic is union in truth, not literal existence, and why expressions like 'existence' are metaphorical.

Core Idea
Affirmation is about truth, not existence.

In logic, an affirmative proposition does not require the subject or predicate to exist. The commentator clarifies that affirmation is union in truth (صدق), not union in existence. This distinction resolves puzzles about propositions like 'The partner of God is impossible' or 'Zayd is blind', where the predicate seemingly implies non-existence. The passage also explains that terms like 'existence' are used metaphorically for truth.

Guided Path
1
Understand the core definition
Read the passage that defines affirmation as union in truth, not existence. Note the examples given.
2
Examine the examples
Look at the examples 'شريك الباري ممتنع' and 'زيد أعمى'. See how they illustrate the point that truth does not require existence.
3
Grasp the metaphorical usage
The commentator explains that when we say 'the predicate is established for the subject', this is metaphorical. Understand why.
4
Reflect on the common misunderstanding
Identify the misconception that existence in a proposition is literal. See how the commentator refutes it.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (بل الموجبة ما حكم فيه إلخ) فإن الإيجاب هو الاتحاد في الصدق لا الاتحاد في الوجود، إذ قد لا يكون لشيء منهما وجود فكيف يتحدان في الوجود ؟ قوله : ( وقد لا يوجد إلخ هذه المقدمة مما لا حاجة إليه بعد ذكر أنهم قد لا يوجدان إلا أنه ذكرها لدفع أن يقال : إن القضية التي لا يوجد فيها الطرفان وإن كانت موجبة صورة لكنها في الحقيقة سالبة، فإن قولنا : شريك الباري ممتنع معناه أنه ليس بموجود بالضرورة. قوله : (كقولك زيد أعمى) فإن الأعمى لكون العمى مأخوذاً في مفهومه يمتنع وجوده مع اتحاده بزيد في الصدق فإن قيل : إن لم يكن له وجود في نفسه فله وجود رابطي قلت : إن أردت به الاتحاد في الصدق أو الاتصاف بالمبدأ فليس هاهنا وجود مقيد ليستدل ببداهته على بداهة الوجود المطلق، وإن أردت به شيئاً آخر فلا نسلم تحققه في القضية الموجبة والتعبير بثبوت المحمول للموضوع، وحصوله له على سبيل التجوز والاستعارة هكذا ينبغي أن يفهم هذا المقام فإنه مما خفي على أقوام . قوله : ( ممنوع بل ما حكم فيه إلخ) نعم قد يطلق لفظ الوجود والثبوت والتحقق والحصول على ذلك الصدق والاتصاف لمشابهته لمعناه الحقيقي كما سيصرح به الشارح.
His saying: ('Rather, the affirmative is that in which it is judged, etc.') For affirmation is union in truth, not union in existence, since it may be that neither of them has existence, so how can they be united in existence? His saying: ('And they may not exist, etc.') This premise is unnecessary after mentioning that they may not exist, but he mentioned it to repel the objection that a proposition whose two terms do not exist, even if it is affirmative in form, is in reality negative. For our saying: 'The partner of God is impossible' means that it is necessarily not existent. His saying: ('Like your saying: Zayd is blind') For the blind, because blindness is taken in its concept, its existence is impossible while it is united with Zayd in truth. For if it is said: it does not have existence in itself, but it has a relational existence (وجود رابطي). I say: if you mean by it the union in truth or the attribution to the principle, then there is no restricted existence here that would be used to prove the self-evidence of absolute existence. And if you mean something else, we do not grant its realization in the affirmative proposition. The expression of the predicate's 'thubut' (establishment) for the subject and its 'hushul' (occurrence) for it is metaphorical and figurative. This is how this station should be understood, for it is something that has been hidden from some people. His saying: ('It is denied; rather, that in which it is judged, etc.') Yes, the words 'existence', 'thubut', 'tahqiuq', and 'hushul' may be applied to that truth and attribution, due to their resemblance to their true meaning, as the commentator will explicitly state.
Source pages p. 85

Commentary
This passage provides a detailed explanation of the corrected definition of an affirmative proposition. The commentator emphasizes that affirmation (إيجاب) is essentially a union in truth (صدق), not a union in existence. If neither subject nor predicate exists externally, how could they be united in existence? He then addresses a potential misunderstanding about the example 'شريك الباري ممتنع' (the partner of God is impossible). Some might think that although the proposition is affirmative in form, it is actually negative in meaning. The commentator clarifies that it means the partner is necessarily non-existent. Then he addresses the example 'Zayd is blind'. 'Blind' (أعمى) has blindness built into its concept; so the existence of 'blindness' as a separate entity is impossible, yet the predicate 'blind' is true of Zayd. If someone objects that 'blindness' has a relational existence (وجود رابطي), the commentator replies that if by 'relational existence' you mean the truth-relation or attribution, then that is not a restricted existence (وجود مقيد) that could serve as evidence for the self-evidence of absolute existence. If you mean something else, it is not granted. He then explains that expressions like 'ثبوت المحمول للموضوع' (establishment of the predicate for the subject) are metaphorical, not literal. Finally, he notes that terms like 'وجود', 'ثبوت', 'تحقق', 'حصول' are sometimes used metaphorically for truth and attribution due to similarity, as the commentator (the original author of the commentary) will later clarify.

Key Terms
الإيجاب
الصدق
الوجود الرابطي
الاستعارة
ثبوت
تحقق
حصول

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That in an affirmative proposition, the predicate must have some kind of existence (whether mental, external, or relational) for the proposition to be true.
Correction: Affirmation is about truth, not existence. The predicate may not exist at all; the truth lies in the union of subject and predicate in judgment.

Checkpoint
According to the commentator, what does 'affirmation' (إيجاب) mean?
A) Union in existence
B) Union in truth
C) Union in concept
D) Union in essence

Key Takeaway
Affirmation is about truth, not existence; expressions of 'existence' for predicates are metaphorical.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'Allah is merciful', do you think 'mercy' exists as a separate entity, or is it a truth about Him? How does your answer affect the way you feel His mercy in your life?
?
The commentator says that even saying 'Zayd is blind' is an affirmation, despite 'blindness' having no existence. Reflect on a belief you hold that involves something you cannot see or prove—does your affirmation of it rest on truth or on a mistaken assumption of existence?
?
If terms like 'thubut' and 'hushul' are metaphorical when applied to predicates, what other religious terms might you be taking too literally? How would your faith change if you understood them as metaphors for deeper truths?
