قوله : ( الوجه الخامس قال إلخ) تقريره أنه لو لم يكن الوجود مشتركاً معنى لكان الحكم بأنه غير مشترك مطابقاً للواقع والتالي باطل لأن الحكم بأنه غير مشترك يستلزم الاشتراك، فلا يكون مطابقاً للواقع. قوله : ( يحكم عليه أي على ذلك المفهوم الواحد من حيث اتحاده بأفراده أعني الوجودات، فلا يرد عليه أن المحكوم عليه هي الأفراد لا العنوان فالصواب أن يقال : يحكم بملاحظته على تلك الوجودات .

قوله : ( وإذا لم تكن الظاهر لأنه إذا لم تكن لأنه دليل للملازمة المستفادة من الشرطية السابقة إلا أنه أورده بالعطف إشارة إلى أن هذه المقدمة محققة مقررة لا شبهة فيها مع قطع النظر عن جعلها دليل الملازمة . قوله : ( عامة لها بأن لا يوجد مفهوم مشترك بينها يجعل عنواناً لملاحظتها. قوله : ( لأن تلك الدعوى حينئذ أي حين فرض أن لا يوجد مفهوم شامل لتلك الأمور متعددة بحسب تعدد تلك الأمور، فكانت قضايا متعددة لا بد في الاستدلال عليها ملاحظة من كل واحد من تلك الأمور، بخصوصه وجعله أصغر وإثبات الأوسط له فيحصل صغريات متعددة تتعدد الدلائل بحسب تعددها مثلا إذا قيل : الوجودات نفس الحقائق ولا شيء من الحقائق بمشتركة وفرض أنه ليس مفهوماً واحداً يجعل آلة لملاحظة تلك الوجودات لا بد من ملاحظة كل واحد منها بخصوصه، ويقال : هذه حقيقة وتلك حقيقة فيحصل صغريات متكثرة حسب تكثر الوجودات، فضم إلى تلك الكبرى فلا يكون الدليل واحداً.
قوله : (بل يتناول إلخ لا يتوهم من هذا أن من قال : بأن الوجود غير مشترك أراد به أن الوجودات الخاصة غير مشتركة لأنه لا يقبل النزاع بل أراد أن لا شيء من الوجود بمشترك، ويلزم من هذا كون كل وجود خاصاً وغير مشترك فتدبر، فإنه زل فيه أقدام .
The fifth face: That some of the virtuous said: Whoever claims that existence is not common has admitted that it is common without realizing it, because if he had not conceived a single concept encompassing all existents, he would not have judged it to be not common among existents. For if the claim concerning multiple things is not general, it cannot be proven by a single general proof, because that claim would then be multiple in meaning according to the multiplicity of those things, so each of those claims would require a separate proof. The conclusion is that if a proof is one and covers multiple things, the claim must be general and cover that multiplicity, and its generality over them is only by taking a single common meaning for all of them, for otherwise one would have to attend to the particularity of each of those multiples. So whoever says that existence is not common, his judgment is undoubtedly not restricted to one existence but encompasses every existence; if it were... His saying: (The fifth face, he said, etc.) Its purport is that if existence were not common in meaning, then the judgment that it is not common would correspond to reality, but the consequent is false because the judgment that it is not common entails commonality, so it would not correspond to reality. His saying: (He judges it) meaning that single concept in terms of its unity with its individuals, i.e., existences, so it is not objected that the subject of judgment is the individuals, not the concept; rather the correct thing is to say: He judges by attending to it upon those existences. His saying: (And if it is not) the apparent meaning is 'because if it is not' as a proof for the implication derived from the preceding conditional, except that he presented it with a conjunction to indicate that this premise is established and determined with no doubt, regardless of making it a proof for the implication. His saying: (General for them) meaning that there is no common concept between them that could be made a title for attending to them. His saying: (Because that claim then) meaning when it is assumed that there is no inclusive concept for those matters, it becomes multiple according to the multiplicity of those matters, so they are multiple propositions; in inferring them, it is necessary to attend to each one of those matters individually, to make it the minor term, and to establish the middle term for it, resulting in multiple minor premises, and the proofs become multiple according to their multiplicity. For example, if it is said: 'Existences are the same as realities, and none of the realities are common,' and it is assumed that there is no single concept to use as an instrument for attending to those existences, then one must attend to each one individually, saying: 'This is a reality and that is a reality,' thus obtaining numerous minor premises according to the multiplicity of existences, then adding the major premise; so the proof is not one. His saying: (Rather it encompasses, etc.) Do not imagine from this that whoever says existence is not common means that particular existences are not common, because this is not debatable; rather he means that nothing of existence is common, and from this it follows that every existence is particular and not common. So reflect, for feet have slipped on this.
Source pages p. 123

Commentary
This passage presents the fifth argument for the commonality of existence, attributed to some virtuous scholar, along with the commentator's explanation. The argument is: If someone claims that existence is not common (i.e., not a single concept shared by all existents), he must already have a single concept of existence in mind to make that claim. Otherwise, his claim would not be a single judgment covering all existents, and he would need separate proofs for each existence. The commentator explains the logic: The judgment 'existence is not common' is a single universal judgment; if existence were not a single concept, the judgment would not be universal. The commentator then clarifies that the subject of the judgment is the concept, not the individuals, and that the opponent's position is self-defeating because to deny commonality, one must use a common concept. The commentator also addresses the objection that the argument assumes a single concept when the opponent might deny that. He explains that the opponent's own claim of a universal statement implies the existence of a common concept. The passage concludes with a warning not to misunderstand: the opponent does not deny that particular existences are common (that is obvious), but that no existence at all is common; yet this very denial requires a common concept.

Correction: The opponent actually means that no existence at all is common (i.e., existence itself is not a shared concept). But this very universal denial requires a common concept, making the position self-defeating.

Defending the Commonality of Existence: Objection and Answer
Objection and answer regarding the common person in existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the objection against the commonality of existence (that it would require a real universal) is refuted by distinguishing between mental conception and real existence, and by interpreting 'existence' as a comprehensive name.

Core Idea
A universal concept does not need to be a real entity; it suffices as a mental notion or a comprehensive name.

The claim that existence is a common concept seems intuitive, but it faces a powerful objection: if existence is shared, it would require a real 'common person' existing in multiple individuals, which is impossible. In this passage, the theologian defends the commonality by showing that a negative proposition (like 'there is no common person') only requires conceiving the subject, not its external existence, and by reinterpreting existence as the name itself, which includes all particularities.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Understand the objection that if existence is common, it must be a real universal (common person) shared by distinct things, but that is impossible.
The objection uses the term 'common person' (الشخص المشترك) meaning a universal entity.
2
Analyze the first answer: negative proposition
The text says a negative proposition (السالبة) does not require the existence of its subject, only its mental conception. So saying 'there is no common person' does not assume such a person exists; it only requires us to conceive of it.
This is a key logical principle in kalam.
3
Examine the second answer: existence as a name
Alternatively, what is meant by 'existence' is just the word itself, which is a single meaning covering all particularities. Judgments about existence are made under this general title without proving each particular.
This avoids the need for a real universal.
4
Reflect on the overall defense
See how both answers show that commonality of existence is not refuted by the impossibility of a real universal, because the common concept is mental, not real.
This protects the theological usage of existence applied to God and creation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الأول - المقصد الثاني : في أنه مشترك عاماً (كما يقال : لا يوجد شخص مشترك فيه بين اثنين فإنه لا يقتضي شخصاً مشتركاً بينهما لاستحالته بل يقتضي تصوره ) وتحقيقه أن السالبة لا تقتضي وجود الموضوع، بل تصوره فقط، ويمكن أن يجاب أيضاً بأن المراد بالوجود هو المسمى بلفظ الوجود، وهذا معنى واحد شامل لجميع الخصوصيات، فيحكم عليه حكماً لها بهذا العنوان المتناول إياها من غير حاجة إلى أن يبرهن على خصوصية كل واحد منها
The first observatory - the second aim: concerning that it (existence) is common/general. (As it is said: There is no person shared between two, for it does not necessitate a person shared between them due to its impossibility, but it necessitates its conception.) And its verification is that a negative proposition does not necessitate the existence of the subject, but only its conception. And it can also be answered that what is meant by existence is what is named by the word 'existence', and this is one meaning inclusive of all particularities, so that a judgment is made about it (existence) by this title that encompasses them without needing to prove the particularity of each one of them.
Source pages p. 125

Commentary
This passage presents an objection against the view that existence is a common concept and provides an answer. The objection states that if existence were a common concept, it would require a common person (a universal) shared between two individuals, which is impossible. The verification explains that a negative proposition does not require the existence of its subject, only its conception. The answer is that what is meant by existence is the name 'existence', which is a single meaning inclusive of all particularities, and judgments are made about it under this title without needing to prove each particularity.

Misconception: Many people assume that if a concept is common (like existence), it must correspond to a real entity existing in the external world that is literally shared by multiple things.
Correction: The passage clarifies that a common concept only needs to be a mental conception (for the negative proposition) or a linguistic term (the name) that encompasses all instances. It does not require a real universal entity.

The Sixth Argument for Common Existence and Its Refutation
Sanusi presents the sixth argument and its refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the sixth argument for the commonality of existence and why it fails due to its reliance on an impossible assumption.

Core Idea
The sixth argument fails because it assumes one thing can have two existences – which is impossible.

The sixth argument attempts to prove that existence must be a single common meaning by arguing that otherwise the distinction between necessary and contingent collapses. However, the refutation shows this argument relies on an impossible assumption. Let's examine the logic and its flaw.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its literal English translation carefully. Try to grasp the argument and the response.
The passage is short but dense; read slowly.
2
Identify the argument and its key assumption
The argument says that if existence had multiple meanings, a thing could be necessary in one sense and contingent in another. Spot why this is supposedly problematic.
The key assumption is that a single thing can have two existences.
3
Understand the refutation
The refutation points out that the argument's logic only works if one thing can have two existences – which is impossible. See how this completely invalidates the argument.
This shows a logical error in the argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه السادس) لو لم يكن (الوجود معنى واحداً مشتركاً لم يتميز الواجب عن الممكن فإنا إذا قلنا على تقدير كون الوجود معاني متعددة الشيء ( إما أن يجب وجوده أو لا، فقد يجب له الوجود بمعني ولا يجب بمعنى آخر) فيكون الشيء الواحد واجباً ممكناً معاً، فلا يتميز أن أصلاً بخلاف ما إذا كان الوجود معنى واحداً لاستحالة أن يكون نسبة المعنى الواحد إلى شيء واحد بالوجوب، والإمكان معاً بالنظر إلى ذاته ( والجواب ) إن ما ذكرتم مبني على جواز أن يكون لشيء واحد وجودان وكون الشيء الواحد له وجودان وإن كان الوجود نفس الحقيقة أو زائداً عليها معلوم الانتفاء بالضرورة لامتناع أن تكون الحقيقة الواحدة حقيقتين أو أن تكون موجودة بوجودين وإن كانا زائدين عليها
(The sixth aspect) If existence were not a single common meaning, then the necessary and the contingent would not be distinguished. For if we say, assuming that existence has multiple meanings, a thing is either its existence is necessary or not, so its existence may be necessary in one sense and not necessary in another, so the same thing would be necessary and contingent together, and thus it is not distinguished at all. Contrary to if existence were a single meaning, because it is impossible for the relation of a single meaning to one thing to be both necessity and possibility simultaneously with respect to its essence. (The answer) What you mentioned is based on the permissibility of one thing having two existences. And the fact that one thing has two existences – whether existence is the essence itself or additional to it – is known to be impossible necessarily, because it is impossible for a single essence to be two essences or to exist with two existences, even if they are additional to it.
Source pages p. 125

Commentary
This passage presents the sixth argument that existence must be a common meaning, otherwise the distinction between necessary and contingent would fail. The argument assumes that if existence has multiple meanings, the same thing could be necessary in one sense and contingent in another, making it impossible to distinguish. The answer refutes this by stating that the argument relies on the possibility of a single thing having two existences, which is impossible. Therefore, the argument is invalid.

Key Terms

الواجب
الممكن
الوجودان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Thinking that the sixth argument is a valid proof for the commonality of existence, ignoring the refutation that it assumes the impossible.
Correction: The argument is invalid because it relies on the impossible assumption that one thing can have two existences.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The sixth argument fails because it assumes a thing can have two existences, which is impossible.

The Three Positions on Existence and Essence
Enumeration of the three positions on existence being identical to essence or additional.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three possible positions on the relationship between existence and essence in kalam, and why the fourth possibility is logically excluded.

Core Idea
The relationship between existence and essence is limited to three logical positions.

This passage from Sharh al-Mawaqif introduces a foundational classification in Islamic theology: the possible views on whether existence is identical to essence, a part of it, or additional to it. The author systematically eliminates the impossible options to arrive at the three schools held by theologians. This classification is essential for understanding debates about God's attributes and the nature of contingent beings.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation once to get the overall structure. Note the three positions listed at the end.
The passage includes both the main text and a line-by-line commentary.
2
Identify the logical possibilities
Understand why the fourth possibility (existence identical in contingent but additional in Necessary) is dismissed because no one held it.
This elimination reduces the options to three.
3
Clarify the term 'or'
Notice the commentary's point that the word 'or' in 'identical or a part' is not a division into two separate schools but a disjunction within a single proposition.
This prevents misunderstanding about the number of positions.
4
Connect to theological schools
Consider how each position corresponds to known theological schools (e.g., Maturidi, Ash'ari, etc.) as discussed in broader context.
The passage itself does not name schools but the commentary hints at Ash'ari.
5
Reflect on the significance
Ask yourself why this classification matters for understanding God's essence and attributes.
This foundation affects subsequent discussions on divine attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
في أن الوجود نفس الماهية أو جزؤها أو زائد عليها وفيه مذاهب) ثلاثة لأنه إذ لم يقل أحد بأن الوجود جزء الماهية فإما أن يكون نفس الماهية في الكل أي الواجب والممكن جميعاً أو زائداً عليها في الكل، أو يكون نفس الماهية في الواجب زائداً عليها في الممكن أو بالعكس وهذا الاحتمال الأخير لم يقل به أحد، فانحصرت المذاهب في ثلاثة

قوله : ( فإن الوجود إلخ) أي ما يطلق عليه الوجود لا الوجود المطلق، فيشمل مذهب الأشعري أيضاً ولذا وضع المظهر موضع المضمر. قوله : (نفس الماهية أو جزؤها إلخ) كلمة أو ليس للتقسيم ولا للترديد إذ لا مذهب في انقسامه وترديده ،للعقليين بل هو لأحد الأمرين على ما هو أصل وضعه فهو قضية حملية محمولها أحد الأمور الثلاثة عند العقل وفي هذه القضية التي محمولها أحد الأمور الثلاثة عند العقل على سبيل منع الخلو مذاهب ثلاثة باعتبار تعيين محمولها . قوله : ( بأن الوجود جزء الماهية) فسقط كونه جزءاً في الكل وكونه جزءاً في البعض سواء كان عيناً في البعض الآخر أو زائداً . قوله: (فإما أن يكون إلخ الانحصار في هذه الاحتمالات الأربعة بناء على عدم اعتبار التفصيل في الممكن .
Concerning that existence is identical to essence or a part of it or additional to it, and regarding it are three schools: because since no one has said that existence is a part of essence, then either existence is identical to essence in all, i.e., the Necessary and the contingent both, or additional to it in all, or it is identical to essence in the Necessary and additional to it in the contingent, or the opposite; and this last possibility no one has adopted, so the schools are reduced to three.

His statement: 'for existence etc.' means what is termed existence, not absolute existence, so it includes the school of Ash'ari as well, and therefore he placed the manifest pronoun in place of the covert pronoun. His statement: 'identical to essence or a part of it etc.' The word 'or' is not for division nor for hesitation, since there is no school regarding its division or hesitation for the rationalists, but rather it is for one of the two things according to its original usage; so it is a predicative proposition whose predicate is one of three things according to reason, and in this proposition whose predicate is one of three things according to reason by way of prevention of emptiness, there are three schools with regard to determining its predicate. His statement: 'that existence is a part of essence'—so it falls that it is a part in all or a part in some, whether it is identical in the other part or additional. His statement: 'then either it is etc.'—the restriction to these four possibilities is based on not considering detail in the contingent.
Source pages p. 127

Commentary
This passage is a classification of the possible positions regarding the relationship between existence (wujud) and essence (mahiyyah). The author begins by stating that the topic is whether existence is identical to essence, a part of it, or additional to it. He notes that no one has held that existence is a part of essence, so the remaining possibilities are narrowed down. The logical possibilities are: (1) existence is identical to essence in all cases (both Necessary and contingent), (2) existence is additional to essence in all cases, or (3) existence is identical in the Necessary and additional in the contingent, or (4) the reverse. Since no one has held the last possibility, the schools are reduced to three. The commentary then clarifies that the word 'or' in the phrase 'naturally identical or a part' is not a disjunction of two separate positions but rather indicates a choice between the two possibilities in a single proposition. The commentator explains that this proposition has a predicate that could be any one of three things (identical, part, or additional) and that the schools differ in determining which one is actually the case. He also notes that the possibility of existence being a part of essence is excluded entirely.

Key Terms
الوجود (al-wujud)
الماهية (al-mahiyyah)
المذاهب (al-madhahib)
الواجب (al-wajib)
الممكن (al-mumkin)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The phrase 'existence is identical to essence or a part of it' indicates two separate schools of thought.
Correction: The word 'or' here is not a division into multiple positions but a disjunction within a single proposition; the schools are determined by which of the three possibilities (identical, part, additional) is held.

Checkpoint
How many positions (mazahib) are mentioned regarding whether existence is identical to essence or additional?
A) 1
B) 2
C) 3
D) 4

Key Takeaway
The three schools on existence and essence are determined by eliminating logically untenable positions, resulting in three viable doctrines.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you examine your own existence, do you perceive it as something added to your essence (i.e., you might have not existed), or as identical to your essence? How does that perception affect your gratitude and reliance on God?
?
If God's existence is identical to His essence, and your existence is additional to your essence, what does that imply about your absolute dependence on Him? Are you living with that awareness?
?
The school that holds existence additional to essence in all things (including God) would imply God's existence is separate from His essence. Does your heart find more comfort in God's simplicity or in viewing His attributes as distinct? What does your worship reveal?

First Proof: Acceptance of Non-Existence
Al-Jurjani presents the first proof that existence is not identical to essence nor a part of it.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Jurjani argues that existence is neither identical to essence nor a part of it, by showing that the opponent's denial of essence's acceptance of non-existence is invalid under the identity assumption.

Core Idea
Existence's removability proves it cannot be identical to essence.

Al-Jurjani begins the chapter on the relationship between existence and essence with the first proof. He challenges the opponent who claims that essence does not accept non-existence. By analyzing the logical consequences of identifying existence with essence, he shows that the opponent's position is self-defeating. This lesson unpacks that subtle argument.

Guided Path
1
Understand the opponent's claim
The opponent asserts that if existence were identical to essence, then essence would not accept non-existence. Grasp this premise before following the rebuttal.
2
Follow the conditional reasoning
Al-Jurjani responds by questioning the opponent's conditional. He argues that even if existence were identical, essence could still accept non-existence because existence itself can be removed.
3
Examine the key premise: existence's removability
Existence of a possible thing can be completely removed when the essence is removed. This is because existence does not subsist by itself or by another when its substratum is gone.
4
See the conclusion
Since existence can be qualified by its opposite (non-existence), under the identity hypothesis the essence can also be so qualified. Thus the opponent's argument fails.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هي نفي صرف ( وإن أردت ) بقبولها العدم ( ارتفاعها ) بالكلية، فلا نسلم أنها لو كانت نفس الوجود أو كان الوجود جزءها ( لما قبلته أي لما قبلت الماهية من حيث هي هي العدم وذلك لأن الوجود نفسه يرتفع بالكلية ( لأنه إذا ارتفع الماهية ) الممكنة ) فقد ارتفع وجودها قطعاً ) إذ لا يجوز قيام ذلك الوجود بذاته ولا بغير تلك الماهية ولو قام بها لم تكن مرتفعة بل ،موجودة وإذا جاز ارتفاع الوجود بالكلية واتصافه اشتقاقاً بنقيضه الذي هو العدم جاز ذلك في الماهية على تقدير كون الوجود نفسها .
It is a pure negation (and if you mean) by its acceptance of non-existence (its complete removal), then we do not accept that if it were existence itself or existence were a part of it (it would not accept it, i.e., the essence qua essence would not accept non-existence), because existence itself is completely removed (because if the possible essence is removed), its existence is certainly removed, since it is not permissible for that existence to subsist by itself or by something other than that essence, and if it subsisted by it, it would not be removed but existent. And if it is permissible for existence to be completely removed and to be qualified derivationally by its opposite, which is non-existence, then that is permissible for the essence on the supposition that existence is it itself.
Source pages p. 143

Commentary
This passage is a proof (al-wajh al-awwal) from the commentary to establish that existence (al-wujud) is neither identical to essence (al-mahiyya) nor a part of it. The argument proceeds as follows: The opponent (who claims identity or parthood) might say that the essence does not accept non-existence ('adam). The author responds: we do not accept that if existence were identical to essence or a part of it, the essence would be impervious to non-existence. Why? Because existence itself can be completely removed (yarta fi'u bil-kulliyya). For a possible essence, when it ceases, its existence necessarily ceases—it cannot persist by itself or by another. Conversely, if existence can be qualified by its opposite (non-existence), then the essence, if it were existence itself, would also be able to be qualified by non-existence. Thus the supposed identity or parthood does not prevent the essence from accepting non-existence; in fact, it would necessitate that it can accept it, which undermines the opponent's claim. The key point is that the possibility of existence's removal entails the possibility of the essence's removal under identity, so the opponent's denial that essence accepts non-existence is invalid.

Key Terms
وجود (wujud)
ماهية (mahiyya)
عدم ('adam)
ارتفاع (irtifa')
قبول (qabul)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the argument is about essence being intrinsically receptive to non-existence.
Correction: The argument is about the logical consequence of identity with existence: if existence is identical to essence, then essence must also be capable of being removed because existence is removable.

Checkpoint
According to the first proof, why does the author reject the claim that if existence were identical to essence, the essence would not accept non-existence?
A) Because existence itself can be completely removed, so identity would allow essence to be removed as well.
B) Because essence is eternal and cannot accept non-existence.
C) Because existence is an accident that cannot subsist by itself.
D) Because the opponent's argument is based on a false premise about acceptance.

Key Takeaway
Existence's ability to be removed shows it cannot be identical to essence, because if it were, essence would also be removable, contradicting the opponent's claim.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Al-Jurjani argues that even if existence were identical to essence, essence could still accept non-existence. This implies that your very self (essence) is not inherently existent; it is contingent. Do you truly believe in your heart that you are not self-existent? In what areas of your life do you act as if you are independent and self-sufficient, forgetting that your existence is a gift from God that could be removed at any moment?
?
The proof shows that existence can be 'completely removed' from a possible essence. Think about something you deeply attach yourself to—a person, a role, or an achievement. If your existence were removed, that thing would cease for you. How does this reality challenge your attachment to worldly things and push you to prioritize your relationship with the Eternal?
?
If your essence is not the same as your existence, then your identity is not fixed by your own being—it is defined by God's act of creating and sustaining you. Does this shake your sense of self-importance? How can you live today with the constant awareness that you are, moment by moment, dependent on the One who gives and takes existence?

The Second Proof: Conceivability with Doubt of Existence
Al-Jurjani presents the second proof, then addresses an objection with a response.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the second proof demonstrates that existence is not identical to or part of essence, and how it answers the objection about mental existence.

Core Idea
If you can conceive a thing while doubting its existence, then existence is not the thing itself.

Al-Jurjani now presents the second proof. He argues that we can conceive a possible essence, like a triangle, while doubting its existence. This shows that existence is separate from the essence. But an objector raises a subtle point: the doubt is only about external existence, not mental existence. The author responds by distinguishing between awareness of a thing and awareness of that awareness, and by noting that mental existence is not self-evident. This lesson walks through the proof, objection, and answer step by step.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Proof
Read the main argument: we conceive a possible essence with doubt about its existence → existence is neither identical to nor a part of the essence.
2
Grasp the Objection
Note the objection: doubt only concerns external existence, not mental existence, because mental existence is the very act of conception.
3
Analyze the Answer
See how the author rebuts by distinguishing awareness of a thing from awareness of that awareness, and by noting that mental existence is not self-evident.
The answer also points out that mental existence requires proof, so it is not beyond doubt.
4
Relate to Broader Claim
Understand that the proof targets absolute existence (both mental and external), not just external existence.
5
Check Understanding
Take the checkpoint quiz to test your comprehension of the objection and reply.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه ( الثاني أنا نعقل الماهية الممكنة ( كالمثلث ) مثلاً ( مع الشك في وجودها فلا يكون الوجود نفسها ولا جزءها لما سيصرح به ( لا يقال الشك : إنما يتصور في وجودها الخارجي دون الوجود ( الذهني فإنه ) أي الوجود الذهني (نفس التعقل والتصور، فإذا تعقلت الماهية كانت موجودة في الذهن فكيف يشك بعد تعقلها في وجودها الذهني؟ فاللازم مما ذكرتم أن الوجود الخارجي ليس نفس الماهية ولا جزءها ) والكلام في الوجود المطلق وأنه زائد على الماهية سواء كان وجوداً خارجياً أو ذهنياً فالدليل قاصر عن المدعى ( لأنا نقول: ) على تقدير تسليم الوجود الذهني لا قصور فيه إذ ) تحقق الوجود (الذهني حال كون الماهية معقولة متصورة (لا يمنع الشك فيه لأن حصول الشيء في الذهن لا يستلزم تعقل ذلك الحصول والحكم بثبوته له فإن الشعور الشيء غير الشعور بذلك الشعور وغير مستلزم مانعاً من الشك له على وجه لا يشك فيه ) ولذلك اختلف فيه ) أي في الوجود الذهني ( ومن أثبته أثبته ببرهان لا بكونه معلوماً بالضرورة، ولو كان تحقق الوجود الذهني فيه، وموجباً للجزم به لما أنكره عاقل ولما احتيج إلى برهان
The second proof: we conceive the possible essence (like a triangle) for example with doubt about its existence, so existence is not identical to it nor a part of it, as he will state explicitly. It cannot be said: doubt is only conceivable about its external existence, not about mental existence (al-wujud al-dhihni), for mental existence is itself intellection and conception; so when the essence is conceived, it exists in the mind, so how can one doubt its mental existence after conceiving it? Therefore, what you have mentioned entails that external existence is not identical to essence nor a part of it. But the discussion is about absolute existence (al-wujud al-mutlaq) and that it is additional to essence, whether external or mental, so the proof falls short of the claim. We say: on the supposition of granting mental existence, there is no shortcoming, for the realization of mental existence when the essence is conceived and represented does not prevent doubt about it, because the occurrence of a thing in the mind does not necessitate the intellection of that occurrence and the judgment of its being established for it. For the awareness of a thing is other than the awareness of that awareness and does not entail a prevention from doubt about it in a manner that leaves no doubt. Therefore, there is disagreement about it, i.e., about mental existence, and whoever affirms it affirms it by proof, not because it is known necessarily. And if the realization of mental existence prevented doubt and necessitated certainty, no rational person would deny it and no proof would be needed.
Source pages p. 143

Commentary
This passage is the second proof (al-wajh al-thani) from the commentary, followed by an objection and its answer. The proof argues: We can conceive a possible essence (e.g., a triangle) while doubting its existence; therefore, existence is neither identical to the essence nor a part of it. The objection (la yuqalu) raises a nuance: the doubt is only about external existence, not about mental existence, because mental existence is the very act of conception. Once we conceive the triangle, it exists in the mind, so we cannot doubt its mental existence. The objector then claims that the argument only disproves identity with external existence, not with absolute existence (which includes both mental and external). Thus the proof is insufficient. The answer (fa-innana naqulu) first provisionally accepts mental existence and then refutes: even if mental existence is granted, the occurrence of the thing in the mind does not automatically entail awareness of that occurrence or a judgment about it. Awareness of a thing (shu'ur bi-shay') is distinct from awareness of that awareness. Therefore, one can conceive an essence without being aware of its mental existence, allowing doubt about mental existence itself. Moreover, mental existence is not self-evident; there is disagreement about it, and it requires proof. If it were obvious and prevented doubt, no rational person would deny it and no proof would be needed. Thus the proof remains valid against the claim that existence is identical to or part of essence, whether the opponent affirms mental existence or not.

Key Terms
ماهية ممكنة (mahiyya mumkina)
وجود ذهني (wujud dhihni)
وجود خارجي (wujud khariji)
تعقل (ta'aqqul)
شك (shakk)
شعور (shu'ur)
برهان (burhan)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The second proof only works for external existence, not mental existence.
Correction: The proof targets absolute existence. The reply shows that even mental existence can be doubted, because awareness of a thing is distinct from awareness of that awareness, and mental existence is not self-evident.

Checkpoint
How does the author respond to the objection that the doubt is only about external existence, not mental existence?
A) He denies mental existence altogether.
B) He argues that mental existence is not self-evident and requires proof, so one can still doubt it.
C) He concedes the objection and changes the argument.
D) He says the doubt is about both external and mental existence equally.

Key Takeaway
Conceiving an essence while doubting its existence proves that existence is not inherent to the essence, even when considering mental existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Can you recall a time when you were absolutely certain of something you believed, yet never stopped to doubt it to test its foundation? How does this lesson challenge you to examine your assumptions about God's existence as something you merely conceive versus something you truly know is real?

You conceive of God every day, but have you ever genuinely doubted His existence in your mind to see whether your faith is based on a concept or on certainty? If you found you could conceive of God while still doubting, what would that mean for your understanding of His reality?
?
The lesson says awareness of a thing is not the same as awareness of that awareness. In your worship, are you aware of God, or are you aware of your own act of worship? What practical step can you take to move from self-consciousness to God-consciousness in your next prayer?

Al-Jurjani's Third Proof: Essence and Existence
Al-Jurjani presents a third proof that existence is not identical to essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Jurjani's third argument shows that mental existence is not identical to essence by considering an external essence when no one conceives it.

Core Idea
An external essence not conceived is devoid of mental existence.

Al-Jurjani presents a concise but powerful argument that reinforces the distinction between essence and existence. By considering an essence that exists in the external world yet is not conceived by any intellect, he shows that mental existence is not an essential attribute of that essence. This lesson unpacks that brief statement and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Examine the proof's core claim
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice that the proof is presented as an additional (wa-aydan) argument, building on previous discussion. The key statement: an external essence, if not conceived, is devoid of existence.
The existence referred to is mental existence, not external existence.
2
Analyze the logical implication
Ask: If the essence were identical with existence, then every essence would always have existence. But here, when not conceived, it lacks mental existence. Therefore existence is not essential.
This is a reductio ad absurdum argument.
3
Consider potential objections
One might object that the essence still has external existence. The argument targets only mental existence. Al-Jurjani's point is that mental existence is not part of the essence's definition.
The commentary addresses such objections, but this passage stands as a complete point.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضاً فالماهية الخارجية ) أي المتحققة في الخارج إذا لم تكن معقولة لأحد ( خالية عن الوجود
Also, the external essence, i.e., that which is realized in the external world, if it is not conceived by anyone, is devoid of existence.
Source pages p. 144

Commentary
This passage is a brief additional proof (wa-aydan) that existence is not identical to essence. The argument: An external essence—the essence that exists in the external world—when it is not conceived by any intellect, is devoid of existence (in the mind). The implied conclusion is that existence (meaning mental existence) is not intrinsic to the essence, because the same essence can be without that existence when not conceived. This further supports the claim that existence is additional to essence, not identical to nor a part of it. The brevity of the passage suggests it is a supplementary argument following the longer discussion on mental existence. The commentary that follows (not included here) discusses objections to this argument, but the main text itself stands as a complete point.

Key Terms

خالية عن الوجود (khaliyya 'an al-wujud)
معقولة (ma'qula)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the argument proves external existence is not essential, but it actually targets mental existence as not essential.
Correction: The argument shows that mental existence is not part of the essence; external existence is not discussed as being non-essential.

Checkpoint
What does the third argument conclude about an external essence when no one conceives it?
A) It still has mental existence because it is real.
B) It is devoid of existence, showing existence is not identical to essence.
C) It ceases to exist externally.
D) It becomes identical to existence in the mind.

Key Takeaway
Al-Jurjani's third argument demonstrates that mental existence is not an essential part of an external essence, reinforcing the distinction between essence and existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

So what is your true reality beyond your self-image? Are you ready to stop overanalyzing and simply exist before your Creator?

The First Proof: Awareness of a Thing Is Not Awareness of That Awareness
First proof that mental existence is not self-evident

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first proof that mental existence is not self-evident, and why awareness of a thing does not entail awareness of that awareness.

Core Idea
Awareness of a thing is not awareness of that awareness.

This lesson introduces the first proof in a key theological debate: whether mental existence is self-evident. The author argues that simply having a concept in mind does not automatically prove that mental existence is beyond doubt. As you read, notice how the commentator distinguishes between the occurrence of a thing in the mind and our awareness of that occurrence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read both the Arabic and translation without over-analysis. Notice the commentator's structure: he quotes the author, then explains each phrase.
The commentary is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the key distinction
Find where the passage distinguishes between 'awareness of a thing' and 'awareness of that awareness'. This is the core of the proof.
3
Understand the consequence
The passage explains that because of this distinction, mental existence is not self-evident; it requires proof. Those who affirm it do so by argument.
This is the main point of the proof.
4
Note the warning about pronouns
The commentator warns about a common mistake in interpreting pronouns. Pay attention to what 'it' and 'him' refer to.
Misreading pronouns can lead to confusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
متصورة (لا يمنع الشك فيه لأن حصول الشيء في الذهن لا يستلزم تعقل ذلك الحصول والحكم بثبوته له فإن الشعور بالشيء غير الشعور بذلك الشعور وغير مستلزم له على وجه لا يشك فيه) ولذلك اختلف فيه أي في الوجود الذهني (ومن أثبته أثبته ببرهان لا بكونه معلوماً بالضرورة، ولو كان تحقق الوجود الذهني فيه، وموجباً للجزم به لما أنكره عاقل ولما احتيج إلى برهان. قوله (الشك فيه) أي في الوجود الذهني أي في أنه وجود ذهني. قوله (لأن حصول الشيء إلخ) بمعنى أن عدم الشك في أن حصول الماهية في الذهن وجود ذهني لها، موقوف على تصور ذلك الحصول، وعلى الحكم بثبوت الحصول في الذهن، لذلك الحصول أي الحكم بأن ذلك الحصول حصول ذهني، وتحقق الحصول المذكور لا يستلزمهما. قوله (فإن الشعور بالشيء الذي هو عبارة عن الحصول في الذهن غير الشعور بذلك الشعور، وهو ظاهر وغير مستلزم له على وجه لا يشك في أنه شعور لأنه ليس بين الثبوت لأفراده، وإنما قيد بذلك لأن الكلام فيه ولأن الشعور بالشيء يستلزم الشعور بعد الالتفات على ما قالوا من أن العلم بالعلم ضروري بعد الالتفات. قوله (في الوجود الذهني) أي في أن للأشياء وجوداً ذهنياً. قوله (ولو كان تحقق إلخ) أي تحقق ما هو وجود ذهني في نفس الأمر مانعاً من الشك في كونه وجوداً ذهنياً لما أنكره عاقل، ولما احتيج إلى البرهان عليه إذ لا شك في تعقل الأشياء وهو وجود ذهني فتدبر، فقد زل فيه أقدام بسبب إرجاع ضمير له في قوله بثبوته له إلى الشيء مع أنه في قول المصنف لا يمنع الشك فيه راجع إلى الوجود الذهني، وكذا في قول الشارح على وجه لا يشك فيه راجع إلى الشعور.
Conceived (does not prevent doubt about it because the occurrence of a thing in the mind does not entail the conceptualization of that occurrence and the judgment that it is established for it, for awareness of a thing is not awareness of that awareness and is not entailing it in a manner free of doubt). Therefore it was differed about it (i.e., mental existence). And whoever affirmed it affirmed it by proof, not because it is known necessarily. And if the realization of mental existence were in it and necessitating certainty about it, no rational person would have denied it and no proof would have been needed. He said: (doubt about it) i.e., about mental existence, i.e., that it is mental existence. He said: (Because the occurrence of the thing etc.) meaning that the absence of doubt that the occurrence of the essence in the mind is a mental existence for it depends on the conceptualization of that occurrence, and on the judgment that the occurrence is established in the mind, for that occurrence (i.e., the judgment that that occurrence is a mental occurrence), and the realization of the mentioned occurrence does not entail them. He said: (For awareness of a thing, which is expression for occurrence in the mind, is not awareness of that awareness, and it is apparent and not entailing it in a manner that there is no doubt that it is awareness, because it is not from among the established ones for its individuals, and it is only restricted by that because the discussion is about it, and because awareness of a thing entails awareness after attention according to what they said that knowledge of knowledge is necessary after attention. He said: (about mental existence) i.e., that things have a mental existence. He said: (And if the realization etc.) i.e., if the realization of what is mental existence in reality prevented doubt about its being mental existence, no rational person would have denied it, and no proof would have been needed, since there is no doubt in the conceptualization of things and it is mental existence, so ponder, for feet have slipped in it due to referring the pronoun 'him' in his saying 'established for him' to the thing, while in the author's saying 'does not prevent doubt about it' it refers to mental existence, and similarly in the commentator's saying 'in a manner that there is no doubt about it' it refers to awareness.
Source pages p. 144

Commentary
This passage is an explanation of the first proof that mental existence is not self-evident. The commentator begins by quoting the original text: the fact that we conceive things (i.e., they occur in the mind) does not preclude doubt about mental existence itself. The reason is that the occurrence of a thing in the mind does not entail the conceptualization of that occurrence or the judgment that it is an occurrence. One can be aware of a thing without being aware of that awareness. Therefore, people have differed about mental existence, and those who affirm it do so by proof, not because it is obvious. If mental existence were self-evident, no rational person would deny it. The commentator then clarifies each phrase: 'doubt about it' refers to mental existence. He explains that the absence of doubt depends on two things: conceptualizing the occurrence and judging it to be mental. The occurrence itself does not entail these. Awareness of a thing (i.e., its mental occurrence) is distinct from awareness of that awareness; the latter requires attention (ilitifat). The commentator notes that knowledge of knowledge is necessary only after attention. Finally, he warns about a common mistake in pronoun reference: the pronoun 'him' in the original phrase 'established for him' might be misread as referring to the thing, but it actually refers to mental existence; similarly, in the commentator's own phrase, 'free of doubt' refers to the awareness, not the thing.

Key Terms
الوجود الذهني
الشعور
تعقل
ضروري
التفات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that simply having a concept in mind proves mental existence is self-evident.
Correction: The passage shows that awareness of the concept is not the same as awareness of that awareness, so mental existence is not self-evident.

Checkpoint
According to the first proof, why does the occurrence of a thing in the mind not remove doubt about mental existence?
A) Because mental existence is impossible.
B) Because the occurrence does not entail awareness of that occurrence or judgment about it.
C) Because all people agree on mental existence.
D) Because awareness of a thing requires no attention.

Key Takeaway
The first proof shows that mental existence is not self-evident because awareness of a concept is distinct from awareness of that awareness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you are aware of a thought, do you ever become aware of that very act of awareness? According to this lesson, that second awareness is not automatic—it requires attention. How often do you actually pay attention to your own thinking, and what might you discover about yourself if you did more often?
?
This lesson argues that even the existence of your own mental concepts is not self-evident. If you can doubt something so basic, what else in your mind might be built on assumptions rather than certainty? Will you commit to examining one belief you held today to see if it truly rests on proof?
?
The scholars differed about mental existence because awareness of awareness is not given. In your prayer or worship, do you merely go through motions (awareness of actions) without reflecting on your awareness itself? What would change if you brought that second-level awareness into your next act of worship?

The Second Proof: External Essence Free from Mental Existence
Sanusi presents the second proof using the example of an unconceived external essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the second proof demonstrates that mental existence is additional to external essence.

Core Idea
If an external essence is not conceived, it lacks mental existence, proving mental existence is distinct.

This lesson presents the second proof for the addedness of mental existence. The proof uses the concept of an external essence that is not in anyone's mind. By showing that such an essence is free from mental existence, the proof establishes that mental existence is not part of the essence itself.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof passage
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation.
Notice the logical structure: condition (if not conceived) leads to conclusion (free from mental existence).
2
Identify the claim being completed
The passage says 'the other part of the claim is completed.' Consider what the overall claim is that this proof supports.
The claim is that mental existence is additional to essence, not identical or part of it.
3
Reflect on the implication
Why does this proof show that mental existence is not part of the essence? Think about the relationship between existence and essence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضاً فالماهية الخارجية أي المتحققة في الخارج إذا لم تكن معقولة لأحد خالية عن الوجود الذهني فيغايرها فلا يكون نفسها ولا جزءها أيضاً، فبهذا يتم الجزء الآخر من المدعى ولا يمكن أن يقال : الماهية الموجودة في الذهن خالية عن الوجود الخارجي، فيكون زائداً عليها أيضاً إذ يتوجه عليه أنا لا نسلم حصول الماهية في الذهن. قوله (إذا لم تكن معقولة لأحد إلخ قيل عليه : البرهان على الوجود الذهني دل على ثبوت وجود مغاير لوجودات عينية. وأما أنه في أنفسنا فلا لجواز أن يكون في المبادي العالية، ويكون التفات نفوسنا إليها هناك كافياً في الحكم عليها، وحينئذ يكون فرض عدم معقوليتها المستلزم لخلوها عن الوجود الذهني مجرد فرض المحال لكونها معقولة للمبادي العالية وموجودة ذهنية بذلك الاعتبار قطعاً، وأقول : يمكن أن يكون علم المبادي العالية بالأشياء علماً حضورياً وإليه. قوله ( ولا يمكن أن يقال إلخ ) دفع لما يتراءى من كفاية هذا الدليل في تغاير الوجودين. قوله (لا نسلم حصول الماهية أي الماهية الموجودة في الذهن إنما الحاصل بعض وجوهها، وهو ليس من الموجودات الخارجية، فلا يرد أن ذلك الوجه ماهية وجوده في الذهن خالية عن الوجود الخارجي.
And also the external essence, i.e., the one realized in the outside, if it is not conceived by anyone, is free from mental existence; so it (mental existence) is different from it and thus is neither its essence nor its part. So by this the other part of the claim is completed. And it cannot be said: the essence existing in the mind is free from external existence, so it (external existence) is also additional, because it would be objected that we do not accept that the essence occurs in the mind. He said: (If it is not conceived by anyone etc.) it was objected to it: The proof for mental existence indicates the affirmation of an existence different from concrete existences. But that it is within ourselves is not necessary, for it could be in the higher principles, and the attention of our souls to them there is sufficient for judging them. In that case, the assumption of their being unconceived, which entails their being free from mental existence, is merely an assumption of the impossible, because they are conceived by the higher principles and are mentally existent by that consideration necessarily. And I say: The knowledge of the higher principles of things can be presential knowledge, and to it. He said: (And it cannot be said etc.) is a repulsion of what appears from the sufficiency of this proof in the differentiation of the two existences. He said: (We do not accept the occurrence of the essence) i.e., the essence existing in the mind, only some of its aspects occur, which is not among external existences, so it is not problematic that that aspect is an essence its existence in the mind is free from external existence.
Source pages p. 144

Commentary
This passage presents the second proof and addresses an objection. The commentator first states the proof: an external essence, if not conceived by anyone, is free from mental existence, so mental existence is distinct from the essence—it is neither identical to it nor a part of it. This completes the claim that mental existence is additional to essence. Then an objection is anticipated: one might argue that the essence in the mind is free from external existence, so external existence would also be additional. The objector responds: we do not accept that the essence itself (dhāt al-māhiyya) occurs in the mind; only some aspects (wujūh) do. The commentator then notes an earlier objection: the proof for mental existence shows some existence distinct from concrete ones, but it may be in higher principles (e.g., the ten intellects), not in our own minds. In that case, assuming essences are unconceived is impossible because they are conceived by higher principles. The commentator counters that the knowledge of higher principles may be presential (ḥuḍūrī), not representational, so it does not entail mental existence. He then clarifies that the phrase 'it cannot be said' is a defense: the apparent sufficiency of the proof is addressed. Finally, he explains the objection 'we do not accept the occurrence of the essence': what occurs in the mind is only some aspects of the essence, not the essence itself, so it is not problematic that those aspects lack external existence.

Key Terms
الماهية الخارجية
الوجود الذهني

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What does the second proof conclude from an external essence that is not conceived?
A) The essence has mental existence.
B) The essence is free from mental existence, so mental existence is distinct.
C) Mental existence is a part of the essence.
D) The essence is identical to mental existence.

Key Takeaway
An external essence that is not conceived lacks mental existence, proving that mental existence is not essential to the essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Your mental image of yourself is not the same as your actual being before Allah. What specific false belief about yourself have you been holding based solely on your own conception, and what step will you take today to measure it against reality?
?
This lesson proves that an external essence can exist without being mentally conceived. How many of your worries are about things that exist only in your mind? Will you commit to distinguishing between real problems and imagined ones starting right now?
?
If your mental concept of Allah is not His true essence, are you willing to discard any comfortable but inaccurate ideas you have about Him? Name one such idea and replace it with what the Qur'an and Sunnah actually say, even if it makes you uncomfortable.

Defending the Proof: Objections and Responses
Commentator addresses objections to the second proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objections raised against the second proof and how the commentator defends it.

Core Idea
Objections show the proof relies on an impossible assumption, but the commentator argues it is valid.

The second proof is immediately challenged with two objections. The first questions whether essences can ever be unconceived, and the second argues that mental existence might be essential if we consider essences in the mind. The commentator responds carefully, clarifying the nature of mental existence and the role of higher principles.

Guided Path
1
Identify the first objection
Read the first objection: the proof for mental existence shows some existence distinct from concrete existences, but it might be in higher principles, not in our minds. Thus, assuming unconceived essences is impossible because higher principles always conceive them.
2
Understand the commentator's response
The commentator responds that the knowledge of higher principles may be presential (ḥuḍūrī), not representational, so it does not entail mental existence as usually understood.
Presential knowledge is direct awareness without mental representation.
3
Identify the second objection
The second objection argues that if the proof works, then by symmetry, external existence would be additional to mental essences. The response is that we do not accept that the entire essence occurs in the mind; only some aspects do.
4
Review the overall defense
The commentator concludes that the proof remains valid because the objections are based on misunderstandings of what mental existence entails.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The Objection from Identity of Existence and Essence
Objection and answer on the identity view.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the view that existence is identical to essence challenges the proof and how the commentator responds by clarifying the meaning of identity.

Core Idea
The identity view demands precise understanding, not refutation.

This lesson addresses a final objection to the entire proof. Some argue that if existence and essence are one, then the previous arguments lose their force. The commentator presents the objection and offers a subtle answer, inviting careful reflection. Understanding this exchange sharpens your grasp of foundational metaphysical distinctions in Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully to grasp the claim: on the identity view, external existence is identical to external essence, and mental existence to mental essence.
The objection is that this makes the earlier proof's premises meaningless.
2
Understand why it challenges the proof
The proof relies on the distinction between essence and existence. If they are identical, then saying 'the external essence lacks mental existence' or 'the mental essence lacks external existence' becomes incoherent because the essence is not separate from its own existence.
This is a serious objection that must be addressed.
3
Study the commentator's answer
The answer points to the meaning of identity (العينية): the essence existing in the mind is the same as the external essence. This preserves the distinction between mental and external existence while affirming the unity of essence.
Notice the subtlety: the identity is of the essence, not of its modes of existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بقى هاهنا بحث آخر وهو أن من يقول : إن الوجود عين الماهية يقول : إن الوجود الخارجي عين الماهية الخارجية والوجود الذهني عين الماهية الذهنية فلا معنى لأن يقال في رده الماهية الخارجية خالية عن الوجود الذهني أو الماهية الموجودة في الذهن خالية عن الوجود الخارجي، وجوابه معنی العينية وأن الماهية الموجودة في الذهن نفس الماهية الخارجية فتأمل.
There remains here another discussion, which is that one who says that existence is identical to essence says that external existence is identical to the external essence, and mental existence is identical to the mental essence, so it is meaningless to say in refutation of him: the external essence is free from mental existence, or the essence existing in the mind is free from external existence. And the answer to it is the meaning of identity and that the essence existing in the mind is the same as the external essence, so reflect.
Source pages p. 145

Commentary
This passage raises a final objection against the entire line of argument. If one holds that existence is identical to essence (i.e., the view that existence and essence are one and the same), then the previous proofs fail. On that view, external existence is identical to external essence, and mental existence is identical to mental essence. Therefore, it is meaningless to say that the external essence is free from mental existence, because the external essence, being identical to its own existence, is not something separate that could lack mental existence. Similarly, one cannot say that the essence in the mind lacks external existence, because the mental essence is identical to mental existence, not external. The commentator gives the answer: the identity thesis means that the essence existing in the mind is the very same as the external essence (i.e., the essence is one, only its mode of existence differs). This distinction preserves the argument. The commentator ends with 'fa-ta'ammal' (so reflect), inviting careful consideration.

Key Terms
عين الماهية
الوجود الخارجي
الوجود الذهني
العينية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The identity view completely refutes the proof.
Correction: The passage shows that the identity view can be answered by clarifying that the essence in the mind is the same as the external essence, preserving the distinction between modes of existence.

Checkpoint
According to the final discussion, why does the view that existence is identical to essence challenge the proof?
A) Because it denies mental existence altogether.
B) Because if existence and essence are identical, then external essence is not separate from its existence, so the premise of the proof fails.
C) Because it proves that mental existence is not additional to essence.
D) Because it shows that essence cannot be conceived.

Key Takeaway
The identity view does not automatically refute the proof; the key is understanding that the essence is one but its mode of existence distinguishes mental from external.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You often picture yourself as a person of faith, but is your mental image of your righteousness identical to your actual deeds? Where have you confused a concept with reality, and what one step will you take today to align your mental existence with your external actions?
?
The commentator ends with 'so reflect'. What mental habit or assumption about your own existence have you never questioned? How will you examine it now in light of this lesson?
?
If your essence is one but your existence takes different modes (mind vs. world), what mode dominates your awareness of yourself? Are you living more in your mental narrative than in your actual encounter with Allah? What will you change?

Existence as a Specific Nature: Proof and Refutation
Presents a proof that existence is a specific nature and its refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why existence cannot be a specific nature and how the refutation preserves the distinction between existence and essence.

Core Idea
Existence is not a natural kind; it is an accidental attribute across diverse essences.

This passage presents a key debate in Islamic philosophy: is existence identical to essence or something additional? The author first gives the opponents' argument that existence must be a specific nature requiring matter, then refutes it by denying that existence is a natural kind. This refutation is crucial for understanding the contingency of existence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic text and its English translation carefully. Notice the structure: statement, proof, refutation.
The proof uses the uniformity of specific natures to argue for matter in all beings.
2
Identify the opponents' proof
Locate the argument that if essences need matter in elementary beings, they must need it in celestial beings too. Understand why this would make existence a specific nature.
This argument was used to reject Platonic forms and Democritean atoms.
3
Understand the refutation
The answer denies that existence is a specific nature. Instead, existence is an accidental attribute that can attach to essences of diverse realities. Consider why this refutation is effective.
This shows that existence does not have a uniform requirement across all beings.

المرصد الأول - المقصد الثالث : أن الوجود نفس الماهية أو جزؤها كانت قائمة بالهيولى في العنصريات وجب قيامها بها في الفلكيات، لأن مقتضى الطبيعة النوعية لا يختلف (و) به (أبطلوا المثل المجردة التي قال بها أفلاطون كما سيأتي في مباحث الماهية وأبطلوا أيضاً مذهب ديمقراطيس في تركب الأجسام البسيطة الطباع من أجزاء متفقة الحقيقة قابلة للانقسام وهماً لا خارجاً ) والجواب منع كونه أي الوجود ( طبيعة نوعية بل هو أمر عارض لأفراده المتخالفة الحقائق .
The first observatory - the third objective: That existence is identical with essence or part of it. If they [essences] were subsisting by hyle in the elementary beings, then their subsistence by it in the celestial beings is necessary, because the requirement of the specific nature does not differ. And by it they refuted the abstract forms which Plato held, as will come in the discussions of quiddity, and they also refuted the doctrine of Democritus concerning the composition of simple-natured bodies from parts agreeing in reality, divisible in estimation not externally. And the answer is to deny its being, i.e., existence, a specific nature, but rather it is an accidental attribute for its individuals of diverse realities.

Commentary
This passage is a definition and proof followed by a refutation. It opens by stating the objective: to discuss whether existence is identical to essence or part of it. Then it presents a proof from the opponents: if essences require matter (hyle) in elementary beings, they must require it in celestial beings because a specific nature's requirement does not differ. Using this, they refuted Platonic abstract forms and Democritus's atoms. The answer (الجواب) is to deny that existence is a specific nature; instead, it is an accidental attribute that occurs to individuals of diverse realities.

Logic and Technical Terms

Objection from the Absolute Non-Existent and the Core Answer
Al-Jurjani presents the objection from the absolute non-existent and gives the core answer distinguishing negative from affirmative judgments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the objection that mental existence leads to a contradiction fails, based on the distinction between negative and affirmative judgments.

Core Idea
Negative judgments do not require the existence of their subject.

A key objection against mental existence is that if all positive judgments require their subject to exist, then even saying 'the absolute non-existent is unknown' would imply its existence, creating a contradiction. Al-Jurjani answers by showing that this statement is a negative proposition, which does not require subject existence. This distinction is foundational for understanding the theory of mental existence.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the objection from 'If you say...' to understand the apparent contradiction: the absolute non-existent is said to be unknown, yet being unknown seems to necessitate its existence.
Notice that the objector assumes all judgments require existence.
2
Identify the core answer
The answer states that the statement is a negative proposition: 'It is not an absolute non-existent that is known.' This type of judgment does not require subject existence.
Only affirmative propositions require existence.
3
Understand the distinction
Contrast negative and affirmative judgments. Negative propositions deny a predicate of a subject without affirming the subject's existence. Affirmative propositions assert a predicate and require the subject to exist.
This distinction resolves the contradiction.
